The Red Page
War
Besieged in Kut and After, by Major
Charles 11. Barber ( Blackwood, 55.); is
highly interesting narrative of an adven
ttsre about which the world has heard very
few details. The author, who was in. the
medical service, chats discursively of the
incidents of the siege. All through his lios
pital was shelled, and casualties both to
patients and staff were frequent. So' close
was the enemy that there was no site be
yond tiie reach of his guns. This doctoi
has a hitter prejudice against shells: “They
are indelicate and beastly in their blind
blundering range. To the surgeon, suet
indiscriminate violence is intensely abhor-
rent; the clumsy damage to the beautiful,
perfectly-planned human organism makes his
professional spirit wince....and he wonders
at all the farcical talk and controversy over
explosives and dum-dum bullets whilst the
effects of shell-fire are tolerated without a
murmur.” As the siege progressed there
grew in the cemetery a rapidly spreading
forest of small wooden crosses, and the
padres were never at rest. The trenches
were in places within 20 yards of each other.
At first the garrison was buoyed up
with the hope of early release; later on they
heard the guns of the relieving force, but
that was all. The tobacco problem became
acute; lime leaves were a poor substitute.
Rations were successively reduced, but the
garrison kept up heart. The attempt to
feed that crowded village by aeroplane, as
the ravens did Elijah—a brilliant idea, but
begun two weeks too late —is humorously
described. Evidently the pilots were bad
shots; sometimes they dropped their sacks
of flour into the river or into the Turkish
lines; and then a wireless message would
go: “Would H.M. Navy mind dropping us
something now and then instead of to the
Turks?” or: “Was the R.F. quite sure it
was on the right river?”
So with indomitable cheerfulness and
stolid British humor the author takes us to
the surrender, lie was ultimately exchanged,
but he had first to go in charge of wounded
to Baghdad. The Turks treated their British
prisoners of rank courteously, though the
wounded and sick privates were regarded
with a callousness that is no doubt natural
to the Turk. One German, whom the author
believed to be exaggerating, said: “I’m sorry
to see your fellows going away into captivity.
I don’t suppose 10 per cent, of them will
ever see their homes again.” The captured
British army was marched through the
crowded streets of Baghdad in triumph ; but
the multitude remained silent. The author
found many evidences of the svmpathy of
the citizens. A brief history of Baghdad up
to its final capture by the British forms one
of the most interesting chapters of an ex-
tremely readable book.
The death on active service of Donald
Ilankey, author of A Student in Arms, is
another serious loss to literature. However,
from his papers enough has been gathered
to make a second series under the same title
(Melrose, per Dymock’s Library). Hankey
was meant to be a soldier: he got, his com-
mission in the Royal Garrison Artillery and
served in Mauritius; buLreligious considera-
tions intervening he resigned his commission
in order to study theology. He went to
Oxford, came down to run a boys’ club in
the London slums, roughed it in Australia.
The outbreak of the war found him in Eng-
land, and he enlisted and received his com-
mission in his old regiment. But he was
not too fond of the guns: he exchanged into
an infantry regiment, as a private.
This second series of A Student in Arms
was written in the trenches —the first was
written in England; and the author, in a
private letter, admits a change of tone. This
graver note is to be found in his article upon
the bad side of military service. The ordi-
nary soldier “loathes the war, and the grand-
iloquent speeches of politicians irritate him
by their failure to realise how loathsome
war is. At the same time he knows he has
got to go through with it, and only longs
for the chance to hurry up.”
The charge that military service has a
brutalising effect on the soldier is fully and
frankly met. “Wherever there are troops,
and especially in wartime, there are ‘bad’
women and weak women. The result is
inevitable. A certain number of both officers
and men ‘go wrong.’ ” When the author
was a private in camp in England he states
that most of the privates spent a great
deal of their leisure, owing to the lack of
rational amusement—as then the Army
recreation tents, etc., were too small—“on
the streets,” poor and impecunious; “and
if a fellow ran up against ‘a bit of skirt,’
he was generally just in the mood to follow
it wherever it might lead. The moral of this
is, double your subscription to the Y.M.C.A.,
church huts, soldiers' clubs or whatever
organisation you fancy! You will be help-
ing to combat vice in the only sensible way.
I don't suppose that the officers were much
better off than we were.... They had more
money to spend, and perhaps more to occupy
their minds. But I fancy that as great a
proportion of them as of us took the false
step.” This is of the army at home.
J*
On active service every feature is a hun-
dred times intensified. liaukey contrasts the
state of the men in the trendies, a state of
disgusting uncleanliness,’ of nervous, tension,
of sleepless nights filled with anxieties and
responsibilities, with danger and death
always waiting. Then after a week or a
month of this they return to billets. “Ten-
sion is relaxed. There is an inevitable re-
action. Officers and men alike determine to
‘gather rosebuds’ while they may. Their
bodies are fit, their wills are relaxed. If
they are built that way. and an opportunity
offers, they will ‘satisfy the’ lusts of the
flesh.’ ”
After real fighting the dangers of the reac-
tion are intensified. We who stay at home
and doubtless deplore the morality of the
bloke who is fighting for us, do not realise
What* his first bayonet-thrust means to a
soldier. He has to overcome his first re-
pugnance ; after that he sees red; he lets
loose his long-controlled, primitive blood-
lust. “And that letting loose of a primitive
lust is not going to be without its effect on
a man’s character. At the same time, of
course, not all of us become animals out
here. There are other influences at work.
... .The letters of my platoon are largely
love letters—often the love-letters of mar-
ried men to their wives.” There is immo-
rality in the army; if there is more abroad
it is because there is far greater temptation.
Hankey asks the padres, especially those
who are saints, to realise that in civil life
as in army life the average man is immoral,
both in thought and in deed. Hankey men-
tions four powers which are stronger than
“the flesh”—-the zest of a quest, religion,
hero-worship, and the love of a good woman.
“If a man is not possessed by one of these
he will be immoral.” Surely Hankey is too
sweeping; he omits at least three other
rowers that are stronger than the bodily
appetites—the power of convention, the fear
of consequences and mental control—with-
out considering the class of men whose pas-
sionate desjres are weak.
The Student in Arms concludes: “Per-
haps most men are immoral if they get the
chance. Most soldiers are immoral if they get
the chance. And those who are trying to help
the soldiers can do so with a good heart if
they realise that in him they have a founda-
tion on which to build. Alreadv he is half
a hero-worshipper. Already he half believes
in the beauty of sacrifice and the life im-
mortal. Already he is predisposed to value
exceedingly all that savors of clean, whole-
some home-life. On that foundation it
should be possible to build a strong ideal-
ism which shall prevail against the flesh.”
There is. however, the medical side to con-
sider : the doctors could perhaps tell us
whether in this period of relaxation after
intense strain “the lusts of the flesh” play
a beneficent or a dangerous part.
J*
He also acutely discusses the good side
of militarism. The war correspondent sees
only the outside of the individual soul. He
sees the steadiness of eye and mouth of the
men as they march to the front, hears the
cheery jest. He sees those that return
weary, yet still with a quip for the passer-
by. “He gives us a picture of men without
nerves.. . .The ‘Tommy’ of the war-corres-
pondent is not a human being, but a lay
figure with a gift for repartee. We soldiers
know better. know that each of these
men is an individual with a healthy in-
dividual’s repugnance to being maimed
The knowledge of all this does not do away
with the even tread of the troops as they
pass, the steady eye and mouth, the cheery
jest, but it makes these a hundred times
more significant.”
Analysing the fear of deatE in war, this
acute observer finds there is practically no
tear. At the moment of a charge men are in
an absolutely abnormal condition : “There is
a _ sensation of tense excitement combined
with a sort of uncanny calm. Noises, sights
and sensations which would ordinarily pro-
duce intense feeling, horror or dread have no
effect on them at all, and yet never was their
mmd clearer, their sight, hearing, etc., more
acute. „ .But with the issue before them,
ith victory or death or the prospect of
eternity, their minds blankly refuse to come
to grips Very few men are afraid of
death m the abstract. Very few men believe
in Hell, or are tortured by their consciences.
I hey are doubtful about after death, hesitat-
mg between a belief in eternal oblivion and
a belief in a new life under the same manage-
ment as the present; and neither prospect
hlls them with terror. If only one’s ‘people’
would be sensible, one would not mind.”
There is, however, a purely physical re-
action and shrinking from the pain of death
and wounds; but such a reaction can be, and
usually is, controlled by the mind. Hankey
testifies that the cases where any sort of
fear, even for a moment, obtains the mastery
of a man are very rare. And these cases
are confined to young boys, very egotistical
or very introspective men. or men of obvi-
ously low intelligence.
jt
Guy Thornton, a Baptist padre with the
M.L. forces, tells of his experiences With
the Anzacs in Cairo (Allenson, per Sydney
Book Club). “Persistent rumors, more or
less exaggerated, have been circulated wfith
regard to the behavior of our brave Aus-
tralian, M.L. and British troops whilst they
were stationed in or near Cairo.” This padre
concludes that taking into consideration the
abnormal temptations of Cairo the wonder
is not that so many fell, but that so many
didn’t. As plainly as a Baptist padre can,
lie describes the cesspool of vice that was
left open to trap our clean-living and inex-
perienced Billjims. And he spent most of
liis time “slumming” in that abode of white,
brown and mottled Flossie. Of licensed
native women there were 21100, and over 800
licensed European women; while “un-
licensed women were everywhere”; and
liquor was plentiful, cheap and frequently
doped.
“Cooped up in transports for eight weeks,
under what is to them severe discipline, is it
to be wondered at that the reaction was
great; that a certain proportion, loosed for
the first time from the restraints of home
and of a Christian land, should plunge into
excess; that, withheld from intoxicants for
weeks, they should, in a city where nearly
every shop sold liquor, drink to excess?”
Yet Padre Thornton puts down the propor-
tion as lower than 5 per cent. And nine-
tenths of the men who went wrong did so
under the influence of strong—or doped—
drink.
The padre’s self-appointed job was to fre-
quent the worst streets and warn Billjim
away from the houses. Sometimes be was
attacked by the natives, but he knew how to
use his fists —and his boot. And he found
that Billjim, unless very drunk, always took
his warnings in good part. lie must have
saved hundreds of young Billjims from dis-
astrous consequences; but the pity is that
the temptation was so easy of access to the
camps. And this worthy wowser was even
more astonished when he reached London to
find street-vice there so rampant. Reading
his faithful, if slightly unctuous, account of
his efforts, interspersed with various anec-
dotes of the conversion of Billjims, one feels
that our boys had not a fair deal. Of the
trouble in Cairo that C. E. W. Bean cabled
about, this padre mentions not a word. A
book for The Ernest Wowser.
BillJim Verses
THE GLORY OF WAR.
Once they sang uv th’ Glory uv War —
Bolt an’ peasant, an’ Emperor!
'Ow shall they know uv th’ things that are —
Th’ ’orrer, th’ stink, an’ th’ shime uv warf
Premier, Guverner, Emperor,
’Ow shall they know orl th' shime uv wart
’Ow can they know uv th’ bind that drips,
Drips frum th ’ 'oilers that once were lips;
Wot do they knoio uv th’ shell, that rips
Mussel an’ flesh frum a cocky’s 'ips!
Wot do they think uv th’ burstin ’ shell,
Scndin ’ a dozen ter ’eaven —or ’ell —
Say, ’ave they smelt uv th' powder's smell,
’Urd ther best cobber’s larst dyin' yell?
Know wot it is ter feel scared ter diet
Scareder ter live — -wi’ a shattered thight
Laid in th’ nite time, beneath th’ sky,
Wond’rin ’ if this is ter be good-bye?
Oh, they tork uv th’ glory uv war—
’Ave they seen tvimen lie ded on th’ floor?
Premier, Guverner, Emperor,
Wot do they know uv th’ Glory uv War?
THE BASE HOSPITAL.
The long white ward with quilted beds arow,
Each tidy locker gay with nodding flowers ,
The white-capped sisters, hastening to and
fro
Through all the silent hours.
The weary, white-gowned surgeons, skilfully
With probe and forceps search each gap-
ing wound;
And through the morning’s cold air, drearily,
The stifled groanings sound.
Upon the bed he lies, and counts the ropes
And poles above his head: the weak sun-
beams
That play about the beds; then, dozing,
gropes
His way through weary dreams.
He listens to the imnderings of his mates’
Delirium; the frogs; the million
Strange sounds that make the day; and
waits, and waits
The setting of the sun.
For whatf He knows not. Still the day is
past;
Then, through the tent, he counts the
stars, until
A stab within the arm—and then, at last
His weary brain is still.

By Peter Austin, in “Bill-Jim.”
Peter Austin, author of Bill-Jim (Gordon
and Gotcli, Brisbane), does not pretend to
be a poet; he is a Billjim, returned after
three years service, who remembers. These
verses are the real thing, too true to need
literary graces. Such themes would have
been worthy of a great poet; but Peter
Austin’s sincerity is sufficient for his modest
claim to be the mere interpreter of his mates
He does not pretend to be a Leon Gellert'
he is The Sentimental Bloke with the senti-
ment mislaid. Despite Dennis, this page
doubts if it ever was there. Incidentally
this dialect, peppered with apostrophes, is
annoying both to the typist and the lino-
man, and the attempt of writers of dialeet or
slang to conform to literary usage, by indi-
cating by apostrophes the"missing letters, is
both futile and false. It purposes to be the
actual reproduction of the sound of the
spoken word; but it also pretends to be the
writing not of the author but of the charac-
ter to whom the author has given speech. Iu
this case the speaker is supposed to be Bill-
jim, not Peter Austin, who knows how to
spdl and to speak correctly. But Billjim,
writing the line (if he pronounced it that
way) :
Th’ ’ orrer , th’ stink, an ’ til’ shime nr war
would not write it like that. He would not
know that he was leaving letters out; how
then could he indicate each omission by an
apostrophe? Really, he would write it like
this:
I'll orrer, th stink, an th shime uv war.
Now, that is more easily read, more easily
written, and gives the actual sound of the
words more clearly than the grammarian’s
punctilious rule that for every letter
omitted in the spoken word an apostrophe
should appear in the written word to remind
the reader of the omission. The reader does
not leant to be reminded of the correct form
of the written word : it is an unwarrantable
intrusion of the pedagogue between the
reader and the page. Consider how much
more attractive The Sentimental Bloke would
appear without the peppering of every page
with impertinent apostrophes! And how
much more readable. Try the experiment.
The Commonwealth of Australia: Its
Development and Resources, by Evans
Lewin (Allen and Unwin, Is.), is the third
volume of the British Empire Section in the
International Information Series. The
author, who is Librarian of the Royal
Colonial Institute, furnishes an unbiased
and lucid account of us. He advocates an
extension of the railway from Oodnadatta to
the Macdonnell Ranges, not only as a neces-
sary step in the settlement of the Territory,
but as the main consideration of the develop-
ment of the huge latent and pastoral wealth
of the interior. As for rainfall, he makes
the two points that already we possess an
area the size of Austria-Hungary, France,
Germany and Spain, with an annual fall of
over 20 inches, and that the “deserts” are
continually shrinking at the approach of
scientific methods of water conservation.
“There remain vast territories within the
Commonwealth, kingdoms in point of size,
of which the possibilities are as yet but
dimly perceived.” We are a little surprised
to learn, though, that: “It should be clearly
understood that the Australian people are pur-
suing a national industrial ideal. They have
escaped from the thraldom of the pastoral
and agricultural idea.” The author, noting
our tendency toward the formation of
“spheres of influence” and the extension of
territorial sovereignty, considers that within
the near future Australia and Maoriland
will be responsible for the administration
and defence of all the British possessions in
the South Pacific.

direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared, for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cherry-Time.
In an orchard far away,
Youth and I together
Watched the seasons day by day
Painting flower and feather ;
Watched the cherry blossom glow,
Heard the thrush elated —
Oh, but it was long ago
Since we two were mated !
Came the Summer hot on Spring,
Blooding all the berries,
And the parrots, banqueting,
Plashed among the cherries.
After winter’s frost and rime,
After heavens leaden,
A merry time is cherry-time
When the cherries redden.
Sun-caressed, and zephyr-fanned,
Roses drooped and slumbered;
Half the land was orchard-land,
Half was flower-encumbered.
Now a moth his calling plied,
Now a brown bee settled
On the roses, damask-dyed,
Tall and velvet petalled.
After winter’s frost and rime,
After heavens leaden,
A cosy time is posy time,
When the roses redden.
Lighting all the scented scene,
Through the orchard-garden,
Soft of foot and gay of mien,
Walked the roses’ warden.
Roses, roses dead and gone!
Cherry-branches fruity!
Where is she who shed upon
Fruit and petal beauty?
Ah. though sweet be earth at Spring,
Donning green o’ grasses,
A magic thing, a tragic thing,
Is love that laughs and passes.
N.S.W
RODERIC QUINN.
Of Books.
This was myself that I had lost!
And this myself that I had found !
Eager the eye ran o’er each page
As, nose to earth, a hunting hound.
This way and that the mind played wide,
With here a pause for something new.
Strange that I never knew myself
Till I had found her here in you !
You, as it were, upon my shelf!
In you am I ! Ah. wonderingly
I have been asking all day long
If you have found yourself in me.
N.S.W
MARY GILMORE.
The Kiss.
Just yesternoon, ’mid sunlight hidden,
Reckless of every coming morrow,
A dear fay kissed me all unbidden,
And drove away my sorrow.
Sweet lips had she, demurely parted,
Quite adequately for true kissing.
I heard no more (I, broken-hearted)
The Serpent’s ominous hissing.
She kissed me once, but now for ever
I’ll feel her pouted lips’ surrender.
Sure the grim Fates before have never
So blessed an old offender!
And now that creeping shadows lengthen,
Whatever hoped-for joy may miss me,
Still shall I cry, my dreams to strengthen,
“Fay of my heart, come kiss me !’’
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
Week End.
Some shadows round the trees I saw, and
in the dawn.
And round the edges of the dark. _
The furtive, purring spirit voice of each
brown faun
Was in mine ears ; and I did hark
To all the trembling twilit fields’ long call.
My heart held speech with myriad reddened
leaves that fall
Into the lap of coming Spring;
And long I listened rapt to Summer burgeon-
ing.
Beside the shadowy stream-side nymphs I
knelt
And hearkened faithfully their words.
And under mossy river-banks warm hands I
felt,
Like small field-mice or frightened birds.
Deep in a cloud of hurrying brown leaves I
fled
Down to a battlemented sunset red and red;
Past woodland companies of elves
And woodland dreams that sorrowing dream
themselves.
Three days I walked there silent, hand in
hand with Pan.
Three days—and all my earthly days.
Long-hoarded magic of the woods was mine ;
I ran
From hedge to hedge by Pan’s own ways;
And under each curled leaf I found my talis-
man,
Close-furled and wrapt in mist which no
man’s eyes might scan
Save mine, who swept past harebells. tall.
Fast following with Pan his deep, insistent
call.
And in the fourth day’s dawn I saw and felt
the rain.
And sadly hied me back to town to work
again.
N.S.W.
HILARY LOFTING.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal. Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. IVAe/i MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 27, 1917.
AUTOCRACY’S BADGE OF
DISHONOR.
Even if it were true, as lately alleged by
Willie Watt in Sydney, that some immu-
table law is in operation “making it almost
impossible for a democracy to fight as the
autocracies of the world fight,” the circum-
stance would seem to be only a further
guarantee that Providence had doomed all
autocrats and potentates to destruction. The
rising tide of democracy cannot be dammed
back by the delivery of speeches from thrones
that monarchy and privileged classes are the
best international butchers. There are mul-
titudinous reasons for this, but two of them
alone are sufficient to eventually put the
autocrat’s pot on. The first is that the pro-
letariat yearns more for peace than for war.
The second is that the heap of festering
bones called military glory is no substitute
for the right of the people to say whether
they want any more such heaps. The
greater eminence of autocracy in bash, if it
exists, is not, then, a good reason why
democratically-inclined people should begin
to get nervous about their theories and start
admiring crowned heads. Crowned heads
have been carrying on war with varying
results for thousands of years, and modern
democracy is really a reflection of the com-
paratively new idea that the time is ripe
to curb their habit of butting in on the
slightest provocation or without any at all
and disturbing the peace. There were times
when the person inhabiting the local castle
made a hobby of rushing off with his retainers
to tilt at somebody else in the same line of
business, lie often did the job like a trades-
man ; but the finish of him was that he got
his collective nose put out of joint, mainly
because of the growth of a popular prejudice
against being slain and pillaged. When a
monopoly of authority passed into the hands
of king or emperor, the next business the
people found they had on hand was to deal
with him and restrain his hourly impulse
to provide them with another battle. Some-
times the proletariat got tired of the poten-
tate and chopped his head off. Occasionally
they exchanged him for someone else—and
generally made matters worse. At other
times they merely hunted him off the pre-
mises and spent the rest of their lives leap-
ing about the frontier chasing him away
again. But all the time the movement was
towards emancipation from the tyranny of
autocracy by widening tbe basis of citizen-
ship, responsibility and control. Now the
world has reached the stage when as a
generalisation it can be said that even where
monarchy exists it only survives by the sanc-
tion of democracy, and where autocracy sits
enthroned it is fighting for life against moral
forces compared with which its armies and
its victories are futile.
But is it true that autocracy is more
efficient in war than democracy merely be-
cause it is autocracy? The autocracy of
the Czars was absolute. He who may per-
haps be the last of his wretched line saw
his armies march into the field to be
slaughtered like sheep. It was because
autocracy in Russia, always a failure in
peace, had once more in this war proved
itself a nerveless imposture, that the revolu-
tion was launched. Germany is not quite an
autocracy, though it is very near being one.
France is a democracy, or a very close
approach to one. In Austria mediaeval ideas
about kings and queens survive in more
full-blooded form than anywhere else in
Europe. Yet whatever may be the verdict
of the historian on the evolution of govern-
ment, there is certainly no evidence so far
to justify anyone in believing that the
democracy of France has borne itself with
less resolution or has shown less efficiency
in its direction of the fight than the
autocracies east of the Rhine. And then
take Britain—a democracy of advanced type,
in comparison with her enemies, at any rate.
Admit all the muddling. Concede all the
preliminary and incidental errors of judg-
ment and execution. Would it be a fair
thing to say that she has shown any less
efficiency than autocratic Germany, less
resiliency than any or all of the Central
Powers? This paper does not think so. Nor
does it believe that had one of those
autocracies entered the war with the equip-
ment possessed by Britain it would have
accomplished one-fourth of what the Old
Cold Country has achieved. It is a sheer
delusion, then, to imagine that any historical
justification exists for looking upon democ-
racy as incompetent in war or upon
autocracy as being universally efficient.
Kings and queens have scores of times
despatched armies to overwhelming defeat,
have witnessed their countries being ravaged
and their people reduced to bondage. In
the hour of defeat an autocracy is the most
impotent form of government the -world
knows anything about. When it perishes
there is usually nothing but anarchy to take
its place. Let any doubter compare the
history of North America with that of
Russia. The United States carried on a
sanguinary civil war, and at the end the
nation’s institutions were unimpaired, and
democracy was more firmly established than
before. To-day that country is entering into
what may be an even greater trial, but with
a firmer faith and clearer hope in the
triumph of her arms than Germany has in
the victory of Csesarism. What is true is
that in scheming to provoke wars, in getting
ready for unprovoked assaults upon the life
and property of adjoining peoples, in delud-
ing men about the aim and purpose of
military establishments and in manipulating
the strings of secret diplomacy, autocracy
has an advantage over democracy. It has
this because it is an organised lie; because
it is unclean from top to bottom; because
its claims to admiration on account of its
exploits in war amount to a blasphemous
exaltation of murder.
* * * *
The excuse made for inefficiency and muddle
—for this is what the talk about the difficul-
ties of carrying on war in a democracy
amount to—is own brother to the other one
concerning the “difficulties of government”
in a democracy at peace. Yet who is there
believes the difficulties of government iu
Germany are less than they are in Britain
or France—thgt Austria is more easily gov-
erned than the United States of America?
If there is any such person he has a queer
conception of what goes on in those coun-
tries and a still more quaint notion of the
course of political evolution in Europe. The
loud, threatening yell that arises every few
weeks among democrats and drives quaking
politicians into their lairs is not one-
hundredth part so ominous as the dead
silence surrounding an autocracy, broken
sometimes by the sound of an exploding
bomb. So The Bulletin counsels Willie
Watt to cheer up and lose not his faith in
the crowd. His bowels may stir in pain
when he thinks of the Bolsheviks: but if he
asks himself what Ivan was getting out of
the last autocracy he may come to the con-
clusion that, after all. it were better to
have started life in Victoria than in Petro-
grad. It may cause him a severe headache
when he hears a frantic howl from the
scarcely-awakened, party-ridden democracy
of Australia and sees the paltry and ignoble
put above eternal truth. But the patriot
will exaggerate neither the weaknesses nor
the virtues of democracy. His main job will
be to help it discipline itself, to trust itself,
to believe in its universal mission and jus-
tice. And the more he concentrates on
disciplining himself and scotching the joss
of party that is a more sinister enemy of
democracy than all the autocracies on earth,
and the less he admires a despotism but-
tressed by chicanery and lust for war. the
more is he likely to succeed. It is no good
democracy looking backwards. When it
begins to do that the Devil thoughtfully pro-
vides futher accommodation.

DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
THE FLYING BAGMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A mile down, Emu Plains glowed in the
bright sunshine, all rectangular plots of cul-
tivation. stretches of pasture parcelled out
in little fields as you see in England, and
dark belts of native forest. The ribbon of
the Nepean fluttered along the foot of the
Mountains, emerging from the deep gorge
where the Warragamba joins it, and
meandering down to the Colo, where it
makes a right-angle, and alters course,
easterly, to the Pacific. Thirty miles or so
behind us its long smoke-bank hides Sydney,
and, away ahead, across the rolling relief-
map of the ranges, a white splash on the
deep indigo of the Blue Mountains marks the
position of Katoomba and its diffuse suburb
of Leura. The Great Western railway
stretches its narrow track across the river
above tbe old ford, and winds up the ridge
that Blaxland, Wentworth and Lawson
climbed in lSlo, making a great loop to the
south before it resumes its westerly course
at Glenbrook. Those mighty cuttings in the
new deviation are only small yellow scars in
the blue-green forest.
The humming, roaring, vibrating engine
drew us up over the tumbled chaos of the
mountains. Seven thousand feet, said the
aneroid, and we were past Valley Heights,
and about as far from Springwood in direc-
tion as in elevation, when we wheeled round
on a slope and made back towards the aero-
drome at Richmond. Out along the Grose
Valley-road—an out-track down a long ridge
—in its two-acre clearing, the red roof and
white walls of Norman Lindsay’s bear-
garden, birthplace of the versatile Billy
Bluegl t m. marked the spot where that pleas-
ant gentleman deals out disrespect to the
llun-gorilla. paints pictures, constructs
statuary, and keeps fowls. A white speck
on Ham Common 14 miles away, the big gal-
vanised-iron aerodrome marks our home
port, which, at an easy pace of 80 miles an
hour, we will reach in 10 minutes.
As we sped homeward across those lovely
lands so far below us, the writer fell a-
dreaming.
He thought he was a steel-lined whisky-
traveller calling at the main pubs between
Sydney and Bourke. Or maybe it was boots,
or Manchesters, or rolled oats, or sheep-dip
that sent him winging it out over mountain
and plain with samples and order-book. He
supposed himself to be the up-to-date, look-
out-or-he’ll-sell-you-something, very courteous,
persuasive and persistent drummer of the
not distant future, engaged upon his lawful
occasions in the furtherance of trade and the
development of industry.
From the distillery, or the factory, or the
laboratory that was tariff-protected and
stable he had climbed into the heavens after
breakfast. Behind him was his trusty pilot
—one who never got drunk or trifled with
hotel chambermaids, and was a sober, alert
and greasy-fingered young mechanic who
knew the air-trails better than a Sydney
taxi-driver knows his streets and his traffic
laws. Down Waterloo way the institution
was located, and 15 minutes after starting
we had landed in Liverpool at the fine aero-
drome beside George’s River. Three-quarters
of an hour had sufficed for all the business
that was to be done in Macquarie’s old town,
and another quarter had seen him selling
the wares he trafficked in to the publicans
or shopkeepers of Penrith. And then up
into the glorious morning again, smoking the
good cigar of a generous customer behind
the celluloid wind screen —very high up. so
as to dodge the eddies from the canons of
the Blue Mountains—and 10 minutes saw
him at work in Springwood. Lawson.
Katoomba. Blackheath. Mount Victoria
and Lithgow are dealt with during the fore-
noon and we lunch in Bathurst. We sleep
in Dubbo that night, having traded with
Orange. Wellington and Xarromine in the
course of a leisurely afternoon. The follow-
ing night we perch on the Darling plains
beside Bourke, having eaten our midday mem
as we flew low across the long levels of the
West. And we are back in Sydney tor
lunch on the following day. after a comfort-


able, Quiet and easy-going business journey
across New South Wales.
The subsequent day we go north, to Wal-
-1 ngarra, and, two days after that, are boost-
ing the necessaries of life in Albury. Next
week we take a day’s run out to Broken Hill
t» establish an agency, and are back by the
shores of Botany Bay by sunset on the fol-
lowing evening. We feel remarkably well
and free from fatigue, for there is no' doubt
that this luxurious travel in the pure air
of the higher altitudes is conducive to good
health. At the factory we learn that our
representative in Newcastle has temporarily
relinquished business for the hectic pleasures
of a jag. “Shake him up!” says the boss.
So, in half an hour or so, we are at Port
Hunter, spend more time than he is worth
in hunting up and terrifying our fat-headed
fellow-worker, and sight the earth-lights of
the factory aerodrome about half-past eight
in the evening. The train journey into Syd-
ney is a most insufferable fatigue by contrast
with the light and pleasant Sittings which
have constituted the day’s work.
In the smoking-room of the Commercial
Travellers' Club, old Hilary Bing, lately of
Bayonet Whisky, Ltd., dips his red nose
into his 10th glass of the opposition firm’s
yellow dope, and sighs regrets for the old
days.
“Yes,” he grumbles, “it’s different now.
Five years back th’ Firm had three travel-
lers on each of the three main lines of rail-
way and their branches. What’s th’ game
now? Why one cock-sparrow, hardly
out of his teens, does the three lines on his
pat! How th’ blazes is a man to live in
such times?”
The Curtis leans over to one side, and you
lean the other way, following the instinct to
balance. Far down below—--7000 feet down—
is the white aerodrome. The machine swoops
to the left in a spiral nose-dive that tilts the
horizon up in a grotesque and unnatural
fashion, and the engine stops, only the rush
of the ’plane keeping the propeller twirling
as you corkscrew slowly—that is, compara-
tively slowly—down to earth. You climb
out of the ’plane—conscious of the largeness
of your feet, and the frailty of the varnished
linen stretched across the wings-r-with a
feeling of certain assurance that the day of
the Flying Bagman is very close at hand.
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Roar from the Ballot-box.
It is, at the time of writing, the emphasis of
the repudiation rather than the rejection of
the Ministerial proposals with regard to
so-called Conscription which lingers as a sur-
prise. Heavily handicapped by the burden
of a blundering, hysterical, abusive Jonah,
the case for compulsory service has all along
been prejudiced and the war effort of the
country hampered. Only by some miracle
could the opposition stirred up by Hughes’s
reckless violence have been prevented from
finding expression in hostility to the cause of
which, by manoeuvring in holes and corners,
he had become the head and front. And
miracles are not happening nowadays. How-
ever, there the vote is. and William Hughes
can look at his handiwork ; also those men
who. in defiancte of experience, permitted him
to fasten himself upon the country as its
“leader,” can contemplate the result and
explain what they think of it. But what
is going to happen next? We are more con-
cerned about that. It pleased Hughes re-
cently to threaten the people that unless they
voted “Yes” he would wash his hands of his
pledges—that if compulsory service did not
come through the referendum it would be
introduced some other way. Hughes is ill-
balanced enough to do anything. But he has
saner colleagues who will, it is hoped, at last
assert themselves and take steps to prevent
the little mischief-maker setting a match to
a powder-magazine. Having appealed to
ChESAR, and O.ESAR having answered, we
must put up with the consequences. Only
one power can undo what was done last
week ; and that power is the people. The
sooner there is an appeal to them the better
—not on a question surrounded by trickery,
but as to whether they will have an admin-
istration conducted by those who now stand
for “No.”
[To express the opinions of The Bulletin, by
S. H. Prior, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214
George-street, Sydney.]
The March Past.
Time stands to take the salute as the troops
of 1917 march to the place where the
Past is coaled up for the long voyage to
Oblivion. First in the ranks move the long
ragged lines of the Sorrows. Crepe on arm
and with tattered colors drooping, they limp
slowly by. Long casualty lists, big hopes
that had no fulfilment and black shadows
gathering—the' closing year will be remem-
bered as one of grim suffering and strange
terrors that passed very near to the deeps
of despair. According to Kitchener, we
were to begin seriously hammering the Ger-
mans in the last months of 1917. But
if we hammered ' them a little we
did not break them; ‘ and they re-
turned blow for blow. Russia—the road-
roller —has become a mere discordant babbie
of voices; Roumania is gone; nothing has
happened in the Balkans; I ranee has been
brought almost to a standstill. For such as
this we have paid a fearful price m blood and
tears. Even the impassive face of lime is
heavy as the troops of Sorrow go by. But close
upon their heels, with buoyant step, comes
the legion of Achievement. With all its
blighted hopes. 1917 cannot be talked down
as a year of failure. Very solidly and
menacingly the British might has grown
on that big testing-ground we call the
Western Front. In battle after battle
the Teuton has been proved inferior to the
Briton, and has learned a grim respect tor a
foe that could accomplish under the strain
of war miracles which in their way eclipse
his own preparations through the. years of
peace. In two Asiatic theatres British arms
have steadily triumphed, and the taking of
Jerusalem is a deed of magnitude. The last
remnant of the German overseas possessions
is in our hands; and even the submarine
danger looks a little less formidable. Time
never in all his years saw quite such a troop
as this ; and when it is recalled that practi-
cally every member of the corps enlisted
since August, 1914, there is reason for the
pride in their bearing. Follow the Hopes,
with a light in their eyes that is good for
victory. With all its failures, 1917 closes with
promise. Man for man, the Teutons know
that the Allies are their masters; and the
most desperate rally of the Kaiser’s legions
will ne\er shake the great confidence
that France and England who have held
them so long can hold them still. The new
shipbuilding efforts will relieve the menace
of the submarine; the British navy is
stronger than ever and as ready as ever for
a new Trafalgar. And, above all, there is
the sober preparation of America to enter
the war. The last footsteps die away, and
a strong faith remains that the white banner
of peace will flutter in the next procession.
The Pill with the Coat Rubbed Off.
In orations immediately preceding the poll
Prime Minister Hughes made a point of
the woe awaiting Australia if, as the result
of a German victory, the Commonwealth had
to give German manufacturers a free market
for their goods. Hughes knows very well
that this is only a small instalment of what
Australia would be up against in the event
of Germany fixing the terms of peace. But
take Hughes’s statement as it stands as
an appeal to the Protectionist. What does
it amount to? No Australian Protectionist
who understands his doctrine can show that
at rock bottom there is any difference between
the free admission of German and other goods
to Australia. They both mean the same
thing. There is no more reason to dread the
consequences of admitting German manu-
factures free and paying for them than of
getting the same things in more ample quan-
tity with a p.n. and going through the joke
of putting up the price to the consumer by
levying a tax on the goods, and thus raising
revenue and filling the warehouse by the
same loan operation. The truth is that the
Protection Australia has had against foreign
goods has been more or less a fiction, and
always must be so while the country goes
on living above its means and hypothecating
its credit. Every million borrowed abroad
means a million’s worth of goods. It is
true that the tariff has allowed certain indus-
tries a chance to start into life; but it is
also true that the'y have in the main rested
on the insecure foundation of boom created
by borrowing. In what way would the
interests of Australia be more damaged by
admitting free, say, £5,000,000 worth of paid-
for German goods than by getting six times
the volume of goods from various other
places, putting a duty on them, and promising
to pay 5 per cent, per annum until the next
generation had liquidated the bill? There is
only one ans\yer. Australia has never had
Protection from the German or anyone else,
nor ever will by the dodge of framing a tariff
schedule and then rushing to Cohen for
ways and means of throwing it out of gear.
The country’s problem in the past was to
satisfy the national requirements by local
effort, paying the bill for anything not avail-
able on the premises—and doing it all out
of the national earnings. The problem to-
day is no different.
The Human Longing to Silence the
Other Talker.
That Cabinet memorandum of Holman’s
which drove the No contingent to fury in
the last lap was a queer illustration of the
yearning that takes possession of the elected
person to give censors a job. This is par-
ticularly the case with democrats. As a
rule, the monarchical and aristocratic sys-
tems produce individuals who, if the common
herd go to church regularly, refrain from
composing critical verse and circulating ribald
cartoons, generally show a wide tolerance of
opinion. Among young democracies the
man thrown to the top finds himself nine
times out of 10 panting to throttle someone
and devise methods for stereotyping public
thought. The newspaper is, by this indi-
vidual, secretly loathed, and he spends at
least a substantial part of each waking day
devising methods for handing it a whiff of
chloroform. Holman, for instance, if the
memorandum expressed his wishes, did not
want to see the newspapers giving the com-
munity a fair account of what was happen-
ing at the Front, discussing the progress of
the war in the light of an ample public
understanding, or criticising the administra-
tive end of the business. He wanted a law
compelling the newspapers to print columns
of dope “selected” by censors and other
professional manufacturers of soothing
syrup. What he expected was to be gained
by this, Heaven only knew at the time; but
even Holman himself knows now what the
result would have been, for it is just such a
system as this that Australia has really
been working under for the best part of
three years. Looking back over that time
and surveying the drivel the Australian press
was, if not actually compelled to print about
the war, at least coerced into publishing, is
like contemplating the misspent days of
youth. The hand of the Censor was heavy
on the land and the consequences to the pub-
lic mind wholly disastrous. But men of the
Holman type can only bring themselves to
see the evils of a censorship when somebody
else is exercising it. On the other hand, it
can be said with complete confidence that the
people who yell the loudest against the anaes-
thetic in the other fellow’s power could them-
selves be relied on to use it just as frequently.
Tolerance is a rare virtue. And in this con-
nection it can never be forgotten that it was
a Labor Ministry which gave Australia the
War Precautions Act and all that came of
it. The Bulletin has no doubt, judging
by the glee with which this Act was passed
and the venom w-ith which anyone who dared
to advocate an extension of compulsory mili-
tary service has been pursued, that the men
who in another camp are the most fervent
advocates of free discussion would be just
as ready to dragoon the public with a cen-
sorship if they had a chance. The only
difference would be that they would try to
shut every mouth but their own, instead of
conforming to what the other person ordered.
Give a man a brief authority and the pre-
cariousness of his hold upon it is almost
invariably the measure of his anxiety to give
a tinpot imitation of the Great Mogul.
Cocky and His Wheat.
With a view to exposing the “malicious
statements” made by some unspecified per-
sons who with “sickening, hypocritical cant”
had been “playing the dirty game of Ger-
many,” Prime Minister Hughes last week
threw a mass of figures into the newspapers
about the wheat pool. The purpose was to
show that by selling the Australian farmer’s
wheat for him at 4s. 9d. a good deal had been
made; that the British Government by buy-
ing Canadian wheat at 9s. 2d. had found
the transaction more profitable than paying
4s. 9d. for Australian; that it was untrue to
say that the British Government had made
large profits out of the Australian trans-
actions. In support Hughes quoted the pre-
vailing. rates of freight between _ certain
countries, and arrived at the conclusion that
if these had to be paid on Australian wheat
by private shippers now the current London
quotation would leave a net return of only
Is. per bushel. And in the main
Hughes is right. The Australian farmer
has been paid for wheat which, as events
have turned out, would have been absolutely
unsaleable had not John Bull previously
become the buyer and taken the risk of
transport. But neither Hughes when he
started the pool, nor the British Govern-
ment when it bought the wheat, nor anyone
else in the wide world, expected that thou-
sands of tons of vegetables were going to
be stranded at this end of the transaction.
The criticism levelled against the manage-
ment was that for some occult reason the
Australian wheat-grower’s produce was sold
cheaper than Canadian and Argentine wheat.
And so it was. The allegation that this was
accounted for by high freights is no more
true than that someone or other was not
making an immense profit—probably the
British Government. A statement of
accounts showing the transactions of the
Victorian Wheat Commission to June, 1917,
reveals that 27,565,011 bushels were sold in
London at an average of 6s. 3d., and that
the charges for freight, commission, ex-
change, insurance and so on were Is. 7d. a
bushel. Now, during the greater part of
this time the market price of wheat in Lon-
don and Liverpool ranged from 9s. to 10s.
3d. a bushel. What people who were neither
“malicious” nor “playing a dirty game”
wanted to know during that time was—
why? And why did Hughes sell 20,000,000
bushels of Australian wheat in one line at
4s. f.o.b. when the English consumer was
paying Os. and when freight, etc., did not
eat up more than Is. 7d.? It is no
answer to say that the march of events has
justified the 'pool. The happenings of this
year are not the tests to apply to what
occurred previously, nor do the freights of
3017 explain the transactions of 1915 and
1910. The available facts all go to justify
the general contention of the Prime Minister
that Australian wheatgrowers have been
lucky. But to say that until the torpedo
began to work it was more profitable for
Britain to pay Canada 9s. 2d. for wheat than
trt buy in Australia at 4s. 9d. is merely silly.
The whole truth is that Britain got a lot of
ridiculously cheap wheat from Australia, and
bought a lot more which will be mighty dear
because it will never be shifted.
“Bows” speaks from the bridge:—
A Judge the other day in a Sydney court com-
mented regretfully on the large proportion of
foreigners composing the crew of a certain vessel
which had been in trouble. Another wigged gent, a
month or two back, whilst holding an inquiry into a
stranding, made somewhat similar remarks. But
neither lawyer seemed to be able to point a way out.
As long as we refuse to compel shipowners to carry
boys for deck-\tork there will be little hope of ever
finding half enough Australians to man Australian
vessels. Some vessels carry one or two ordinary sea-
men, who, of course, must have had some seafaring
experience (in the eyes Of the law, not less than two
years’ service). From an educational point of view,
these don’t count. IVe must compel ships to carry
boys and so bring up our own ordinary and able-
seamen. Under present conditions, if a seaman dies
or leaves Australia he must be replaced from
abroad; and as there has been any time this last 30
years a notorious shortage of British seamen in
British ships, in three cases out of four he will be
replaced by an alien.
In the stokehole things are different. An able
young man without seafaring experience may be, and
often is, signed on in the larger coasting vessels as
a trimmer, so it is not uncommon to find a fair pro-
portion of Australian natives as firemen.
In the same way the engine-rooms of Australian
steamers are practically manned by Australian
engineers owing to the facilities the various shops
offer boys to serve their time. But on deck, there
being no thorough apprentice system in our ships,
most of our deck officers and masters are importa-
tions, and many of them hail from alien countries.
If a certain country is drawn into this war on the
side of Germany, Australian ships will either go to
sea seriously shorthanded or else be manned by
duffers.
The poor, dilapidated old Party mule was
trotted out again last week for public in-
spection, and never before looked so miser-
able. No question was ever so far from
party as that over which the Australian
people had been wrangling for a month or
so. Yet there was Joe Cook dragging up
out of the forgotten past particulars of how
Frank Tudor voted on the Australian navy
issue, Tudor feebly calling upon Australia
to rally round Labor and vote “No,”
Hughes yelling that the choice was between
him and the Devil, and 423 minor indi-
viduals roaring that the vote was a party
vote and the side which got the least sup-
port at the ballot was for ever damned.
Apart altogether from the merits of the
question there was an indecency about all
this almost incredible. The theory of the
various spruikers seemed to be that upon
every patriotic citizen there devolved a duty
of either giving the P.L.L. a punch or
stoushing W. Hughes as a preliminary to
dealing with the Kaiser. Whether the Ger-
man Imperial crocodile cares 2d. about the
P.L.L. or Hughes is a question on which
this paper has no information ; but it has a
strong suspicion that if, in the intervals of
dropping a tear for the widows and orphans
of the Fatherland, and ordering bombs to be
deposited on the kids elsewhere, the brute
hears of what transpired at this end of the
world, it will reckon that a vanity greater
than ever inflated a Hohenzollern prince can
take possession of a democracy. That the
piffling squabbles between a few dozen place-
hunters hungry for authority and patronage
should be so magnified would at any other
time have seemed incredible. Yet a divinity
or something shapes our ends, and if from
this wretched debauch of partisan rancor
the Australian people have learned what
Party really is—to appreciate how destruc-
tive of even their own local liberties it may
be —then will they begin to get a grasp of
what democratic government actually means.

“Cato'* in reply to Randolph Bedford
(B. 13/12/H7) : —-
(1) Bedford complains because I stated that
Ryan and co. had more concern for cheap chops
than for reinforcements. We are told that Mr. Ryan
and Mr. Hunter appeared on a recruiting plat-
form only to be insulted. Nobody glories in the
foolish manner in which these Ministers were treated.
But when a Government opposes the only decent
method of getting recruits, and does not even follow
the example of private firms and the N. S. Wales
Government in giving its servants the difference
between A.I.F. pay and their salaries, there is
nothing harsh about a reference to its preference for
cheap chops. For the Government is concerned for
cheap chops.
(2) Bedford refers to Ministers who have sent
sons to the war. I cannot remember a single in-
stance of any public declamation that the individual
Ministers lack patriotic spirit. Bedford is over-
sensitive on the point. But when Australia’s war
policy is made a question of party politics, and every
Labor member in Queensland has acquiesced in the
Anti-Conscriptionist leading of Trades Hall, why
should Bedford squeal when anybody says his party
is not playing the game?
(3) Bedford states that the Government is not
addicted to Funk. He says it settled a strike with-
out compromise and without giving away the law.
If the reference is to the northern railway strike, he
talks through his hat. The northern railwavmen
refused to abide by the decision of the State Arbi-
tration Court, and struck. The Government was not
game to back up the Court, but compromised by
getting the men to start work on a promise that
their dispute would be settled before a Judge
borrowed from the outside world. As a matter of
fact, the unions settled the northern strike. Southern
unions came to the aid of the Government. But a
Government that erects Arbitration machinery and
then runs around the continent and swims to Maori-
land for a Judge to settle its disputes is suffering
from Funk.
The Year’s End.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The voices of the wind and wave
They sigh the Old Year’s requiem ;
The dead are calling from the grave
Good friends, a little space I crave
To turn aside and think df them.
They were as even you and I
When you and I were young as they;
And yet they knew the way to die —
Come, think with me, and tell me why
It should be thus with hearts so gay.
Ah, blessed be the gracious God
Who, moulding us from clay and dew,
From morning dew and clay untrod,
So breathed Himself into the sod
That we, at best, grow Godlike, too.
For, treading pleasure underneath,
These glory-souls, our country’s flower,
They rose responsive to that breath
And looked into the face of Death,
And did not tremble at his power.
Should it not make us sure and tough
As tested steel, and unafraid,
To feel, though Fate ride robber-rough,
That we are fashioned of the stuff
Whereof these heroes, too, were made?
Though they are dead and o’er them bends
A people’s soul in mourning mood,
Proud Honor at their grave attends:
Henceforth we are a nation, friends,
By right of sacrificial blood.
The care-free days of youth are gone;
What once we were no more we are;
And dead are all the dreams that shone
Ere we were bruised and hammered on
The ringing anvils of red war.
The Spirit of Immortal Times,
With lights that dazzle and entice,
Is vitalising all earth’s climes;
Once more in golden tones it chimes
The anthem of High Sacrifice.
And yet, though Fame be very fair,
And great the yearning and desire
Of hero-hearts to do and dare,
Behold, their stands an empty chair
Beside a cold and ashen fire!
Because, as even you and I,
They loved and were beloved, my friends,
Not all the glory-stars on high,
The splendid things for which men die,
Can for their passing make amends.
There is a house that waits in vain
To give them entrance at its door,
When frost’s afoot or chilly rain ;
There is a track across the plain
That they, alas, shall ride no more.
A whip, a saddle and a spur!
(Ah, love-lit rides! The moon above,
Sweet scents around, soft winds astir!)
God give him rest! And what of her?
Why ask? Is love not always love?
Dear eyes that pain has made divine,
Sad eyes that burn with tears unshed,
Within whose depths are griefs that pine
And pilgrim thoughts that seek the shrine,
The grave of their beloved dead ;
Dear eyes, dear hearts, dear folk, tear-blind,
Who greet each morn with grief anew,
Pale cheeks grown cold, and foreheads
lined, —
Since God is good and Heaven is kind,
There shall be recompense for you !
The Old Year dies: and o’er the waves,
Wind-borne, there comes a requiem
Deep-chanted by a sea that laves
The shores they loved. Oh, may their graves
Give goodly rest and peace to them !
N.S.W.

HEAD OFFICE: 14 MARTIN PLACE,
SYDNEY.
BRANCHES in VICTORIA AND SOOTH AUSTRALIA.
The Fourth Christmas of War.
A report received by way of Amsterdam
pictures Berlin turning handsprings over the
Russian peace or whatever it may be. Re-
ports from Amsterdam are something like
the “telegrams from Petrograd” in the dear
old times when we were all three years
younger and correspondingly credulous. Yet
this yarn about hilarity at Berlin may well
he true. Most of the joy may be manufac-
tured ; yet viewed from the Boche’s angle the
collapse of Russia is a big thing. It means
not only the elimination of an enemy, but a
strengthening of the German arms against
other enemies. Coupled with this story
about popular rejoicings comes the state-
ment of a Socialist member of the Reichstag
that the military leaders in the Fatherland
are “drunk with visions of victory.” If this
is somewhere about right it is reasonable to
fear that the break-up of the Central Powers,
of which we have been hearing for three
years and three months, has been postponed.
In any ease, it is merely foolish to look upon
the military position in Europe to-day as not
being more favorable to the German than at
any other period since the campaign of 1914.
What is happening in Italy is not altogether
clear, except that the defence is being hard-
pressed and all hands are praying for winter
to take possession of the northern passes.
The feeding of the Italian armies has appa-
rently become a question of some difficulty,
though probably more easy to settle than
the enemy Will be when he gets busy again
in the Spring. The forces opposed to Italy
will most certainly be then heavily reinforced
with men and guns drawn from what used
to be the Eastern Front. Northern France
and Belgium are again under snow. There
will be hard times there and perhaps hard
fighting in the slush, for inducements to Yon
Hindenbuf.g to do his worst without delay
are substantial. Time is not on his side in
that section. Not only is L T ncle Sam coming,
but Britain is getting ready for a further call
upon the nation’s manhood. The day is very
near when every man and woman in Eng-
land will be either fighting or working for
the common cause —a reflection which doesn’t
make it any easier to swallow the pill the
Australian voters compounded last Thurs-
day.
• ♦
In the midst of cursing Hughes for a
bungler and looking round for somebody to
take his place, there is a good Australian
who is liable to be overlooked, and his name
is Billjim. In many cases he is a tired
Billjim, with wounds yet hardly healed,
and he is doing two men’s work and taking
more than two men’s risks, because the
required reinforcements have not gone to his
relief. The No-Conscriptionists say that
volunteers in sufficient numbers can be got.
It is their business now to get them. Nor is
it any less the business of those who voted
Yes, because they professed to be even more
solicitous about reinforcing Bill. For a
month past the newspapers have been filled
with the reports of the struggle to get votes.
Suppose now that an equal struggle is made
to get recruits. For over a month it has
taken columns of print every day to give
even a list of the meetings called to recruit
voters. Suppose now that an equal effort
is made to recruit reinforcements. It is
little to our credit that hundreds of men
who have taken the stump to bring citizens
to the polling-place because they said that
voluntary recruiting had failed, have never
said a public word to bring citizens to the
recruiting-depdt and prevent that failure.
Almost a year ago a State Premiers’ Con-
ference was held in Melbourne to finalise
the agreements under which the Common-
wealth was to take over the work of re-
patriating Billjim. A Repatriation Bill
was introduced to the Senate and passed by
both Houses, the Act to operate from a
date to be proclaimed. Millen became
Minister of Repatriation, and later Lockyer,
of the fizzled Inter-State Commission, was
made Controller. By this time it would not
be unreasonable to expect the Government’s
scheme, if it has one, to have been estab-
lished ; in fact, a private business carrying
two expensive heads like Senator Millen
and Nicholas Lockyer would be running
on ball-bearings in a mere month or two.
What of Australia’s great repatriation
scheme? Well, to begin with, the Repatria-
tion Act has never been proclaimed.
Further, all the repatriation work in the six
States is still being undertaken by State
officers. When the Federal Act is pro-
claimed. the Repatriation Minister will
seemingly have to find his own staff, the
loaned officers passing back into their old
State-service jobs again. In which case the
experts with several years’ experience, who
have been wrestling with the difficulties of
the problem, are to be dropped for appointees
who will have to learn all about it. If the
Federal Government cannot or does not
want to secure the services of these State
officers it should not delay in organising the
staff that will have to carry the full weight
of the scheme. The future of Australia s
300,000-odd best citizens now under arms is
too precious to be jeopardised by official
bungling.
About the wildest yarn in circulation last
week was a rigmarole that the employment
of ships to carry troops from Australia
represented a treacherous dissipation of
tonnage in the interests of the enemy. “If,”
ran the yarn, “the vessels to take 7000
troops a month from Australia were used
by America. 49.000 soldiers could be landed
in France monthly from the United States.’
The simple answer is that unless the Aus-
tralian sick and wounded are sent from
England to N’York or kept in the vicinity
of the Thames, transports must bring them
home to the Commonwealth. And the trans-
ports must either go back again loaded or
make the journey empty so far as the troop-
ship accommodation is concerned. They
are vessels fitted specially for the service.
To pull the innards out of them every time
they reach Australia so that they could be
filled to the decks with wheat would make
the cost of handling, already enormous, prac-
tically ruinous. Tiie Bulletin can under-
stand objections among the voluntaryists to
going to the Front, but it can’t understand
people putting obstacles in the way of bring-
ing our injured lads home.
“Murkah’s” suggestion
The frequent accounts of privations in open boats
(the torpedoed Rochester provided the latest in-
stance) suggest that ships should carry a small wire-
less set, complete in a case, which could be installed
in, or transferred to, one of the boats before launch-
ing. A complete transmitter, including a hand-
driven dynamo, telescopic masts for aerial, aerial
wires, etc., capable of transmitting up to about 100
miles, and a receiver—all can be packed in a case
less than 2ft. square. While in the danger zone such a
set could be hooked up to an auxiliary aerial and
kept as a stand-by in ease of accident to the main
set. As mentioned in Plain English recently, de-
pending on one aerial is a dangerous and stupid
proceeding. I once sailed with a man who argued
that ships should not carry boats —that if anything
happened the ship the best thing was to get the
misery over. He once spent two weeks in a boat.
The accounts of sufferings so often reported lately
make me think his contention wasn’t so silly as it
sounded at the time.

Does.the mental patient get a square deal?
Some months ago Chief Secretary Donald
McLeod promised Vic. Labor-leader Billson
that there would be a thorough inquiry into
the case of a deceased patient on whose
body the doctors had discovered traces of
violence which the asylum authorities could
not or would not explain. A week or so
ago the N. S. Wales force got rid of a con-
stable who had been convicted of brutally
ill-using a prisoner. And here is the
significant fact: this violent young Hop
had had recent experience as a hospital
attendant. Did he merely make the mis-
take of applying to a sane man, who could
be called as a witness, the methods learnt
in dealing with the insane who have not
this protection? Public confidence in the
humanity of the asylum system will begin
to wane if the curtain keeps on blowing aside
in this way.

Ten years in Papua :
Papua was handed over by the Imperial Govern-
ment in 1906 ; so this is a convenient time, after 10
years’ operations, to take stock.
Our figures are still far too small. Exports last
year were only £125,500; imports were £223,000,
and admittedly that isn’t healthy. But we are still
in the development stage, and very much of our
expenditure is on capital account. Nevertheless,
since the Commonwealth took possession imports
have risen 250 p.c. and exports 200 p.c. The in-
crease is principally due to the extension of the
planting industry. Mining has proceeded much more
slowly.
When the Commonwealth flag was hoisted in 1906
the area under cocoanuts was only 1400 acres; this
has risen to over 34,000 acres, an extension of more
than 24 acres for every acre planted when Australia
took over from Downing-street. Para-rubber, of
which only a negligible area was planted in 1906,
now occupies 7671 acres and promises to be the
most lucrative proposition in tropical culture. Hemp,
of the Mexican sisal variety, is responsible for 5000
acres, an increase of 100 acres for every acre culti-
vated in 1906. Tobacco, a new crop, is being
grown on 200 acres, and the leaf is manufactured in
the Territory. The total area under plantations in
1906 was only 1467 acres. Last year 47.560 acres
were under cocoanuts, rubber, hemp, tobacco and
minor products, an increase of 46,039 acres in the
decade. This acreage is divided among 243 planta-
tions. Some two millions of Australian money are
invested in the properties.
The imports were mainly foodstuffs, drapery, hard-
ware, machinery, clothing, boots and leather goods,
timber and oils.
The principal exports last year were gold (bullion
and ore) £43,000, copra £19,000, rubber £15,000,
copper ore £lO,OOO, hemp £12,000, trochas shell
£6OOO, cotton £l6OO, pearls £lOOO, sandalwood
£l4OO. bSche-de-mer £3OOO and maize £soo—a
list which shows the Territory to be equal to any
other part of the Empire in diversity of products.
Such things as bark, canes, timber and gums are
still hardly touched, but in years to come will be a
big factor. At present they are passed by owing to
the difficulty and expense of transport. With the
exception of a few hundred pounds, the rubber was
all plantation grown, and in competition in the
world’s markets it realised top prices. The coming
year should witness at least double the production.
The export of copra will also increase as the planta-
tions opened in 1907 and 1908 come into the produc-
ing stage. At least 5000 acres were due to give
their first crop this current year, and the value of
next year’s crop will be greater again. ' The future
of this industry is very bright.
The world upheaval has affected settlement con-
siderably, but with peace Papua should get her share
of attention from investors driven out of the older
lands by post-war taxation. Both rubber and cocoa-
nuts are an attractive proposition to the man with
limited capital and lots of energy; and Papua has
plenty of rich land for this type of settler.
Given free entry for her products into Australia,
Papua could supply all the tea, coffee, cocoa, sugar,
maize, sago, tapioca, gum and kapok which the
Commonwealth now buys from Java and other foreign
countries. And instead of assisting pro-Germans and
other undesirables, she would be trading with her-
self; for the development of Papua is in her own
hands—though the average Australian doesn’t realise
it yet.
It isn’t the Labor strongholds that earn
the bitterest contempt on the question of
Conscription and reinforcements. The prize
belongs to some of the “Liberal” country dis-
tricts. There is Burramine South, for in-
stance, in the north-east Murray country
(Vic.), of which Tungamah is the centre,
with the terrible distinction of not having
sent a single soldier to the Front. Tunga-
mah used to be a Liberal stronghold even
in the days when Labor was at its zenith,
while at the recent State elections the vot-
ing throughout that corner of Victoria was
largely Liberal —enough, at any rate, to
keep all local Labor candidates out of Par-
liament. But it has been giving its heart-
felt support, in cash, yells and rotten eggs,
to the Labor propaganda in opposition to
the reinforcements proposals. When Labor
finds itself getting this sort of support it
can make dead-certain that it is on the wrong
track.
The worst fault of the Defence Depart-
ment is that it doesn't give itself or any-
body else time for calm reflection. You no
sooner say you’ve got an idea for winning
the war than it rushes you off your feet to
get it. George Austin, of the Vic. Institute
of Architects, came forward, shortly after
the war began, w T ith particulars of a heavier-
than-air flier, allegedly uncapsizable. In
those days he made an effort to catch the
public eye with modest window-displays in
the suburbs. Later on there was a public
trial at Heidelberg of an Austin model,
which was set free to fly by itself. It flew
all right, and didn’t upset until it hit a
tree. Now, not more than three years after
Austin announced that he had this idea, the
Defence Department is going to submit
specifications to the British authorities.
You’d almost think, from this precipitancy,
that the Defence Department wanted to icin
the blessed war.
A worried returning-ofEcer has been curs-
ing the place where C. J. Dennis rusticates.
He had fixed up a private house as a polling-
booth for the few r stray citizens who nest
amongst the tall timber at Toolangi. and the
ballot-box and voting papers had been depo-
sited there. Then scarlet-fever broke out in
the house, and a new booth had to be
scratched up and the ballot-box and papers
carefully disinfected.
The exiled Belgian art-collector and
expert, Cels, is dead in Adelaide at the
early age of 36. His collection of paintings
I—which1 —which he reckoned enormously valuable—
is still in Sydney, for he was lucky enough
to get it away when he lost everything else
in the Hun invasion. He fought for his
country till he was wounded and gassed, and
then proposed to make Australia his home.
Adelaide Art Gallery is awaiting with some
interest the result of inquiries he made, on
a return trip to Europe this year, into an
unknown picture which it picked up long
ago when R.C. Archoishop Reynolds’s effects
were dispersed. Cels, on seeing it, suggested
that it was something very good and valu-
able. lie wrote from Europe that he had
found out all about it in Paris; but when he
came back he was in a dying condition, and
nobody seems to know where to find the
verdict that he had gleaned.

Allan Wilkie is having a hot time in “For
the King,’ a new Cromwellian disturbance
which reached the Melbourne Princess’s on
Saturday. Allan appears as the Cavalier
Captain Harry Clavering, and it is his duty
to make the Roundheads look like Block
heads and earn the warm favor of Charles I
The gallant warrior suffers terribly for his
allegiance, and is savagely biffed in his
struggles with Colonel Stephen Wenlock a
large and nasty Ironside who covets M rs
Clavering and achieves rare mischief in the
name of the Lord. Wenlock twice ambushes
the reckless Clavering, and each time the
Captain is saved by the miraculous resource
of Dick Wildfire, a semi-loony youth who
hugs his trail for the purpose of being handv
in case of trouble. In the first ambush
Richard rescues Clavering’s priceless des-
patches and sends them to the King, while
the hero is grabbed with the object of pro-

three days to say good-bye to his wife and

cheild, and he leaves his pal, Rupert

carted away and shot for misbehavior un-
becoming a Puritan and a "entleman.
* * * *
The piece is an innocent type of Walter
Howard story, utterly artless and brimming
over with fight and feeling. The stoush is
the most substantial thing in it, barring,
perhaps, the figure of Arthur Styan. who, in
the part of Oliver Cromwell, will satisfy the
most voracious desire for solids. Allan
Wilkie has energv and to spare for the job
of Clavering, and Vivian Edwards can never
get a wink of sleep, so busy is he kept with
the murderous intentions of Captain Wen-
lock. Walter Hunt’s classical features are
borrowed for the role of Rupert Vane, whilst
Eilian Lloyd is not obtrusivelv feminine as
the witless Wildfire. Mrs. Clavering is
played by Miss Olive Wilton, and her little
daughter, whose prayers tickle the fancy of
O. Cromwell, gets a large head of hair from
Dorothy Seacombe. Marie Xey figures as the
beauteous creature-comfort promised to
Rupert aforesaid. Also there is Elwyn
Harvey lavishing more comeliness on Pen-
elope Pennyquick than any reasonable person
has a right to expect.
“Aladdin,” at Melbourne King’s, is a
feast of clothes and curves in 15 courses
that should tickle the light palates of holiday
audiences. The menu is not overburdened
with story, and the characters forget their
purpose therein ; but those are discrepancies
allowable in a show that makes frivolous
irrelevaneies the order of the night. The

best feature, as usual, is the quantity of
gill that gives pictorial weight to many
solos and swarms in marches of patriotic im-
port, filling the stage with color and smile
The scenery, by Clint and Rowell, is also
effective, the sets ranging from a cloisonne
and porcelain palace to a mystic wood where
the weird landscape trembles and goblins
prance to the mutter of drums. In this scene
occurs a pretty effect by several damsels in
the brief ballet skirts of old time coryphees,
whilst the same girleens, or others like them,
furnish a charming poke-bonnet background
to one of Olive Godwin’s songs.
As for the principals, they are likely to
make themselves felt when they have got
properly into their stride. Barry Lupino,
who earns a special prize as producer, capers
whimsically as Abanazar, and is beguiled
into protracted fooling by a rare fund of gags.
Bert Bailey gives Mrs. Twankey the profile
of a Roman emperor and a soft satiny manner
that has periods of droll quiet. Hi’s natural
humor is unstressed in speech, but his ward-
robe runs to quaint extravagances; a hat
decorated with toothbrushes and pocket-
mirrors being an arresting work of art.
Winifred La France, as Aladdin, presents
engaging contours to every angle of vision,
and is not lost in comparison with the tall
Princess (Olive Godwin), whose manifest
comeliness is inhabited by a voice of great
value. Fayette Perry is a fragile sunbeam
as Nicee. who is wooed by the robust Prince
Pekoe of Daisy Revette. In the job of the
Emperor, Andrew Iligginson is a court card
in every sense of the expression, whilst Bert
Barton, as the demon magician, dispenses
frightfulness and croaks threats with con-
siderable emphasis. Fred Macdonald and
John Beck, as two policemen, amount to only
a few abbreviated sentences, and Leslie
Donaghey is once conspicuous as a convul-
sive fraction of the grotesque “Splish-splash-
splosh” chorus in Mrs. Twankey’s kitchen.
Zola Terrell, a barefooted dancer who is all
rippling limbs and undulations, is an im-
ported specialist in sensuous Oriental meas-
ures. A harlequinade happening at the mat-
inees is bound to be popular with the
kiddies; and Heller and Morris, acrobats,
also figure prominently in a performance
that must inevitably grow smarter when
it suffers a reduction in bulk.

The J.C.W. children who began their ver-
sion of the Firmi’s 1916 panto, at Sydney
Royal last Saturday don’t need any excuse
on account of their youth. Perfectly drilled
and tutored, they put on “The House That
.Tack Built” in a finished way that chal-
lenges critical examination, and their show
comes out with honors. It does something
better than satisfy the critic who is old
enough to grow a stubble-beard. It amuses
the youngsters on the other side of the foot-
lights more than the best efforts of adult
players could ever do.
