The staging is adequate, the music (mostly
locally composed) sufficiently “catchy,” the
incidental variety acts are clever, and the
ballets brightly dressed. The dialogue, how-
ever, needs much pruning. Victor Prince,
the author, is somewhat ludicrous as Robin-
son Crusoe; his wife, the Dame (Walter
Cornock), is hard-working but hardly
humorous; Davy Jones, the Pirate King
(Chas. Zoli), delivers his pirate song with
verve; the three mariners do some clever
tumbling; Percy Clifton and Kennedy Black
make admirable wowsers ; Maud Fanning is
a quite satisfactory cannibal queen, and her
hula-hula scene, with three cannibal girls, is
an attractive bit of work; the beautiful
voices of Olive Sinclair, as a princess, and
Dorothy Hastings, as her lover, provide the
best duet in the show; Nellie Fallon, as the
heroine, is vivacious and possesses a delight-
ful voice with a doubtful pronunciation;
Nellie Kolle, the principal girl, has the voice
and the swagger for the part; and the goat,
the chimpanzee, the microbes and the sea-
nymphs are all up to sample.
** * *
In “The Bird of Paradise,” an American
drama of Hawaiian life, Melbourne Royal
has a drama suited to hot weather. The
story offers vague reminders of several pre-
vious mixed-marriage romances, including
“Madame Butterfly.” The atmosphere of a
lotus-eating languor is there, George Up-
ward’s scenery harmonises with the drowsy
musical accompaniments supplied by a little
crowd of Hawaiian natives, and nothing
disturbs the sensuous influence of the scenes
save a certain vulgarity of Yankee origin in
the action of the play.
* * * *
The moral lesson of the plot is purely and
emphatically American. A young New York
doctor comes to the heated island with a
high-minded girl for the alleged purpose of
finding a cure for leprosy. They strike a
drunken, brainy beachcomber (Frank
Harvey) and a native princess (Muriel
Starr)' who has the amorous temperament
of Carmen combined with the simple fidelity
of Madame Butterfly. The poor little doctor
(Louis Kimball) is a weak mortal. He looks
upon Muriel Starr in her attractive summer
garments, and is lost. Whilst the nice white
girl is inspiring the beachcomber to the high
and scientific life, Muriel reduces her curly-
haired doctor to degradation by marrying
him and mixing his drinks. Her love is his
undoing. After two years of lazy lawful
matrimony with the lady of inferior race,
Kimball is a pitiful spectacle of petulant in-
ebriety. whereas Harvey has earned a ten-
thousand-dollar testimonial from the Yankee
exploiters of Hawaii, and won the love of
the higkly-superior girl. Atholwood, as a
gloomy native priest, restores the luckless
princess to paganism in a measure, and she
is understood to jump into a blazing volcano
by way of appeasing the wrath of the vol-
canic god. Here endeth the moral lesson.
Muriel Starr has all the natural qualifi-
cations for the part of the hot Hawaiian
enchantress except an impossible loveliness ;
but her head of hair and her striking outfits
go far towards making her admirable in all
respects. Harvey, too, is happily placed as
the rather incredible waster who blossoms
into a choice example of true manliness.
Miss Tien Hogue, formerly of the Taits’ co.,
plays the nice, clean white girl with some
charm of blonde frigidity, and her brief love
scene with her convert is a sweet episode.
Kimball is not fully equal to the unthankful
job of falling into moral degradation under
the spell of a half-civilised donah. He puts
no detail into his manner of deteriorating,
nor expresses anything in particular except
lack of character and want of intellect. For
the rest, an impressive Atholwood in a state
of semi-nakedness ; George Bryant, as a loud
commercial Yankee disgusted by Kimball’s
weakness; Edwin Lester and Marion Clarke,
as a pair of wowser missionaries; Maggie
Moore and James Hughes, as well-meaning
pagans; and a number of Hawaiian natives
whose chanting and steel-stringed mandolins
are more artistically effective than the vol-
cano tableau which closes the unconventional
show.
“Dick Whittington,” the panto, at Mel-
bourne Majesty’s, opens with a seeming
intention to tickle the interest of the young,
but resolves itself into a mainly-spectacular
entertainment for those of older growth.
The dressing is bright throughout, and some
of Coleman’s cloths are very persuasive
works of art. The youth and gaiety of a
large crowd of girls and girleens remain
with them through frequent changes of
costumes, so for ordinary Christmas busi-
ness purposes the J.C.W. panto, should do
very well. Yet it leaves no strong impres-
sions on one’s mind, evokes no shrieks of
laughter to speak of, and is void of new
mechanical tricks. The star specialty is an
item of violent gymnastics by a trio who
have appeared before, and the daintiest of
the musical turns is a duet by two interesting
kiddies, who are probably the pair grown
older that worked together last Christmas.
The voice of one of them, and the unaggres-
sive precocity of both, are a delight.
As for the version of the venerable Whit-
tington story, it is a poor hotchpotch, dis-
tinguished only by the fact that the Dame
and idle ’Prentice conspire to shake Aider-
man Fitzwarren’s till and cast their guilt
upon the massive hero who is our old Tivoli
friend Vera Pearce. Vera is sadly lacking
in frivolity and vim as the principal boy.
Her limbs and method are equally solid,
and her personality is merely picturesque.
Miss Pauline Bindley is an Alice whose value
is confined to her head notes, and neither
Pearl Ladd nor Constance Cayley scores a
distinct hit at any stage of the proceedings.
After the panto, proper has spun itself out,
there comes a short series of irrelevant scenes
where the comedians Stigant, Roxbury and
Fullbrook get their longest chances. Stigant
is always pretty good as the stereotyped
Dame, but none of the broad comedy scenes
are refreshingly humorous, and the scene
with beehives in it grows utterly wearisome.
After giving a passing word of praise to
Villiers Arnold for his demon vocalism, one
may say of the Whittington panto, that it
dances itself into favor. The Ballet of War—
wherein Maggie Dickinson, who is aaain a
slim young fay, Sydney Yates and a lissome
brunette are principal interpreters—is a
delightful dumb show. Likewise the Vogue
Ballet, with its studies in strange fashions,
and the Flying Ballet, which is full of legs
and evolutions. The grace and agility of
Young Australia have never been better dis-
played than in the Firm’s present Christmas
annual; but the appeal to small boys and
girls is very occasional. There is nothing
much for the infant mind to catch on to; a
clown with a red-hot poker, or a bottle-nosed
baron whose stummic-k played tunes when
punched, would be a useful addition to the
gentle gambols of Le Brun as the inevitable
cat.
>:-. * *
J. and N. Tait belong to a school of humor-
ists that Australia can well do with. Not
below “Very Good Eddie” is their latest
catch, “The New Henrietta,” played in the
Palace (Sydney) on Saturday night. The
story might parallel that epic that will never
be written about Job the miserable and
Hughes the highly delighted, so wide and
diverse is the humor. A good cast, too.
Perhaps the best of the bunch is—well, let
there be peace. But see the show.

“The Red Widow” has only a few more
nights in which to throw superfluous bombs
nt a Czar who is no longer there. On Satur-

Bing Boys are Here.”
“Winnie Brooke, Widow,” the musical
play put on at Sydney Tivoli by Ada Reeve
ana co. as a Christmas attraction, is a crisp
novelty, and the electric Ada wires into it
and lights it up, lending extra candle-power
every now and then with a Reevian song.
The long cast includes Clarence Blakiston,
Talleur Andrews, Charles Albert and about
20 others who vrill be praised or blamed
next week when the most infernally-arranged
Christmas this paper has ever struggled with
has rushed over the horizon and broken its
neck on a pile of cheap imported toys.
Nieman and Kennedy, the tramp specialists,
and the Satinettos are headlining.
The major portion of the Christmas bill
at Melbourne Tilvoli is “Baby Mine,” which
was produced by J.C.W. at Sydney Criterion
two months ago, and proved but a poor little
effort at an all-night entertainment. In its
pruned and practised reappearance as a
vaudeville item, “Baby Mine” is more in the
fitness of things. The pretence of a plot
remains as thin as ever, but the incidental
larfs are broadened, and Beatrice Holloway
has developed her art of leaping into bed
until her agility almost excuses the hollow
cause thereof. There’s a superfluity of bed
and bedroom in this alleged comedy for
married people. The bed is a double one,
and three imitation infants, mere creatures
of celluloid, made for chucking about, are
associated with the commanding piece of
furniture. When they have been sufficiently
chucked about the story ceases, and the com-
pany lines up to receive the reward of its
labors. Beatrice Holloway and Bob Greig
(who never approaches the dignity of Rob-
ert) figure as leading voluntary workers. The
dolls have their labors thrust upon them.
For the rest of the bill four vaudeville turns
have been provided in the first part. Del-
more and Lee recite poetry of motion in an
aerial act, Rogero utters sweet sounds as a
Human Musical Box, and Reynolds and
White appear again. Until Saturday of next
week the hospitable doors of the Tivoli will
be opened twice daily, every afternoon a
matinee.
* * * *
The age of miracles is not past. The film
magnate is actually turning into a human
being who remembers that he was once a
schoolboy ; so the picture specially designed
for the kid becomes more frequent. This
week Sydney Lyceum has a winner for its
matinees in “Robinson Crusoe” —a desert
island, Man Friday, thrilling adventures and
all. The problem of the birth-rate is being
added and subtracted by Alice Brady at the
Crystal Palace, and this time it is called
“God’s Greatest Gift.” At the Strand Fanny
Ward does her darnedest, but fails to throw
any light on several situations in “The Crys-
tal Gazer”; while Mrs. Vernon Castle has
two-stepoed to the Lyric, where she and her
young man go through their impossible
“Stranded in Aroady.”

Hoyt’s Melbourne house promises some
rare programmes for 1918. The firm has
secured the sole Australian rights for the

Irene Vernon Castle is again the main
suit at Melbourne Britannia this week in
a further helping of the “Patria” serial.
Margery Wilson is another strong feature
on the" same screen in the illicit whisky
romance called “Mountain Dew.” The
week’s offerings at the Melba are Henry
Morey in “Richard the Brazen” and Virginia
Pearson in “A War Bride’s Secret.” Tne
latter can be recommended as a strong war
yarn that will hit the pacifist intellect
under the belt. Moreover, Virginia is great
in the leading rQle. She fills the eye with
her sumptuous charms.
Roasting weather in Adelaide has been all
in favor of outdoor shows, the Pierrots at
the Exhibition, the .Testers at Austral Gar-
dens. Nothing really great about either,
perhaps, yet good all round, and frivolous
enough for a hot night. Wondergraph
scores, too. in having an open-air pitch;
George Cohan is the star of the moment,
just as William S. Hart the tragic and
Chaplin the farcical run the show at West’s.
The Pav., however, still relies on feminine
attraction, with a Saunders lady who calls
herself “Jackie.” At the Royal, Fox films,
with the reliable Stuart Holmes; at the
Tivoli, still more melodrama; at the Majes-
tic, the immensely popular Denny and Duni-
gan, and soldier Copeland, whose thought-
reading turn is novel in that the “wizardry”
is done by a very small boy.

Beauty is King.
IjADIES! As culture begets taste, taste begets
beauty; even as music soothes the soul, so beauty
soothes the heart. It is true, all good things come
to an end, but you can delay that end from coming
too soon. Flowers even will fade without nourish-
ment and care. Heaven helps them who help them-
selves. The great war has made you the greatest
factor on earth, but, with the wastage of manhood,
in the struggle to come it will be the survival of the
fittest. Nature in her wonderful way has determined
that all life must display its beauty to the opposite
sex to gain approbation, and to each she has allotted
its own particular charm. Beauty is only skin deep,
and it is exposure to wind and weather principally that
prevents face from being as perfect as the rest
of your body. Your skin needs care and nourishment
to keep pace with the wastage caused from exposure.
In modern skin hygiene the aim is to cleanse and
improve the texture of the skin and develop and bring
back its latent beauty rather than gloss over imper-
fections. JOHN STRANGE W INTER 5s TOILET

glow to your cheeks. Dry, tender and chapped skins
are soothed and softened by their use. They are so

* * * *
A considerable fuss has been made over
General Allenby’s modest entry by foot into
holy Jerusalem. The correspondents on
the rocky spot represent that the ceremony
was arranged that way to stress the fact that
the Holy City hadn’t been “captured,” but
was merely being “occupied.” But this
paper, knowing the ruinous Turk, and hav-
ing inspected Jerusalem, guesses that Allenby
left his horse at home that morning because
the street is so narrow that even a Palestine
ass has to hold its breath while passing
through the narrower places. And presently
the street becomes an interminable flight of
steps. No British general with a regard for
his dignity w T ould attempt to ride a horse
up a ladder.

save women's souls for nothing. One un-

“138”: This is the record of an Australian
boy who wanted to go with his big brothers.
While still short of 16 he boarded an out-
going transport for Egypt. The matter of
a uniform was easy, and the kid landed
successfully and lived in various camps
around the desert (Zietoun and Tel-el-Kebir)
until the boys moved to France. Again he
managed to beat the embarkation people, and
got mixed up with some ironmongery at
Pozieres. Evacuated to England carrying
some of it with him, he was presently
granted 14 days’ “wounded furlough.' He
reported to a command depot to time, but
decided that Scotland was worth a visit.
There he was picked up by the military
police for being absent without leave and
was returned to his depot. Giving his cor-
rect name to the authorities and the umtieth
battalion as his unit, he puzzled Horseferry-
road for a time, until he explained that he
had never attested, that he wasn’t entitled
to wear the uniform according to K.R.R„
and had served for two years with the A.I.F.
without ever receiving pay ! Asked how on
earth he managed, he replied that he had
plenty of good cobbers who knew his history
and supplied him with coin. Now the heads
at A.H.Q. are scratching their ears and won-
dering what they are to do with Billjim s
Little Brother. What couldn’t you do with
a nation of him !
“Country Ped” : The record of some of the
little Yic. State schools up this way—l’m ou
the fringe of the Mallee —makes me feel very
mean when I’m not feeling very proud. Wlo t
do you think of little Cannum South, with
an average attendance of 19, which has sent
£7O to the Patriotic Fund during the last
year? Eighteen of our 19 betters belong to
the Young Workers’ Guild, whose members
have to earn their money. Fourteen have
earned £1 apiece. And there’s Nypo, with an
average attendance of less than 30. Every
child there is a member of the Young
Workers’ Guild. Twenty have earned £1
each, five have each earned £5 —chiefly by
farm work during holidays—and one £lO.
In addition the girls have knitted 186 pairs
of socks (one girl alone knitted 50 pairs) and
90 scarves, and they’ve all sent their love
to Bill, and told him about Rankine's crop
and the other things that it’s good to keep
Bill interested in, in dozens of letters.
* * * *
A Sydney reporter timed three short-
measure cases lately, and it makes brisk
reading. A wood and coal juggler was fixed
and fined in 2min. 29sec.; a fruit barrowman
who tried some sleight-of-hand got his lot in
2min. 31sec.; and a banana merchant who
tried to shove off a customer with only 14
fruit-sausages for 6d. when the banana-
drunkard expected to be loaded with 24. was
peeled by the Law in 4min. 12sec. But they
ere all retail frauds. It takes years and
years and years for the Law to even make
up its mind to prosecute a wholesale brigand.
In Melbourne the other day three or four
People were charged with selling as flannel a
“WHEN CONSTABULARY DUTY'S TO BE DONE."
RETURNED ISLAND TOURIST: “Take me in charge! What for ?”
B OBBY: "For carryin’ con-saled weppins."
substance that was barely on nodding terms
with wool. One miscreant produced his in-
voices, and showed that the stuff had been
sold to him as flannel. The P.M.. who should
have know better, asked why the wholesaler
wasn’t prosecuted. The uniformed Law re-
plied that it didn’t know—it had never seen
a wholesaler’s invoice before. How could it
be expected to —the practice of selling this
stuff as flannel had been in existence for pro-
bably no more than 20 years, one witness
swore! A barrow may be disposed of in
2min. Slsee.. but it takes a lot longer to put
through a Rolls Royce.
* * * *
“Jobber": Will you please ask Senator
Pearce what he is going to do about this?
On many occasions—l blush to have to tell
you—l’ve seen Australian soldiers marched
through the streets of Cairo. London
and provincial towns in England by the
Military Police, handcuffed! Their “crime”
is invariably A.W.L. (absent without leave),
and I, with many others, declare that such
a thing is an insult to our uniform and our
country, not to mention our men. Isn’t
there something in King’s Regulations about
putting a prisoner in a conveyance where
one is to be had, and there are one or two in
London, at any rate. Incidentally, why not cut
out the hideous word “crime” and substitute
“offence”? If a man is a few minutes late,
lie’s “crimed.” I’m not weeping for Billjim.
But I am anxious about the effect these silly
anachronisms, these senseless old military
customs, these t autocratic practices of the
military caste, are going to have on Aus-
tralia. You observe that, whereas universal
military service was once accepted by prac-
tically everybody in the Commonwealth, there
are now tens of thousands of conscientious
opponents of it even among the troops. I
have talked with hundreds of men in Egypt,
Palestine, England and France and have lis-
tened to the talk of thousands more, and I
declare that indignities which Billjim knows
to be totally unnecessary are almost wholly
responsible for the change of feeling.
* * * *
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : Pfahlert’s corner
pub, historic amongst the beer-pumps of
Sydney, is on the market. The site is a
part of the old Military Barracks Square,
cut up and sold in the first fifties of last
century. In the middle of the decade the
house was built and occupied as a German
club, the southern part of the ground being
a garden where the Teuton swilled his lager.
The secretary of the club was Simon Zollner,
who was in the galvanised-iron business. In
the first ’sixties Abraham Cohen, father of
the late Justice Cohen, took the property over,
opened it as a hotel and kept it for many
years; and one sign or another has been over
the door ever since. In these latter days
the shingle bears the name of a man who
probably never personally sold a pot in his
life —Andrew Black, the professional singer.
He married the landlady.

“Kharn” (from Palestine) : Don’t know
why, but I’m annoyed about this. Out of
S 9 awards for bravery handed out by the
Royal Humane Society of Australasia in
July last no less than 18 go to Sale in Vic-
toria, or 20 per cent. Further, I find
that 33 per cent, go to Gippsland.
And, finally, of the SU awards, no less
than 73 go to Victoria, the remain-
ing 16 being divided up between Banana-
land. X. S. Wales, Tassy. S.A. and West-
ralia. not forgetting Canberra and the
Northern Territory. Do the all-powerful
“directors” who make the awards live in
Sale or somewhere else in Gippsland? And
why not cut the “Australasia” out of the title
and make it the Royal Humane Society of
Victoria? Incidentally, if it were not for
the Censor I would tell you how some offi-
cers get decorations for scraps they only
hear about afterwards. I might even tell
you of the answer which the colonel of a
resting regiment gave to a communication
from up above asking him to nominate four
men for a French decoration.

WASTE IN WAR TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Silly Father Christmas! Sitting in my bed,
I can hear them talking. Know what mother said?
“Fancy that for Christmas! Oh, it’s a disgrace!
Such a waste in war-time! How you have the face !
Drink in every cupboard! Well, thank goodness, dear,
Christmas only happens once each blessed year!”
Doll for little sister, tricycle for me,
Diamond ring for mother —that's all right, you see!
Then old Father Christmas suddenly goes mad,
Sends a case of whisky to poor old thirsty dad!
N.S.W.

There are hundreds of teachers of both sexes employed in
the N.S.W. Educational Service to-day who suffer from weak
nerves or acute nervousness, through continuous nerve strain in
teaching and study, who never really enjoy good health. It is
as much the worry of keeping their schools up to the educa-
tional standard as the work which accounts for their feeling of
general ill-health. They should keep Clements Tonic on hand
as a “stand-by” medicine, feeling certain that in its possession
they will have a sweet soother of the nerves, a promoter of
keen appetite, and a general healer of the rundown and over-
wrought system. It makes weak people well and strong people
stronger. Get it to-day.
“Caboolture” : There is still in existence a
plan of Brisbane made in 1539 by
Major Burney, C.E.; this was three
years before the settlement was thrown
open to free persons. The prisoners’ bar-
racks were located about where Spencer’s
picture-show now stands. The “lumber yard,”
or prison workshop, was on the block
where West’s pictures are. The old
“Female Factory” was located on the site
of the present post office; but the female

convicts had been removed to Eagle Farm a
few years before, and the buildings w r ere used
as a residence by that fine man, Andrew
Petrie, Superintendent of Works for the
settlement, for a year or so after his arrival
from Sydney in 1837. A house was after-
wards built for him almost on the corner of
the present Wharf and Queen streets, whence
arose the name Petrie’s Bight. Creek-street
was a real creek in those days, and the pre-
sent horse-ferry lands just about the place
where there was a large fish-trap built across
the creek-mouth. The Petries’ garden ex-
tended from Creek-street to the river bank
at the rear of Thomas Brown and Co. s
warehouse, and the blacks used to swim
across from Kangaroo Point and loot the
sweet potatoes. An aged convict gardener
attended to them with a horse-pistol full
of pebbles whenever he got a chance. The
hospitals for officers, soldiers and convicts
were on the site of the Law Courts, and the
soldiers’ barracks stood where the Treasury
is now. One of the first cemeteries was just
opposite to the coal shoots at the Roma-street
’ station. The hills overlooking Roma-street
(now being cut away for the new goods
yards) were the favorite site for corroborees
and camping grounds. Another was at the
Exhibition Grounds. The scrubs about Too-
wong Railway Station and West End were
great hunting grounds for the aborigines.
The present Government Store buildings date
back to the convict days, except for a new
floor added recently. The Present Obser-
vatorv was the original windmill built tc
grind the maize meal on which the prisoners
were mostly fed. The first execution by
hanging in Brisbane took place here in the
’forties. The leading parts were played by two
aboriginals who had murdered the surveyors
Stapylton and Tuck.
The Union Jack has been flying over the
benches all right; but that kind of thing has
its limits, and the other day the High Court
simply couldn’t see how it could go as tar
as it was invited to. A Russian ship came
to Australia and wanted to get a clearance
for S. America, being under charter to carry
nitrates. The Commonwealth Government
snapped its fingers at charters and told the
Russian that he couldn’t get a clearance
unless he loaded wheat. So, while others
were making big money, he was hung up
here; and now he naturally wants com-
pensation. In effect the point for the High
Court was whether, Billy Hughes being the
Crowrn, Billy could do any wrong:—
The requirement of the Commonwealth was that
aliens should themselves engage in the service of
the Commonwealth as well as permit their property
to be used. To some extent, this appeared to be
recognised as permissible. But in the authorities
to which they had been referred there was not any
statement showing that the mere fact of war sup-
ported an attempt to compel aliens to personally
enter the King’s service outside the territory and
on the open sea, and while there to risk capture or
death at the hands of the enemy.
So the Court blasphemously decided that
Billy may do wrong, and that the Common-
wealth is liable to pay heavy damages for
unlawful acts that he may commit in its
name.
“Tweed Heads”: When my old friend
Bethstreet was finding his feet in the city,
years ago, he started to refurnish his
mother’s house, which had lost a good deal
of furniture in the storms. He was a proud
lad when he had finished buying a good Ger-
man piano on time-payment—a matter of
five years or so. Then, when he had put
the home people on their feet, he must needs
get married and buy a second good German
piano on time-payment. His wife used to
play all the British patriotic airs on it. I
remembered Bethstreet, the patriot, yester-
day when I read of the tremendous prepara-
tions Germany is making for the aerial war
in 191 S. Some 29 new airplane factories
have been started, and the Fokker people
have taken over the German works whence
came Bethstreet’s two musical boxes. It
seems that the plants of piano factories can
quickly be altered to make aircraft. Beth-
street is now at the Front waiting for some
flying Hun to bomb him. In his saner
moments he says he deserves it.
* * * *
Baird, Vic. Minister for Education, has
made an early start in his new job by
doing the right thing for his constituents,
besides helping the rising generation to re-
member him in its prayers. This haying
season the State schools are to close up for
six weeks instead of five —to reward Little
Willie and Annie for having raised nearly
a quarter-million patriotic sovs.: giving them
back, in short, some of their own time, so
much of which has been spent, before and
after school hours, in pursuing the adult popu-
lation with things to sell, or in making
things and growing things to help the sol-
diers’ funds. The results are all the more
notable because director Tate frowns
severely on raffles. The money lias nearly
all been raised by direct collections, enter-
tainments or the giving of value for value.
Now, while Minister Baird is feeling benign
towards Willie, he might perhaps be pre-
vailed on to go round a few of the schools
and look at the ventilation.

“D.S.” : If the Victorian train that wan-
ders up the hill from Tallangatta to Shelly
does not hold the Australasian record for
want of speed it should. The time-table
gives it three hours to get past 23 miles, and
it usually takes a little extra. But it has
the excuse that Shelly is the highest railway
station in Vic., 2560 ft. above sea-level, and
that in the 23 miles the line climbs nearly
2000 ft.

“Sacred visions,” frequently seen in the
night skies during the siege of Przemysl by
the Russians in 1915, had a considerable
effect in bucking up the defending Austrians
and disgruntling the Russian artillerymen.
The explanation really reflects credit on the
ingenuity of our friends the enemy. The
visions appeared only on cloudy nights, and
in those latitudes rain-clouds travel at an
altitude of about half a mile. By using a
THE GENTLE RETORT.
S OUBRETTE: “A woman would need the
patience of an angel to get along with
you."
MANAGER: "I know, my dear. I do try to
remember you haven't got it.”
searchlight with a special mirror and a
stereopticon, it is quite practicable to pro-
ject fixed pictures to a distance of 1000 feet,
with full illumination. A generator of suffi-
cient power, run off an aeroplane engine,
would weigh less than 10001 b., which is quite
an easy load for a large biplane. The Aus-
trians had aeroplanes fitted with the neces-
sary apparatus, and prepared slides from
the celebrated picture of the Madonna and
Child at the Czenostochowa monastery, which
was in their hands at the time. This picture
is said to have been painted by St. Luke, and
has a wide reputation in Eastern Europe.
The airmen were then sent to fly at from 700
to 1000 feet below the clouds, turning on the
apparatus as required. At 1000 feet the
image of an ordinary lantern slide would be
magnified to 185 feet in diameter. Some
Russian soldiers refused to fire for hours
after each release of this decidedly striking
feature, and a Russian artillery commander
who suspected the trick and ordered, a bat-
tery to blaze away at the “vision” had a
narrow escape of being scuppered by his
scared men.
* * * *
A letter written by Edmund Fisher to an
old Anglo-Australian friend who is doing
good war work in England seems to have
had influential circulation. Fisher’s corres-
pondent writes (23/10/T7) :
Every word of your letter is God’s truth. I
showed it to , who at once asked me to supply
him with a copy that he might place it before the
War Cabinet. I saw him hurriedly yesterday morn-
ing. He was just able to tell me that it had been
before the Cabinet and Lloyd George.
Meanwhile Edmund Fisher has only a vague
recollection of what he wrote. But The
Bulletin knows. Fifty-two times a year
Fisher wants The Bulletin to assert that
Britain’s war efforts are discounted,
thwarted, hampered at every turn by Ger-
man and Germanised interests in high politi-
cal, financial and social circles; that English
unsuspiciousness is a great national peril;
that the winning of the war from the air,
where alone it can be won for the Allies, is
being delayed almost beyond possibility of
salvation by the jealousy of the military and
naval bosses; that the contented drone of
unreasoning optimists is lulling the nation to
sleep whilst profiteers are plundering its
pockets. All this, and more, is what this
paper’s old-established Melbourne philoso-
pher is certain to have said in the letter
which was placed before Lloyd George and
the War Council, and was probably dis-
missed as being too unpleasant for words.
OYER THE FENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I always played with little girls,
With smooth pigtails or tidy curls,
lu lacey frills, and laundered frocks,
And shiny shoes, and faultless socks.
But while we learned the smiling code,
Wild laughter reached us from the road ;
And, oh, I longed with every sense
To join the rebels o’er the fence!
Grown up, I still am hemmed about
By friends impeccable who flout
No social law, who smoothly talk,
And use Life’s proper knife and fork.
But when I can I slyly creep
And at the outside revels peep.
Oh, someday will I have the sense
To climb across the silly fence?
x.s.w.

MILACHRIO
CIGARETTES
Agar Wynne is lending a hand with the
Edward Lascelles memorial, which is to take
the sensible form of a new textile college in
Victoria. But after the college—what then?
Jobs will have to be found for the experts
whom the new concern will turn loose, and
where is the adequate tariff to guarantee
them scope for their energies?
There have been nine Presidents of the
N. S. Wales Leg. Council. Three of these
aave either not been offered knight-
hood or declined it —Plunkett, Wentworth
and Flowers. The first four resigned
office —Stephen. Plunkett, Burton and
Wentworth. The next four died in
office. The ninth man has held the job some
months short of three years. There have
hitherto been seven Chairmen of Committees.
All are dead. The eighth, Barnabas O’Conor,
son of an Anglican parson, has been in office
54 years. Among Speakers, numbering 14,
Abbott had the Chair for close on 10 years.
McCourt for 10 years and five months. The
shortest term was Morton’s, less than four
months.
In a Melbourne suburb the other day two
Misses Long, sisters, were married to two
Briefs, brothers. Now we shan’t be Long.
Only one of the leading barristers
engaged in the protracted Tichborne trial
now survives, and he recently started his
93rd year. He is Lord Halsbury, ex-Lord
Chancellor. As Sir Hardinge Gifford he
pocketed some thousands of pounds for his
strenuous but unsuccessful efforts to make
the jury believe that the Wagga butcher
was the long missing English Baronet.
“Vic”: It is now beyond doubt that Mel-
bourne’s most prominent magistrate, S.
Goldsmith, P.M., was drowned off the little
bay steamer Planet during the ferry trip
from Williamstown. A better beak never
sat by the Yarra. He was well known for
years as police-court clerk; then he quali-
fied for city magistracy by service in vari-
ous minor places. With higher training he
would have been a first-rate judge.
Joe Carruthers’s elder brother J. E., Pre-
sident of the Australian Methody church,
will be 70 next month—his anniversary
comes with that of N. S. Wales. Ex-Pre-
mier Joe is the youngest of four brothers,
and he is 61. The eldest, Henry, was
accountant of N. S. Wales Railways: J.E.
came next; and the third has been mayor
and most other things at Kogarah (N.S.W.).
PETHEBRIDGE, formerly Secretary to the
Australian Defence Department, now
Administrator at Rabaul.
“T. 0.1.”: Though ex-Justice Mathew
Henry Stephen (B. 13/12/T7) was the first
Australian-trained lawyer admitted to our
Bar, he was not the first Australian ad-
mitted. That honor belongs to William
Charles Wentworth, who was called in Lon-
don in 1822 and admitted in Sydney in 1824
—the first barrister in practice in Australia.
Here’s a remarkable family history. Of
the children of that daughter of pioneer
parson Marsden who in 1822 married
Thomas Hassail, the first rector of Cob-
bitty (N.S.W.), no less than three are still
living. Thomas Hassail was born in Cov-
entry, England, and accompanied his mis-
sionary to Tahiti in the ship Duff.
He was one of a family of eight, six of
whom were born in Australia. When a lad
Tom went to England to study for the
Church. It was a 10-months’ vovage; he
returned fully ordained in 1821. 'with an
appointment as Colonial Chaplain and assist-
ant to Marsden. In 1522 he married Sam-
uel’s daughter. This lady, who died at 92.
may be considered an Australian, as she
was born just outside Sydney Heads on
board the ship which brought parson
Marsden and his bride to Port Jackson.
Their three children who still survive are
Mrs. Hope, of Melbourne. 90; Charles
Hassail. of Victoria. So; and Miss Eliza
Hassail (N.S.W.), 82.
After 57 years of peace George Williams
of Tallangatta (Vic.), thought that it was
up to him to enlist, because he reckoned that
his two boys wanted someone to look after
them. He persuaded the recruiting officials
that he was 44. and fought as well as plenty

leg sent him back to Australia.
• From the other end of the earth :
Rear-Admiral Sims, who commands Uncle Sam's
naval forces in Europe, met his old friend ex-
Admiral Percy Scott in England lately. The two
were comrades in China in the ’nineties. Both were
mad on gunnery and were almost insubordinate in
their efforts to improve the shooting of their fleets.
Sims conveyed his enthusiasm to Washington in an
unsolicited report criticising the conservatism of the
High Command, and was very nearly sacked. Scott
showed the same spirit a few years later when con-
trolling a squadron of a fleet commanded bv Lord
Charles Beresford. To the old Irishman Scott's
mania for efficiency was incompatible with the be-
haviah of an officah and a gentleman; so Beresford
was not so shocked when his subordinate translated
an order to “Paint Ship” into “Make Yourselves
Look Pretty,” and flung- out the jeer from his own
signal halliards. The Morris tube that revolution-
ised target work was invented by Scott in China
on the alleged initiative of Sims, who had always
regretted that effective practice was barred by the
cost of ammunition. Sims introduced the Morris
tube to the U.S. Navy and caused a momentous in-
crease in the record of hits. Sims is also responsible
for the lattice masts in Yankee Dreadnoughts, which
are alleged to be superior to the British tripod
variety, and for other innovations—including the
fracture of the hallowed Yankee system of promotion
by seniority. Like General Pershing, who beat
over 800 seniors in his jump for promotion, he is
the junior in service to several officers who are his
inferiors in rank. He is a Canadian, by the way,
born in Ontario in 1858.
Parson Blacket, the qid chronicler of S.
Aus., is struggling to get Kangaroo Island
given its full historical value. The island
was, like Maoriland, inhabited by sealers
before any Government came along. One
of them had such strength of character (or
of body) that he got the name of “Governor”
Whalley, and Blacket wants his hut identi-
fied and labelled.
Mazzini Tron, chaplain, who won the M.C.
in Flanders lately, is an Anglican priest
who before the war belonged to the Brother-
hood of St. Boniface in the diocese of Bun-
bury (W.A.). Bigjohn Forrest is generally
regarded as the patron saint of Bunbury;
but when the place became an Anglican see
in 1902, Dr. Goldsmith, who wore the first
gaiters, discovered that the English Bunbury,
after which the W.A. seaport is named, is
really Boniface-bury, or Boniface’s town,
and the local Anglicans adopted St. Boni-
face as their patron, and have kept his
“day”—June s—as5 —as their festival ever since.
Boniface, by the way, was an English monk
who died about 1200 years ago. He is
reckoned “the apostle of Germany” and the
father of German Christianity; but this
paper doesn't know what he thinks of his
offspring.
“Trooper Bluegum” ; Another good Aus-
tralian gone—Lieut. H. E. Bowd, white man.
He left Sydney with the Light Horse, and
last year transferred to the Flying Corps.
For several months he scouted over Abdul’s
lines. On his last day he was up 4000 feet,
when something went wrong, and the ’bus,
in flames, dived to earth. A couple of hun-
dred feet from the ground Bowd jumped or
was thrown out, and was probably suffocated
before he landed. His pal. Lieut. C. E.
Stones, who left the Light Horse with him,
went into the Valley some little time ago.
Captain Harry Storrer, who has been
killed in France, gave up a motor-expert’s
job at Dennys Lascelles’ when he enlisted.
He left Australia with the A.F.C., and as
a qualified flier had gained some local pres-
tige before he embarked for the Front. A
brother, Murray, went West during the Gal-
lipoli campaign.
Of Portuguese extraction, Tom Bak Hap
obtained his Oriental moniker and a know-
ledge of Chinese from his stepfather. Before
being elected as member for Bass in 1909 he
made a crust as a mining and commission
agent, whilst an occasional trip to the “Far
East” would generally return its share of
oats. He got the electors to transfer him to
a job as Senator in 1913, and when war
started 12
months later he
went all in for
O o n s c ription,
and hasn’t
stopped advocat-
ing national ser-
vice since. With
Harry Keating
he was respon-
sible for forcing
the double dis-
solution last
March. The
Mining •Stan-
dard said they
did it at the bid-
ding of their
Church, and the
two Senators
split £9OO as a
result of a libel
action based
thereon and
lately finished
at Hobart.

The first professional schoolmaster in
Australasia was Thomas Bowden, recom-
mended by William Wilberforce, and
appointed master of the Orphan School in
1814 at a salary of £25 per year with quar-
ters, and, presumably, rations. Some
descendants of his are in the law in N. S.
Wales to-day.

A NATURAL SOLICITUDE.
TOM: “ You take a great interest in the
war?”
GIRLIE: “ I should think so! Five of my
fiancees are fighting.”
‘*Y\ aiwera’s” yarns of a crimson character:
Nicholas Minister (B. 6/12/’l7) was, and is, best
known in the Straits as Nicholas the Greek, and the
tales about him and his doings aie innumerable.
Once a warship made a call in the Trobriands for
the special purpose of interviewing Nicholas with
reference to one of his little difficulties with the
natives. Navigating cautiously amongst the un-
charted reefs, the vessel came on a European fishing
in a canoe, and asked the way to the abode of that
turbulent descendant of Leonidas whom they sought.
The European very obligingly acted as pilot, and after
a hair-raising passage through a series of hairpin
channels, brought them without mishap to the de-
sired address. Nicholas was found to be out, and
after acting as interpreter to ascertain this fact
from the local natives, the obliging stranger took
them safely out again and promised to do what
he could to secure Nicholas’s attendance on some
future occasion. It was not till quite a long while
afterwards that the captain of the war vessel learned
that the obliging stranger who had performed the
miracle of navigation was Nicholas himself. What
could a navigator do to a pastmaster like that?
One thing H.M. Navy knew' it couldn't do, and that
was follow him.
[For any paragraphs that require a signature and
are not signed by anybody else, S. H. Prior. John
Barr and E. O’Ferrall, of 214 George-street, Syd-
ney, take the responsibility.]
SISTER ANN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m lyin’ in a narrow bed,
’X' starin’ at a wall.
Where all is white my plastered head
Is whitest of it all.
My life is jist a whitewashed blank,
With flamin' spurts of pain.
I duiino who I’ve got to thank,
I’ve p’raps been trod on by a tank,
Or caught out in the rain
When skies were peltin’ fish-plates, bricks ’n'
lengths of bullock-chain.
I’m lyin’ here, a sulky swine,
’N’ hatin’ of the bloke
Who’s in the doss right next to mine
With ’arf his girders broke.
He never done no ’arm to me,
’X’ he's pertickler ill;
But I have got him snouted, see,
’X’ all old earth beside; but she
Comes with the chemist’s swill,
’N’ puts a kind, soft ’and on mine, ’n’ all my
nark is still.
She ain't a beaut, she’s thirty-two,
She scales eleven stone;
But, 'struth, I didn’t think it true
There was such women grown!
She's nurse ’n’ sister, mum. ’n’ dad,
’X’ all that's straight ’n’ fine
In every girl I ever had.
When Gabr’el comes, ’n’ all the glad,
Young saints are tipped the sign,
You’ll see this donah take her place, first angel
in the line!
I dunno if she ever sleeps
Ten minutes at a stretch.
A dozen times a night she creeps
To soothe a screamin’ wretch
Who has a tiger-headed Hun
A-gnawin’ at his chest.
’X’ when the long, ’ard fight is won,
’X’ he is still ’n’ nearly done,
She smiles down on his rest,
’X’ minds me of a mother with a baby at her
breast—
The curly kid we cuddled when
There was no splendid row:
It seemed a little matter then,
But feels so wondrous now.
It’s part of her. She’s Jenny Dark,
Flo Xightingale, all fair
’X’ dinkum women since the ark.
If she comes in without a “spark”
We blighters feel her there.
The whole pack perks up like a bird, ’n’ sorter
takes the air.
She chat sv on in a ’lghland botch ;
But if our Sis saw fit
To pitch Hindoo instead of Scotch
I'd get the hang of it,
Because her heart it is that talks
What now is plain to me.
At war where bloody murder stalks,
’X’ Hell its foulest samples hawks,
I have been given to see
What simple human kindness is, what brother-
hood may be.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.
THE WAR-MOTHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
bene er I think of you, my fighting son,
You draw quite near to me.
ou speak, you more, as you have always done,
Through my quick memory.
You are myself—bone of my bones; all me.
Sometimes I seem to leap
Red o’er your parapets of misery,
While you lie here asleep.
I ha\e known pain and patience both for vou.
Rough torments I have slain—
Moulded you, manned you. made you bold and true,
yielded you heart and brain.
Tou were my babe, my all, my fighting son,
Taking full life from me.
Still, still you draw it, and our two lives run
In perfect unity.
I fight with you. I charge the hot, dark hill.
I meet your pangs, your woes.
And I shall hold you, strong of heart and will,
Till our full triumph grows.
If you fall, I must fall—but not to death.
Oh, to eternity
Ton still are mine beyond the sobbing breath
The last pain draws from me.
K.S.TT.

Notwithstanding that there is an unfriendly
person at large whose present business is to
make things uncomfortable for British insur-
ance offices, the Colonial Mutual, with a little
help from Jellicoe, has managed to look after
itself very well; and the loss rate is actually
less than usual. To some extent this is no
doubt due to the fact that property in Aus-
tralia is much too valuable to destroy just
at present. There is a thing known to under-
writers as the moral hazard, and it costs
them hundreds of thousands a year in times
when merchandise stock is hard to get rid of
and house and shop properties are difficult
to let. Ever since we have been scattering
borrowed war millions —scattering them at
a rate hitherto undreamt of —and ever since
the U-boats have given Australian industries
a Protection that no politician ever gave
them, the underwriter has been faii'ly easy
in his mind about his old enemy Moral
Hazard. Later on—but we simply don’t
know what is going to happen later on. If
somebody would tell us what price we shall
get for our wheat, wool, copper, lead and
other staples, and what we shall have to pay
for freights, we might make a guess at it.
The most hopeful feature, perhaps, is that
Britain is doing what we are scarcely
attempting to do, and paying so large a pro-
portion of her war bill out of current taxa-
tion. Actually one-seventh of Britain’s enor-
mous war expenditure has been thus far met
out of special war-time taxation. Leaving
out of the reckoning the loans made to the
Allies, a recent compilation, issued by
authority, shows that Britain is raising £1
by taxation for every £2 11s. 2d. spent on all
accounts. This, while it ought to make
Hughes and his Ministers blush, if they
have a blush about them, will make things
vastly easier for Britain after the war; and
therein lies our best hope that our markets
will not go to pieces.
The co. had a long struggle to get firmly
on its feet; and when it had got there it had
the good fortune to find that thousands of
people were beginning to think that if it was
good to encourage home industries it was
also good to encourage home insurances.
The results have been gilt-edged for this co.
Yet it can’t be said that the public has had
to pay any more for the local goods. If the
Colonial Mutual has made a lot of money it
can reply that it made it. by accepting the
rates fixed by an association in which the
foreign cos. are still predominant. Whether
this and other private offices will continue
to have quite as good a time as they have had
hitherto is a matter which the investor will
of course consider. During the last few
years, for instance, accident and workmen’s
compensation business has brought in good
revenue to Australian cos. One by one,
however, the State Governments have cut in.
The Queensland Government has not only
taken a monopoly of workmen’s insurance
in that State, but has started to take other
lines; and with the organisation which a
in one line furnishes, it must be a
powerful competitor. Victoria, though it
hasn’t taken a monopoly, has a workmen’s
compensation office, whose purpose is to give
cost-price insurance; and that sort of com-
petition is do good to a co. which is out to
make dividends. N. S. Wales has no Govern-
ment office; but the fear of it produced a
severe overhaul of rates in that State. Maori-
land has gone even further : it has a vigorous
fire office, as well as a workmen's compensa-
tion office : and though the fire office makes no
great progress, it unquestionably keeps others
in the field from getting fat. These and
various co-operative movements appear to be
reflected in this co.’s net premium income;
it will be seen that the total for the past
year was less than for any year since 1911:

The feature of that last line which hits you
hardest in the eye is the increase in the ex-
pense rate: general exes. —that is, salaries,
charges, stationery and advertising, exclusive
of brokerage and commission—ran last year
to £43,512, as against £40,111 for a larger
premium income in 1914; and taxation and
contributions to fire brigades are £3OOO more.
This is one of the other sort of consequences
of the war.
Owing to the rapid capitalisation of re-
serves, the 10 p.c. dividend takes a pretty
big bite out of the underwriting profit. In-
terest earnings provide only £19,252 of the
£30,000 dividend. Interest earnings, by the
way, are £2240 better than in the previous
year, which partly wipes out the higher exes.
The assets are nowadays a particularly
liquid lot: mortgages are only £12,000 and
freeholds £28,068. Add to present total funds
£135,000 to represent earnings for the cur-
rent year in excess of working exes., and the
total will give you an amount six times
greater than the average annual fire losses of
the past five years. When an Australian co. can
during any year meet losses six times greater'
than its average, and more than five times
greater than its worst experience, and can
do it without calling in a single mortgage,
there is no need to go exploring among omces
which send their profits away from Australia.

This little bank serves the north and west
of Tasmania, where it has its head-office and
15 branches; and, though its opportunities
have been restricted, it has managed to more
than double its reserves in 10 years while
paying a 7 p.c. dividend. Its balance-sheet
figures have jogged along in this unemotional
way
1907. 1914. 1917.

The comparatively big decline in “other
liabilities” is due to the retirement of bank-
notes on the arrival of Commonwealth paper.
Apart from the higher prices it hds got for
its wool, its copper and its tin, Tasmania
hasn’t benefited to any great extent from the
expenditure of war money ; nevertheless this
bank holds now £739,385 of the public’s
money as against £645.212 two or three
months after the war started. Of this debt
it has a reasonable 36 p.c. liquid (including
Commonwealth war loan) ; and the book-
value of the non-liquid assets is equal to 83s.
for every 20s. of the liabilities not repre-
sented by casli, etc. With less than £3 of
the public’s money in the business for every
£1 of its own, the bank can’t expect to make
big profits. On the other hand, the compara-
tively small proportion of the public’s money
P the business reduces the risks for ail
hands, shareholders and depositors alike.

This exceedingly affluent co. had a habit’of
making provision for risks that faded away
with the result that a few years ago. when a
solid million was added to the capital the
visible reserves were drawn upon for only
about a quarter of the amount. Latterly a
lot of revenue has been dropped into the
Insurance Fund ; and this has been reckoned
as profit in the foregoing table. Separating
these amounts from the net surplus as dis-
closed by F. and L. account, we get these
results: —
Net Profits. Added to Insurance Fund.
1915. . £136,383 £46,467
1916.. 150,174 158,696
1917.. 157,403 13,134
Possibly the co. carries some of its own in-
surance risks ; and it may be that the losses
have been exceptionally serious during the
past year. Theoretically, the proper course
would be to reserve the extra freight earn-
ings through war as a provision for (1) war
taxation and (2) war risks carried by the
co.; and maybe this is being done. In that
case the net earnings shown in the last little
table may be called the normal earnings.
There is, however, another semi-reserve
which isn’t included among the reserves in
either table. This was established in 1915
as a “reserve for liabilities not yet adjusted,
including provision for allowances on adjust-
ment of contracts, Imperial, Commonwealth
and Dominion taxes, and extra depreciation
on steamers in Government service.” In the
first year (1915) £209,227 was dropped into
this quasi-reserve, in the following year it
grew to £359,227, and now it is up to
£487,478. It is no doubt possible that these
contingent liabilities are steadily getting
bigger; but, generally speaking, when a
contingency fund keeps on growing, one
has a suspicion that the co. is having
better luck than it expected. Which
means that sooner or later more or
less is transferred to profits as being
no longer required to cover contingencies.
At any rate, one can say this: that, while
admitting a net profit of £157,403 for the
past year, this co., besides providing in the
ordinary way for depreciation, has set aside
about £140,000 which may or may not be
called for.
Comparing balance-sheet figures since the
co. was reconstructed, these results show up :

So in three years the liabilities (apart from

have increased by £306,559, while liquid

though the fleet has been written down,

than it was three years ago. This paper
doesn’t suggest that the co.’s profits in excess
of the dividend have amounted to between
f 750 odd dnrl £BOO,OOO in three years ; but it
is a fact that the co. has put that much by
to provide for contingencies. In the circum-
stances, The Bulletin doesn’t see any
strong reason for sending managing-director
Iloldsworth a cable of condolence.

This was one of the humorous flotations of
the last company-promoting boom. The
goodwill, referred to above, was in respect
of two businesses carried on in Pitt-street.
One was in a leasehold which had only a few
months to run ; and as that business was un-
profitable no serious effort was made to renew
the lease. Business was then confined to the
other premises; and in a year or two word
went round that the lease there was just
about up, too. As a matter of fact, it expired
in September of this year, and the co. walked
out. Thus the businesses for whose goodwill
the co. had paid about half its capital evap-
orated. Meantime, however, the co. had got
hold of one or two little businesses run by
Miss Bishop: and Miss Bishop’s name being
evidently regarded as better than the one for
which the co. had paid so much, it is under
that sign that it now trades at a dozen
places in city and suburbs. Further:—
Regarding the writing down of the assets and
plant, and the general reconstruction foreshadowed
in last report, the directors have decided to submit
the necessary resolutions at a later meeting.
Presumably the old name will go. It was a
poor, inexpressive thing, anyhow, and w’hen
you look hungry and talk in Sydney aW
gcng to the A.8.C., most peopl/ utfS
stand that you are off to the corner ?f
George and King streets for a hank over-
draft. M ith the right management there is
a lot of money in this tea-and-scone business
Lp to now the management has no doubt
been handicapped by the businesses and leases
which it paid a lot of money for. but which
it probably should have been paid a bit m
take over. It has still, however, to nm
itself. Until then the investor will beslu
though to the speculator it will appeal as
something which may quite likely make an
excellent recovery.

This co., which paid a dividend at the rate
of 9 p.c. p.a. for the first half of its last year
pays at the rate of 12 p.c. p.a. for the second
half; and the distribution is on capital which
has been mostly transferred from reserves
The results are among the fruits of the bor-
rowing boom of the last seven or eight years
In 1910 the capital was £51,900, and the
reserves at the time were £64,514. Since
then £90,616 of profit has been capitalised
£292 has been paid in cash bonuses in lieu
of shares, and, after payment of a dividend
which is equal so far as the past year is con-
cerned _ to over 30 p.c. on the capital as it
stood in 1910, the reserves are £51,258 in
excess of what they were at that date. In
other words, besides paying the dividend, a
surplus profit has been made in seven years
of £142,166, equal to 2f times the capital as
it stood seven years ago. And there is no
doubt that if it wanted to the co. could show
more astonishing figures even than that, be-
cause the freeholds in hand have apparently
been written down severely. If you want to
know who benefits by a borrowing boom you
have only to ponder over these figures; for
a large part of this co.’s profits have been
made by buying estates, and subdividing them
for subsequent sale on time-payment.
At the present time, of its total assets of
£320,018, “borrowers’ and purchasers’ bal-
ances after deducting suspense accounts” re-
present £249,152, and the directors give share-
holders the very reassuring information that
over 80 p.c. of this is secured on rent-pro-
ducing properties.” The small balance repre-
sents balances on sales of land, and “ample
provision has been made for possible losses.”
The other assets are £27,677 liquid, £38,911
m freeholds (which presumably include the
co s valuable city premises, largely rent-
producing). and £4276 sundries. Including
provision for taxation the liabilities total
±36,107.
While circumstances have so richly favored
the co., the. management has been excellent;
and while it retains that asset the co. will
make money even in bad times. For the
immediate future it has its advances to rely
upon ; , and if there won’t be much in land-
subdivision for some years to come, there will
be good interest to be earned on high-class
securities. The co. needs to earn only 5 p.c.
net on its own funds, to pay its shareholders
8 p.c. on present capital. Losses it may pos-
sibly make; but its inner reserve will then
come in handy, and, unless the management
plunges, which it isn’t likely to do, the dead
weight of unproductive vacant land—which
has been so heavy a burden on many “in-
vestment cos. at the end of a boom —will
be almost negligible.

tit, B hun^tb 5 a . sub , staDt ial credit balance at
hnhW, k .b i ? irecto . rs thinb that the share-
holders should receive some of the profits
made m past years, but which were withheld
on account of the then financial obligations
o the co. 1 hey therefore recommend that
a bonus of o p.c amounting to £6500, should
be declaied. Well, Tiie Bulletin doesn’t
see any good reason why it shouldn’t be
clone, it is a bit rough, however, on former
shareholders who had to sell; to those who
did sell though they weren’t obliged to the
directors can at least say that, year after
jeai, they were at pains to point out that the
profit had actually been made. The policy
or trading so far beyond the capital of a co.
that no dividend at all can be paid for years,
all profits being required to reduce the over-
draft, is, nevertheless, open to grave ques-
tion.. The extent to which this co. launched
out is shown by the following statement of
its liabilities at various dates:—
Mar., 1912. . £46,796 Mar., 1915. . £103,787
Sept., 1912.. 72,276 Sept., 1915.. 65,188
Mar., 1913.. 55,836 Mar., 1916.. 53,482
Sept., 1913.. 70,958 Sept., 1916.. 29,953
Mar., 1914.. 83,444 Mar., 1917.. 15,962
Sept., 1914.. 96,918 Sept., 1917.. 21,284
The capital is £130,000: and, nominally,
reserves of about £29,000 were accumulated
in a recent 24 dry years. Nominally, because
the reserves include a depreciation fund.
The total of this is now £54,753 ; and as the
appropriation to it for the past half-year was
£2964, the P. and L. account claims a net
profit of only £6902 for the term. But this
reserve for depreciation is so very liberal
that it has always seemed doubtful to this
paper whether it should be struck out of the
reserves, as a depreciation reserve generally
should be. For these reasons; (1) repairs
and renewals are regularly charged up to
profit and loss; (2) apparently assets are
also written down occasionally—at any rate,
buildings, office furniture, plant and
machinery were in the balance-sheet at
£70,415 as at March, 1916, and 12 months
later the item had shrunk to £68,879, though
of course that may have been due to sales or
losses by fire covered by insurance; (3)
plant, etc., which has been kept in repair
and renewed, and which stands now at
£69,411. scarcely calls for a depreciation re-
serve of £54,753. Deducting this reserve,
the net assets are worth just upon 28s. per
share; including this reserve, 365. Stock
(£92,222) and advances on logs, etc.
(£35,808) together represent £128,030. which
looks a lot of money to have in building
material these times; but when you consider
that the sales last half-year were £131.100,
and for the past four half-years have aver-
aged £142,000, the figure seems a good deal
less incautious. Further, if building opera-
tions, etc., have slackened, the freight pro-
blem is so serious that timber prices seem in
no danger of an early slump—to say the least
of it.

This poor old cripple is still engaged in the
weary job of getting out of the trouble it
fell into years and years ago, when it bought
unproductive properties at inflated values
and lent borrowed money on. the same basis.
The last payment of principal to the old
depositors was made a year ago, and the pay-
ment of accrued interest will be due in Feb-
ruary. The dead-horse has been worked off
in this way :
Deposits, Property,

Interest, rent and other income was £3859
last year, which is equal to an almost exact
5 p.c. on the assets. But as against
that there was a trifle of interest and
more than a trifle of land-tax and lates.
these two items, along with insurance and
repairs, bit out about £lBOO, leaving less
than £2OOO. After paying management exes.,
including income-tax. the balance was profit,
and it wouldn’t make anyone fat. During
the past three years the property account
has been reduced by £4749. Apparently
there has been no loss on the sales; but it,
on the other hand, there has been a profit. it
lias been put to an inner reserve —probably
used to write down the book-value of the
remnant. The non-liquid assets now consist
of —Property account £19,808. advances
£29,411. and land sales account £10.923; and
there is no good reason why the last two
shouldn’t be worth somewhere near their
book-value. The live capital is now £42,725
in 10s. shares, and £4582 is paid on forfeited
shares. The net assets (book-value) are
thus equal to about 15s. for each live share.
But the realisation has been very slow, and
it isn’t likely that circumstances will be as
favorable for some years to come. Prospects
being what they are, it would probably pay
the shareholders best to look for a buyer* of
the whole outfit in a lump.

The Farmers’ Fertilizers Corporation
(Sydney), which started in May, 1911, with
deficit of £7064, represented by goodwill

Pref. capital is £3976 and ordinary capital
£18,937; but of the ordinaries £5OOO was
issued to A. 11. Ilasell for the goodwill of
his fertiliser business, and for the past
couple of years the vendor has handed the
dividend back to the co. to be applied to
the reduction of preliminary exes. The
refund is shown in the table as additional
profit. It is a very decent thing for the
vendor to do. Certainly he gets a bit other-
wise. Originally he had a contract with the
co. to act as selling agent at a remuneration
of 7s. 6d. per ton of super, and other ferti-
lisers sold; but this has been varied, and
he now receives p.c. on the gross sales
of these goods, which is apparently a gain
to the co. of about p.c. The co. relied
almost entirely upon foreign raw materials;
and the war has made it exceedingly diffi-
cult to procure them. Consequently the
turnover has been very much restricted. The
following shows the sales :
1913 .. £47,684 1916 .. £30,102
1914 . . 38,107 1917 . . 19,547
1915 . . 44,387
Expenses have, however, been cut down;
and that the co. has managed to pay a divi 1
dend in the circumstances is a creditable
performance. In consequence of the
reduced turnover the co. has £9692 of its
modest capital in cash, waiting for the clouds
to roll by. On the other side, certainly, it
has a mortgage of £4500. This was taken
against factory buildings, etc., which stand
at £6561.
The authorised capital of the co. is
£150,000, and some day it may be all wanted.
It ought to be if the co. is run upon a fair
co-operative basis, the dividend on the shares
being limited to, say, 7 or 8 p.c. The
farmers of N. S. Wales might save them-
selves tens of thousands of pounds a year if
they bought and sold co-operatively; and
there is a lot they might also do in the way
of co-operative manufacturing. It isn’t only
that they now provide profits for unneces-
sary middlemen: they support hundreds of
unnecessary travellers, and they. also pay
thousands of pounds more in freights than
they need if their supplies were sent over
the railways in bulk lots. This isn’t the
ideal co-operative co. There are vendor’s
shares, for one thing. But it was worth
paying something to get an organisation, and
there is no visible reason why this shouldn’t
be the nucleus of a concern of great value
to the primary producer; and upon his pros-
perity a great deal is going to depend during
the coming years. If we can make his lot
brighter and increase the number of pro-
ducers and the volume of produce we shall
find it a lot easier to pay our debts.

The mostly Sydney-owned Larut, one of
the most successful of the Malay dredging
cos., is putting some of its surplus cash
into two other concerns, the Kota and the
Tupai. For the property to be acquired by
the Kota, which is to have a capital of
£50,000, with £25,000 unissued and in re-
serve, the co. pays the vendor, Senator
Pratten, £25,000. Says the prospectus;—
The price fixed by the owner and vendor, Senator
Pratten, is £25,000, payable as to £5OOO in cash
and as to £20,000 in tully-paid shares. The cash
consideration represents approximately the outlay of
the vendor in acquiring and prospecting the property
and for reports obtained.
It is separated from the Larut only by a
road ; and somebody ought to explain why,
if Larut w*anted the ground, that co. didn’t
secure it at first hand instead of allowing
anyone to make a profit of £20,000 on the
deal. The area is about 226 acres. Appar-
ently it was previously bored for two Aus-
tralian interests —one of them the Austral
Malay—and turned down; but that, of
course, may have been because of the price
asked. Anyhow, the values seem to be very
erratic and, on the. whole, low. Under
Malcolm Newman’s direction 72 more bores
were put down, Assuming a good many
things, he reckons that there may be a profit
of from £25,000 to £40,000 for the first four
years and £14,000 to £20.000 for the next
54 years—the smaller figures on the basis
of tin (London) at £155; the higher on tin
(London) at £2OO. With a continuance of
the luck that those Malay cos. which have
got on to suitable ground have had, there
may be big profits in the block. But (1)
the ground has been very indifferently
tested; and (2) it will probably be two
years before a dredge can be on the ground,
and it is hard to say what tin will be worth
two years hence.
The Tupai is also to have a capital of
£50,000, with £25,000 in reserve; and in
this case also the owner and vendor is
Senator I’ratten, who takes £5OOO in cash
and £20,000 in shares, the former represent-
ing approximately the outlay of the vendor,
who is the chairman of the Larut Co., which
is the purchaser. Of this ground, which
is also in the neighborhood of the Larut,
Malcolm Newman says
The whole area has been repeatedly bored by
different cos., in particular by the Austral Malay
Co. In August, 1913, Mr. T. W. Horton put down 74
bores giving an average depth of 19ft. and an aver-
age value of .671 b. per cub. yard. About the middle
of 1915 the Austral Malay Co. rebored the area, and,
I believe, put down several hundred bores, but
did not complete its options. I have now put down
165 bores over an area of about 350 acres, the aver-
age value of the whole of these being .651 b. over an
average depth of 25ft. I have not bored the remain-
der of the field, which appears to be very shallow.
It seems, therefore, that Newman’s borings
agree with Horton’s. But Newman insists
that the property can be divided into two
parts, the payable, giving an average for
deep ground of .81b., and the unpayable,
shallow, worth less than He gives 80
acres containing over 44 million yards worth
lib. per yard, and 120 acres containing 5J
million yards worth §lb., and estimates pro-
fits, with tin at from £155 to £2OO London,
at £25,000 to £40,000 p.a. for the first four
years and £14,000 to £20,000 for the next
five.
The new cos. thus back Newman. If he
is right, and the market stands up to £155
for the next eight or 10 years, Larut will
have made two £mod deals, though its chair-
man does get £lO,OOO in cash and nearly
half the shares. Certainly, however, if the
cos. had been floated in the ordinary way,
the public, would have wanted an opinion
from a third party; for obviously there is
a direct conflict between two good “stables”
—one represented by Pratten and Newman;
the other by the Freemans and Horton. And
the public will have to remember this when
the scrip starts to move around.
Austral Malay Tin, Ltd., which has been
steadily turning down Malayan options, has
at last found something in which it thinks
it can smell dividends:—
The area is situated in Selangor, near Kuala Ivubu,
and is known as Ulu Kem. An area of 514 acres
particularly well suited for dredging has been proved
by 2'15 bores to average ,761 b. of tin oxide per cubic
yard, with an average depth of 22ft., and when all
titles have been put in order it is likely that this
area will be increased.
This 514 acres contains a total of 19,900,000
cubic yards, and the property, if equipped with a
dredge of similar c-apacitj' to those of the Kampong
Kamunting, should, with tin at £l7O per ton in
London, yield a divisible profit of £288,000, recover-
able in say 20 years, thus representing a return of
nearly 24 p.c. per annum upon a capital of £60,000.
To overcome the difficulty of getting a dredge
on to the property while the tin market is
still booming the Austral Malay is now woo-
ing Trong, which has a dredge and no suit-
able ground to use it in.
Some of the factors in the extraordinary
rise in the price of silver are disclosed by
the news that comes down slowly from Asia.
There have been half a dozen principal
causes: (1) An enormously increased con-
sumption in India, which is the world’s
largest consumer of the metal. The Hindoo
has never been persuaded to use paper-
money. He wants the metal. He can’t
always get it; but at pi’esent, when Europe
must have some of the things he produces
—jute, for instance —he can insist. He
doesn’t merely use it as circulation. He
hoards it; and a great deal, put into orna-
ments, is buried with him. Hence the con-
tinual demand for silver. In the 18 months
to the end of August India had absorbed
nearly 50,000.000 rupees (a rupee is worth
about Is. 4d.). (2) China has all along
been more or less preparing for war, and has
been accumulating silver for the purpose.
(3) Japan is having a prodigious trade
boom, which has meant a very big increase
in the demand for silver circulation.
(4) The U.S.A., though paying its troops
under trainihg in notes, is preparing to pay
them abroad very largely in silver, just as
Britain is doing. (5) The increase in
prices almost the world over, accompanied
by a general rise in wages, has called for a
world-wide increase in silver circulation.
(6) Meantime Mexico, which until five
years ago was one of the world’s largest
silver-producers—the second largest—is pro-
ducing very little, owing to domestic dis-
order. In all these circumstances an increase
in the price was inevitable ; and the mainten-
ance of high prices is almost certain for a
considerable time to come.
Mt. Morgan is extending its mill work.
In four weeks to December 9 it produced
8370 tons concentrates from 18,512 tons
crudes. The previous millings were 16,261
(Nov.), 14,032 (Oct.), 13,566 tons (Sept.).
During the latest run concentrates contained
441 tons copper and 36930 z. gold, averaging
5.2 p.c. copper, B.Bdwt. gold. With a smelter
so handy it doesn’t aim at a high-grade con-
centrate, but is satisfied if a large propor-
tion of nearly barren silica can be got rid
of in order to reduce the staggering smelting
costs.
Chairman Wharton talked to Hampden
shareholders about the resumption of divi-
dends. The mines worked through the sea-
men’s and waterside strikes ; but that meant
a large accumulation of stocks of blister, and
through shortage of coastal shipping the
copner is still there. However, the co. has
been pulling strings, and “it is possible we
may get forward the present accumulation
at Townsville during the next month or six
weeks, which means that we will be able to
refine same and deliver the refined copper to
the Imperial authorities and resume the pay-
ment of divs.” Wharton, by the way,
reckoned the increase in costs at from 75 p.c.
to 80 p.c.
Great Fingall (W.A.) is stated to be on
its last legs. Gangs, mostly Italians, are
now employed in the dangerous work of
taking out pillars and groping about old
stopes, and some big money is being earned.
(“Wild Cat ” continued on page SJf.)
