Sydney, December 24, 1917.
My Dear Arini. —
Admiralty House was alive with plumes
the other afternoon. Anything that can be
called a party in the pre-war sense is so
rare that the Investiture seemed a dazzling
event. Launches deposited those who also
climbed at the foot of the steep, terraced
gardens, and plain citizens panted up to
seize the vice-regal hand. This done, a
large and early-door audience, assembled on
the lawn, waited for the prisoner’s appear-
ance. Haskett Smith. A.D.C., turned up
first. Proceshing solemuly through the roped
enclosure, carrying a velvet cushion on
which glittered the K.C.31.G., he held the
sacred Thing till James Burns, guarded by
Dr. 3lackellar and William 3lc3lillan,
stepped forward to the garden throne. Here
he found his friend the G.-G., Lady Helen,
the Deputy-Gov. Cullens, and a retinue —
who so smart as General Finn in the long
blue service coat (and etceteras), now rarely
seen out of mothballs? After being severely
talked to in medieval language the new
knight rose up Sir James after a slap on the
shoulder with his Ex.’s sword. Dr. Evan
Jones, in an undertakery black suit, got the
Antarctic medal as the next item. And a
sad old father, the Rev. Howell Price, took
the military decoration awarded as a post-
humous honor to his brave son, the lieut.-col.
who was killed in France.
Then Society struggled out of its veils
and fell upon the refreshments. 3lodest
sailor Glossop helped to carve the cream-puff.
Clifford, A.D.C., loomed like a lighthouse
with a smile. Captain Dunn, R.A.N.. and
his blue-eyed lady, steered a course for a
shady patch, and near by the purple robes
of Archbishop Cattaneo floated in the stiff
nor’-easter. Archbish. Wright was in sombre
black, and the new young man at St. James's,
the ascetic 3licklem, was a clerical raven
alongside the lace frillies of 3loderator
Paterson. Captain d’Apice brought his
bride. 3lrs. Isaacs, the Judge’s wife who
has a Presence; 3lrs. Harry Finn and
daughter Kathleen in nut-brown filmy
drapery ; Colonel Lamrock and his wife';
the Misses Gother 3fann (early naval
history is their family forte) ; Consuls
galore; Alex. Hay and his pretty missus;
the King Salters from Cockatoo—in short,
all the tdfi-hats we haven’t seen for years
got an airing. An Investiture is a very
solemn thing, let me tell you.
Kelso King, who is crowned with a top-hat
oftener than anybody else in Sydney, ate his
way through a dinner given by the directors
and senior officers of the 3lercantile Mutual
Insurance Co. last week. The dapper insur-
ance sovereign, father of that Olive Kelso
King whose little picture I sent you the
other day, had been 40 years manager of the
office. And that hasn’t been his only worry.
For years and years he was the confidential
adviser of Walter Russell Hall; and—well,
the very rich old gentleman was difficult at
times, and nobody knew quite how to handle
him except his tactful young friend.
Brig.-General Antill. home on sick leave
after serving in Gallipoli and France, takes
a local job as Commandant of 3lenangle
camp, where they turn out the best Camel-
leros and Light Horsemen in the world.
G.-G. Ferguson addressed a seething mass
of youngsters and their mothers from the
official stand at the Christmas treat: to sol-
diers’ kiddies. Dr. 3lary Booth. 3lrs. Ted
Antill. the C. le 31. Walkers and Eric Lloyd
Jones were in the pen. Punch and Judy,
sports of all kinds, and, later on, a Christmas
tree in the Agricultural Ilall. But there
was another side to it. Had the commit-
tee realised that 12,000 children and their
maternal guardians would line up outside
the hall more or less at the same moment,
better arrangements might have been made.
Worse they could hardly have been. The
iron shutters rolled down when the helpers
inside had a big contingent of kiddies to
hand the toys to. There was for some time
—-till the damage was done—no one but a
few young lads, mere straws in the mael-
strom, to keep order among the greater num-
ber still outside. The sun blazed on the
struggling women; children fell, and some-
thing like a panic began. Babies screamed,
bigger sisters howled, women fainted on the
shoulders of those uearest. and so it went
on for nearly an hour. Where was the
large and placid Bobby who takes hold of
these human cataclysms? When mothers
with inflammable tempers threatened to
“lay out” all and sundry, perhaps the noise
penetrated the iron doors. At any rate they
rolled up, and we swept forward, a broken
battalion of women, children, and “My
oath !” said an alarmed male.
Now is the time for Sydney to show that
it really wants to keep Australian musicians
and writers in their own country. By stay-
ing away from “The Rajah of Shivapore” at
the rejuvenated Playhouse it puts up a local
"No.” Mrs. Harry Austin, by way of head-
ing a “Yes” publicity campaign, invited David
Souter and Alfred Hill to lunch. Versatile
David, having nothing to do but attend to
the artistic side of the Wm. Brooks business,
do a weekly cartoon for one panel - and a
decorative cat for another, write a capital
Bulletin yarn now and again, and give
the Scotch paper and half a dozen Caledo-
nian and other societies a hand, brought
forth the book of the show; the equally idle
and equally amazing Hill, who merely teaches
all day at the Conservatorium, conducts a
few musical societies and makes concert
appearances, besides composing musical odds
and ends while he nibbles his lunch, filled in
time by writing the music. I understand
that most of the work done by Souter and
his friend was perpetrated at peep o’ dawn.
Frederick Ward and his wife. Mrs. Souter,
the Rajah Company and a few scribblers
put their feet under the table at the Aus-
tralia, while everyoody’s friend the hostess,
a pocket-edition in a rose-pink frock, beamed
under black-lace millinery. Ward, having
proposed the health of the authors and con-
veyed the thanks of the Rajah to little Mrs.
Austin, those present bustled back to their
workshops.
J. D. McKay Brown heard the usual ques-
tion put to Nina Murdoch in St. Philip’s
Church the other afternoon, and listened
while she said she would take it on. Black-
and-white artist Hal Eyre was second-best
man. and Mrs. Murdoch saw that her girl
—a dove-grey bride with a pink posy—didn’t
keep the man waiting on the step. The
’moon is to be spent “somewhere” in a glori-
fied camp-out. with the billy boiling in the
shade of a Christmas bush. Then home to
the bungalow that looks across the blue
Pacific from a Bondi cliff.
The Tom Heneys' only treasure has a
commission in the Coldstream Guards.
When war broke out the lad, then about 15,
began worrying to get into khaki. They kept
him at the King’s School instead. Too
young to enlist in the A.I.F. at 17. he left
for England last February, and got into
prompt training in the Household Brigade’s
cadet battalion at Bushey. Young Heney is
a grandson of one of Sydney’s great journal-
ists, the late Henry Gullett. Father, of
course, is editor of the S. 31. Herald.
In spite of the
war the Fan is a
necessary luxury—
you can hang it on a
wall to save it from
the fiercest knitters
who might poke holes
in its silky waist.
Water-color is the
delicate medium in
which Florence Mof-
flin’s eternal nymph
pirouettes on a pink
toe, while a faun
snatches a kiss from
a plump white cloud.
The National Gal-
lery houses one of this
young Australian
artist’s dainty fan-
cies. and her fairy-
like work was one of the decorative side-
lines of the recent Society of Artists’ show.
But Florence doesn’t take art very seriously.
The war is distracting. So are summer fashions.
She’s a daughter of pastoralist W. A. B.
Greaves and was born with an impediment—
a silver spoon in her mouth. Julian Ash-
ton attacked it with his usual vigor. And
a tour through the galleries of Europe did
a little more to sharpen her pursuit of phan-
tasies on silk. Yet the imp called Dilet-
tante still has a playful habit of tossing her
brushes aside. Then, as suddenly, she turns
to, and out flutter charming families of fairies
who dance by moonlight on a drop o’ dew
and a grass-blade.
At the latest “smart” wedding at St.
James s Church the bride was Zoe Snape. a
Manly girl, who said “I will” to 31. C.
MePhillamy. He’s one of the elan that owns
Warroo Station, Forbes. The leading lady
was garbed in a cloud of pale-pink tulle, and
was veiled in the same tint. Rainbow effects
were the motif, so bridesmaids Mary and
Bobbie Snape, reinforced by Neradah
MePhillamy. floated up behind the bride in
blue, mauve and buttercup georgette. Black
tulle millinery had been built for all three.
Party at the Persian Garden.
Tlie 4th aud 50th Battalion and Ist Aus-
tralian Machine Gun Comforts gather glit-
tering proceeds from a recent show at the
Repertory. Minnie Hooper trained cnorus-
girls from our most expensive suburbs to
simulate the real thing. But the voices were
small and uncertain —with a notable excep-
tion or two. And if the piano didn’t always
hit the same note as the girl—well, be polite
and blame the pe-ahno. Helen Macarthur
Onslow was one of the forces that kept the
sparkling entertainment together. The Mac-
arthur twins, with floating manes of dark
brown hair, were in a song-and-slide turn
with other young charmers —dried-grass cos-
tumes to the knees and green stockings.
Monty Stephen (hardy annual who lends his
aid to these dress-circle showsj, Tony Street,
Ashley Davy and Captain Verge were the
only males available for the show. Lady
Doris Blackwood, in buttercup drapery,
lisped beautifully—we don’t often hear this
once-fashionable slither over the English s—-
as the damsel who held the tame burglar at
bay. Verge, as the burglar, was a polite
drawing-room edition of the Sikes who bangs
the girl on the head and argues after. Every
other car in local Society’s garage snorted
up to the Rep. to unload our most expensive
people for both performances.
With Christmas
comes the memory of
a little paper that
used to put forth a
special number as
the year wane d.
VI an y Australian
artists—George Lam-
bert illustrated a
story by Louis Becke
in one December
i s s u e—gave their
work for a nominal
price. Why? This
benign and dignified
face, the sole pro-
perty of Miss Jose-
phine Fot h e ring-
hame, is the answer.
Upon buying Young
Australia —a juve-
nile mag., founded by Mrs. Lawson, mother
of poet Henry—after it had passed through
various vicissitudes, the new editress got to
work. Budding talent timidly offered its
first contribution, and retired, dazed by the
warm welcome it received, to go on inking
its fingers in competitions of nerve-racking-
import. Harry Julius, then in knickerbock-
ers, was one of the most persistent artists on
the juvenile side. Kutli Bedford won her
first prize for "literary” efforts at the engag-
ing age of 10, and Young Australia moth-
ered the copy. Mary and Edie Stoddart, now
on the other side, were youthful artists who
proudly signed their names as competitors.
Fred Brown sharpened his pencil in an early
attack on its pages. And the too-kind edi-
tress praised every one, aud told them all to
go on trying—which they did, with the in-
credible faith of extreme youth that moves
gallons of ink to win a pen’orth of fame.
On the adult list were the Young Aus-
tralia’s staunch friends, Percy Spence,
Frank Mahony and David Souter. with
Ernest Favenc, who took a trifling fee for
their helo to the Christmas issue because
Miss Fotheringhame shook her bonnet till its
feather stood on end if they offered to give
their services.
Born in “Fair Kirkwall.” as Scott calls
the county town of the Orkney Isles. Jose-
phine Fotheringhame is an authority on that
old Scotland which once mingled Norse
legends with its YYile tales. Steeped in the
ancient history of the town that was a royal
borough when King Hakon’s whiskers were
a feature of the landscape, she is the author
of a book for boys, published in London years
ago. It was “Sir Valdemar,” and its hero
breathed an ancient Norse atmosphere. Val-
demar having sold out. he was followed by a
Jacobite story, “The Tale in a Red Morocco
Book.” Another publication. “Tales for
Young Australia”—in this Miss Fothering-
hame was assisted by Ernest Favenc —was so
popular that there’s a chance if its coming
out again in a new edition. Young Australia
would gladly lap up some more light mental
nourishment of the same sort from its
devoted old friend, to whom it owes and gives
a deal of affection.
Lieut. Christian, a cousin of Brig.-Gen-
eral Christian, has about three vears’ ser-
vice behind him. He’s with the A.F.A. in
the firingriine—or will be. after a brief

to Jean, a daughter of Ronald Campbell,
of Bombala. has just come along. The lass
has been on the other side for some time.
Of the three Mcßride brothers from Ryde
two, Lance-Corporal Hugh and Sapper

-Ihej re with the Field Engineers. Jack
the third brother, is also at the Front This
has been their address for the last two vears.
and the chance of a rest isn’t too good on
the present count of Noes.
There’s a recent line from artist Evelyn
Chapman, now busy with mum and dad
doing war work in England. "The easel is
turned to the wall,” says the gifted 31. L
girl, "and it will stay there, so far as I ani
concerned, till the war ends. A recent visit
to St. Ives, where we meant to holiday for a
few weeks, settled that. I was going to
unpack my almost-forgotten sketch-block. But
the tragedy of the submarined was almost at
our doors, and torpedoed crews and manv
poor dead bodies were brought in during our
stay.... In each case Ivultur had fired on
the small boats after the ships had been
sunk. Struggling in heavy seas or killed as
they tried to reach the coast —there was no
sketching for me after those horrors! Only
those.who see how Gerinany makes war can
understand the living flame of hatred that
now burns in the once-stolid, easy-going Eng-
lishman.... We have sugar' tickets in
December, but otherwise the rationing has
been voluntary.... Father is losing his
figure. Mother and I deal out the bread
with an iron hand and weigh the 2oz.
allowed an adult for each meal.”
The Chapmans have no son, but their
London home is the headquarters of innu-
merable relatives on leave in Blighty. One,
a young cousin, is that Captain Edmund
Cornish who got his captaincy and the M.C.
at 19. He was through Gallipoli in the old
13th Battalion, has been wounded and
patched up several times, and celebrated his
20th birthday in the great push. Ilis people
are in Sydney.
The Mamma State has a semi-detached
interest in a recent wedding in London. The
man was Sergeant Victor McLean. Dragoons,
a great-grandson of George William Evans,
an Australian explorer and a one-time sur-
veyor in Tassy. Gladys Gwendoline Shayer.
of Kensington-in-Fog. was the lady in white.
Neville Cayley, only son of the late bird
artist and a brushman himself, has been
quietly wed to Beatrice Doust, of Cronulla.
Neville makes that picturesque seashore his
abiding place.
Lieut. Reg. Spofforth. elder son of the
Demon bowler, has annexed Yvonne, a
daughter of the late London Spofforth. of
Richmond (Eng.). The bride is a grand-
daughter of the late Markham Spofforth.
Senior Taxing Master in Chancery. He

Evelyn.
“Australia Jane” from Bananaland:—
Sister Eileen King, daughter of T. M. King,
formerly our Comnihsioner of Railways, has been
sent to England from France, suffering from severe
bomb wounds, the gentle exponents of kultur appar-
ently having been at their old game of bombing
hospitals. Sister Amy, also within sound of the big
guns, was on a hospital ship off Gallipoli, where she
had several narrow escapes.
Yours affectionately.

Melbourne, December 24, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The vice-regal Stanleys, off to Maeedon
for the warm months, have already put in
some melting moments on the mountain side.
By the way, young Edward Stanley. looms
large in the home circle since he sported a
Grammar cap. The youth wears his hands
in his pockets, talks of moving incidents in
the tented field, and affects a spirit of
democracy in the best public-schoolboy
manner.
Canon Drought went over to the arch-
angels a week ago, leaving a devoted wife
to mourn. She was the elder of the Godr
frey sisters. The Droughts were visiting
the Fog when the war broke out, and son
Charley, who was with his parents, hurried
into khaki rightaway. He served 12 months
ere falling in the trenches. Only brother
John also arrived from Australia to do his
bit, and now holds a British captain’s com-
mission and a Military Cross. Toorak will
miss the old Canon. He had been in charge
of its most-fashionable Anglican tabernacle
since the retirement through ill-health of
that good clerical sport, the late Fellowes.
The other afternoon the Austral Salon,
with Dame Madden in command, bestowed an
official welcome on the new Mayoress, Mrs.
Frank Stapley. The chairlady, looking re-
juvenated, presented a beautiful lilies-of-the-
valley bouquet and some pretty flowers of
speech to the lady-of-honor, who fired off an
appreciative reply. Mayoress Stapley is
one of the quick talkers who speak feelingly.
When she is pleased she looks it, and when
she isn’t she doesn’t dissemble very much.
Then Mrs. Secretary Quinnell heaped com-
plimentary references on Walter Kirby, inci-
dental to his first appearance since he
parted with his appendix. Walter added a
trio of melting warbles to his expressions
of thanks, by way of assuring the company
that though he had lost his P.S., so to speak,
the sweet note was in the old place.
The least inartistic section of Melbourne
society went into mental mourning last
Thursday, when the Herald spread the news
that Fred McCubbin was dead. He had
meant so much to the artistic world during
his 33 years of teaching at Melbourne
National Gallery. He was a gentle mentor ;
something of a Bohemian whose mellifluous
little tenor warble had been a popular item
at many a smoke night; an amiable nhilo-
sopher and discourser on politics and litera-
ture, as well as art; the estimable husba r '
of bright and busy Mrs. McCubbin, and
“always the little gentleman.” Ilis parental
feelings and natural kindliness tended to
shorten Fred McCubbin’s useful life. The
outbreak of the war saddened him at once.
Anxiety for his son Hugh, who went away
with the first contingent and was wounded
at Gallipoli, made an old man of him at 60.
The work of the gifted painter lives after
him. The disappearance of his personality
is a real loss to Melbourne. He leaves no
enemies nor any harsh critics that I ever
heard of. Few artists of his ability can
have been so utterly devoid of unpleasant
characteristics.
The John Madden pair, assisted by daugh-
ter Lesley from the undies shop, and Mrs.
Guy, will eat their Christmas pudding at
Frankston. Daughter Beatrice, with her
babies, is sheltering in London with the
Vallanges until her husband, Harry Baillieu,
can bring her back to Australia next month.
The Edward Stones, of Westralia, are
making one of their occasional visits to
Melbourne and are staying at Menzies’. j.nis
time the Gropers’ Lieut.-Governor and re-
tired Chief Justice is over to have some
talks with an eye specialist.
However, as there is no serious danger of
loss of sight, he is quite cheerful about the
matter. The chief trouble, so far, is that
he is unable to recognise his old friends
except at very close quarters. The trip
across was made by the new transcon-
tinental, and the travellers reckon it’s a
splendid improvement on the old toss-and-
tumble by sea. Mrs. Norman Ewing, who
used to be Maude Stone and hadn’t seen her
mother for five years, has been up from
Hobart with a Ewing grand-daughter, but
had to go back early to fix up the family
Christmas-tree.
The Lang clan, of Lismore, is rejoicing
over the return of Captain Stewart Lang
from the Front. After a couple of -wounds
he has been passed out with an honorary
captaincy of the K. O. Scottish Borderers.
(No, strictly that doesn’t mean Knock Out.
In the Best Circles it stands for King’s
Own.)
Major Knox Knight, now somewhere in
France, hasn’t won the V.C. yet; but he has
had the agreeable experience of writing the
report on which a brother Australian, Cap-
tain Robert Grieve, Y.C., reached that sum-
mit. Mrs. Knight, by the way, is a
“Friendly Soldier’s Wife,” and almost any
day of the week her wraith-like figure may
be seen flitting by on the way to succor the
victims of a time-payment raid or to apply
first-aid to some mother-of-fourteen who has
been gassed by an impatient landlord.
Vivian de Crespigny, whose dad manages
the Bank of Victoria, now has his majority
and the Military Cross, besides a notable
record of mentions. He joined the R.F.C.
at 18.
Young Thornton Grimwade has gone into
Maribyrnong camp. He left Melbourne
Grammar at midwinter, and then dabbled
awhile in scientific chemistry with uncle
Russell Grimwade, who has been in charge
of the Bria.-General’s son since mother went
to England. The Norton Grimwades, by the
way, have left their flat, to settle once more
in the Toorak mansion they acquired from
Norman Hodges.
The Tahitians were being entertained in
a. South Yarra garden. Soon there was a
line of spectators at the fence. “Toffs hob-
nobbing with the niggers,” one explained.
“Yes,” said his political neighbor, “and
they’re asking you and me to go and fight
alongside ’em.” “That’s right,” said the old
lady who was also having a look. “Don’t
you lose your pride. You stay home and let
the niggers fight for your missus.”
Major Ted Cordner, who went over to
patch our wounded some months back, is the
latest victim. His heart became exceedingly
unwell after encountering Margaret Pruen,
a member of a Cheltenham family, and he
now feels the case demands a parson and a
choral service. The operation is booked for
an early date in 1918.
Professor Wallace has shed his sergeant’s
stripes and wears a star. Others in the
clutch, including Franklin, of the Grammar
School, and Reg Raleigh, are finishing their
military education in Sydney. The pro-
fessor-lieutenant is smiling again now, his
small boy having made a fine recovery after
several desperate weeks of appendicitis.
Miss Adelaide subscribes:—
From London comes news of the marriage of Mrs.
E. J. Peck to Lieut. George Bryce, who was once
Assistant Botanist at some unpronounceable place in
Ceylon, and is now with the R.A.M.C. She, before
her first marriage, was Katie Magarey, daughter of
the James Magareys, of North Adelaide.
On Fiiday a Christmas fair at Torrensville, under
the guiding hand of Mrs. Thomson Green (he is the
M.P. for the district), added to the Trench Comforts
Fund’s bank balance, and a fete at Medindie divided
its proceeds between the same fund, the Cottage
Homes and the Walkerville orphanage. The Burn-
side Repatriation Fund got the profits from a horses-
in-action show at the Kensington Oval, and on Tues-
day Trench Comforts workers took possession of the
Town Hall, hired Father Christmas, arranged stalls
with every variety of buyable oddment, and conducted
a Children s Christmas Party to get money for the
A.C.F. coffee-stalls at the Front. The mothers of
the 10th and 50th Battalions gave their children
a Christmas Tree and Christmas Tea on the Parlia-
ment House lawns on Saturday, and a picnic for
soldiers’ wives and children was managed by the
Unley Soldiers’ Wives committee. While I’m on
these funds I must tell you that the Cheer-up Society
has taken stock of its year’s expenditure, and has
announced that its provision bill for 12 months
was over £4OOO, and that fuel and light cost £240
for the same period, which is a three-cheer result.
Dreaming of soldiers reminds me that the Home
at Fullarton has added a recreation hall. It was
opened by Lady Galway on Saturday.
1 lince Alfred College is to have a bronze bust
of Fred Chappie as a memento of his • 30 years’
headmastership. G. A. Barnes, of the Art Gallery
has the job.
A Military Cross has come to Lieut. Vincent, son
of Major Vincent. Only 18. And—now steady—he
has held a commission in the Royal Lancashires for
nearly two years !
Died at North Adelaide on Thursday, Mrs. De
Rose, whose husband at one time had charge of the
Ordnance Department of the S.A. Military Force.
She was born in Birmingham 92 years ago, and came
to this State with her husband in ’66. I never
heard her say that she would like to go back to
Birmingham.
Ruby Davy, one of our girls, is the first woman
musician in the Commonwealth whose composition
to win a Mus. Doc. degree has been accepted by the
board of examiners.
More than one Melbourne woman had a
passing “hot and cold” spasm when she read
that old “Arthur Worthington” had breathed
his last in a New York gaol. The magnetic
Arthur started his babble of Isis and Osiris
in America, the mother-land of crude-
molasses doctrines. He came to Maoriland
22 years ago and did a dash at Christchurch,
of all places, before trying his luck in Syd-
ney and then Melbourne. A tall man, with
piercing dark eyes and effective white hair,
Worthington was adored by all the women
who fell into his treacly cult. He preached
Love and took their money. The widow
of a Sydney merchant was the Rev. Arthur’s
most lucrative adorer, and she eventually
had the pluck to lay an information before
the-police and tell her artless tale in court.
He did some years of penal servitude in
Beechworth Gaol, and then returned to
America and flourished awhile on the same
old lines. What Arthur didn’t know about
the mysterious affinities between the sexes
wasn’t worth knowing. He was a born lady-
killer of the soulful sort. Yet he carried his
twin warnings on either side of his attractive
phiz. His ears ought to have given him
away. They were set too low, or too far
back, and everybody ought to know that
the surest sign of an impostor is a pair of
misplaced wings. But women are mostly
blind to such signs of the crimes. They
don’t even feel an instinctive distrust of a
square head if it contains two blue eyes
and a decent set of t&eth.
Medical meh seem to change their invalid-
diet ideas with their calendars. In 1915
there was an immense demand for mutton-
shanks, which make a nice broth without
much bother. Last year brought a clamor
for small orders of gravy-beef, shanks being
almost neglected. Now the profesh, seems
to have decided that there is no particular
nourishment in broth or beef-tea, and has
gone baldheaded for sheep’s brains. It’s a
tantalising position for the butcher, since
there is only one head on each sheep, while
one strong invalid, trying to make up for
lost time, can get through the mental works
of three animals inside 24 hours. Besides,
I like ’em myself—cooked in a skillet with
a bit of sage and butter and turned out on
a square of toast.
Margaret Thomas and Elizabeth Glover,
who have made such a success of the Trained
Nurses’ Club, have been secured by an astute
proprietary to run the household side of
Craig’s Hotel, Ballarat. A send-off by the
nurses is to happen at an early date.
Marjorie Raphael is back in Melbourne for
a little while, ever so much plumper than the
slim girl of six years ago, and looking in-
tensely like an' actress. She wears beaute-
ous pearls and is staying at Menzies’. The
war lost no time in making fair Marjorie
a relict. One Friday her husband, Norman
Raphael, left England with his brigade. The
next Friday he was dead on the Flanders
Front. What his blonde young relict thinks
about the war and its management is vastly
different from what the Censor permits to
be printed about it.
Ex-Gaud Mayor Ilenriessy and his dame
have gone down to Frankston to eat their
turkey and plum-pudding and forget all
about the ingratitude of man.
A garden fete raged lately over the Toorak
lawns and flower-beds of the John Grice
pair. The stallholders were all successfully
or unsuccessfully powdered and patched in
the Georgian mode, although the whole
bazaar frenzy had been christened Early
English. The young things handing round
the buttered buns looked just it in white
draperies with mob caps on their floured
locks. _ The Grice dame sounded the opening
note in a neat little speech, and emptied a
well-filled purse to help the show, which was
in aid of the local St. Martin’s.
Fulford, lately British Consul at Tien-
tsin, who gave up the job to enjoy the com-
fort of his native Australia, found he could
not endure standing outside the great
Chinese question which has been born out of
the world-wide war. He has gone north
again, leaving his wife and little daughter to
await events.
The Central Branch of the Soldiers’ Wives
Friendly Union got through the Christmas
frivol this year without a hitch. No pirates
stormed the festive scene this time and got
away with the goods as in 1916. Manager
Denton came in with the loan of Hoyt’s
Olympia, and the staff gave its services.
Nearly 2000 kiddies and beaming mothers
turned up. General Hughes’s missus took
command in the absence of Lady Helen, and
the wives of the best brass hats gave a hand.
The youngest of squatter Quin’s three
unmarried girls has accepted the usual
diamond from young Bill Tetley, sec-
ond of the Weetwood Berwick’’ sons.

The L. K. S. Mackinnon matron ma
daughter Margot have spent many busy
weeks making garments of strange design
for a regiment of kewpies. These eccentS
trifles are being sold to swell the Leaein
funds. Here and there, though, you begin to
find a kick aimed at the worship of these
freak dolls as a thing calculated to warn the
young ideals—“ The Cult of the Monstrous”
it is termed. And there’s some force in the
term. Long years ago John Ruskin passed
caustic remarks on the same thing.
Muriel Atherton, who is to marry Captain
Geoff Grice, did not sparkle quite so brightly
as the two elder sisters in our pre-war smart
set. But she used a paintbrush and neneil
with skill. The family drifted to the Fog
two years ago. Captain Geoff, like brother
Claude, is keen on horseflesh, and did not
shine in two-stepping circles. He never got
into dress-clothes without strong inducement
Ilis mother was a Sumner. The Grices and
Sumners were partners in the firm-that-was.
Walter Kirby celebrated his divorce from
his appendix with an impromptu vocal recital
the other night. Walter ransacked his
repertoire for the benefit of the Red Cross
and filled two or three hats. Previous to
departing from Mount St. Evin’s Hospital
the young man sang to the sisters and lifted
the roof off with his impassioned warbling.
Anybody with a stray roof that he cannot
account tor might forward same to the hospi-
tal as soon as possible.
Lieut. Heron; son of the Commercial Bank
manager, is figuring once more on the list of
wounded. Shirkers will please note that
Heron made one at the Gallipoli Landing,
soon after which he- lost the sight of an eye!
For some months he was in hospital, but got
back to Gallipoli in time for the closing
scene. Since then he has been again and
again in other fighting lines until the shrap-
nel caught him. At Dalgety’s, where he
worked in pre-war days, the clerks talk
admiringly of him, and the girl section is
said to be thinking of a novelty in welcomes.
Their last outburst was for Lieut. Butler,
who came back on one leg. Butler still
blushes at his recollections. The males stood
up and cheered whilst the womankind pelted
him with roses, as though he were Juflus
Beaucaire.
The W. J. T. Clarke people have locked
the doors of their Toorak place and buzzed
off to Mt. Schank, the family sheep-run in
South Aus.
Mrs. Cottrell Dormer has drifted here
from Tassy to meet her husband, the British
major, who is coming back on short leave.
The poor lady is still shaking at the recollec-
tion of that awful night when, going down to
a summer-house for her knitting-bag, she was
half-throttled by a robber, who got clean
away with her best finger-gems and neck
gauds.
The General Bridges widow has taken a
houselet at Frankston, so that the sea
breezes may blow the office cobwebs out of
the hats of daughters Dorothy and Babbie at
Aveek-ends. Dorothy adds and subtracts for
the E. S. and A. Bank ; Babbie toils at the
Barracks.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Fanuela”: While the billy boiled Billy
Wilson told me about it. “I was scrub-falling at
Kingarov last year,” he said, “and took a contract
to do 150 acres. I started on Monday, and had
finished brushing and nicked all the trees by Friday.
Then I went to the top of the hill, where the largest
bottle-tree in the district was standing, cut it down
on Saturday morning, and went to collect my cheque.
The farmer was astonished. ‘Finished already!’ he
said. I said I had, collected, and spent Sunday' in
Brisbane. Six weeks later I went back to Kingaroy,
and that scrub was still falling!”
“Devilfish” ; There was excitement in the
Speck a few years ago when 50 whales came ashore
at Perkins Island, on the north-west coast; but this
was nothing to a recent stranding at Cloudy Bay, a
lonely inlet at the southern end of Bruny Island. A
man who visited the remote place recently came on
between 120 and 130 whales on the
beach, and some of them were beg!
ning to shriek to Heaven about the.r
bad luck.
“Snell”: It was necessary for the honor of
Australia to dull the edge of a small Scot who was
talking of windy conditions he had experienced in
the China seas. “Talkin’ of wind,” said John,
“there ain’t nothin’ windier blows than the willy-
willies of Westralia. I was caught in one once, ’n’
when it started blow in’ houses at me, I got into
the solidest-lookin’ holler tree I could find, ’n’
waited for the chunks to pass. There was nothin’
didn’t blow by while I was in there. There was-
a whole ’orse ’n’ cart goin’ five foot from the
ground, with the bloke drivin’ ez if nothin’ out of
the ordinary was ’appenin’. All the time I'm
thinkin’ I'm all right, till, whiz! bang! somethin'
hit the tree, ’n’ I’m put out with shock. When
I recovers it’s dark ’n’ still, but I’m a pris’ner.
The openin’ I got in at is barred. When daylight
(omes I’m wise enough. The wind had tore up a
rail from the railway a mile off, heaved it through
the air, ’n’ swished it agin the tree-trunk with
such a swipe it curled round twice, ’n’ there was
1 barred in, a hopeless, ’elpless fixture.” John
ceased talking, and resumed the greasing of
his harness. “But how did you get out?” asked
the Scotchman. “I ain’t macle up me mind,” said
John. “Ain’t made up yer mind? What do you
mean—you ain’t made up your mind?” “I don't
know,” said John, “whether the sun was so hot
that day it melted the iron or the willy-willy come
on again ’n' blew the rail back. I ain’t made up
me mind.”
“G.J.H.” : When driving between Bruthen
and Nowa Nowa (Vic.) a day or two ago we raised
a mob of kangaroos of the grey flier variety. As the
last of the mob, a big buck, crossed the road, he
suddenly staggered and fell. We examined him care-
fully. ile appeared to have suddenly lost the use of
his hind legs, but there was no sign of either frac-
ture or dislocation. We helped him to his feet, but
he immediately toppled over again and when we
passed that way a few hours later he was dead. There
was no trace of a wound and no indications of
snake-bite or poison. Heart failure from fright?
“Spud” : Billjim Camellero isn't proud of
himself in his capacity of ship-of-the-desert naviga-
tor, and feels miserable whenever he meets a Light
Horseman’s amused and superior grin. I am skipper
of a brute the boys call H.M.S. Stinker, and although
lm not really in love with myself when on the
bridge, I don't object so much as some to the beast,
in spite of his itch, septic sores, smell, mange and
reckless speed. I remember it to his credit that,
when there’s nothing else to burn in this denuded
Holy Land, the ugly old bag of tricks produces the
usual substitute without extra charge. Giddup!
“E.11.H.": Re distance a camel can be
ridden in one day. Unless the steed with a hump
has deteriorated, “Cole” (B. 22/11/’l7) has not met
a Carbine. In the early 90’s for a wagfer a camel
was ridden from Southern Cross to Coolgardie in a
few minutes under 10 hours; distance, approxi-
mately, 120 miles. Another riding camel owned by
the late W. R. Wilson, and well known about
Menzies, would think “Cole’s” 65 to 70 miles in a
daj a pipe-opener. The camel may be a bit smell-
ful, but he’s a goer of quality.

FAST QUALITIES.
SMART DEALER : “Oh, not a had line of
wethers, Mr. Jumbuck, but very
burry, and there's nothing sticks to
a sheep like Bathurst burr."
MR. JUMBUCK: “Except the price I have
on them 'ere sheep."
of all—a fox-terrier is afraid of a fox! Attended a
fox hunt recently in a Victorian village—not the
sort conducted by the Quo.rn or the Beauvoir Hunt
Club, but a chase got up by the lads of the village
after a puppy fox which ran up a drain in the main
street. They burnt brown paper, bark, cow pats,
picture-show posters, referendum circulars and three-
penn’orth of sulphur, all to no purpose. Then three
fox-terriers were tugged along and pushed violently
up the drain. Directly they sniffed the fox the
dogs, squealing and yelping with fright, dashed
out of the hunt. It raised the grave suspicion that
a fox-terrier is called one because he’s afraid of a fox.
Also it raises a big question: Is any dog any use for
anything? One can understand a horse or a cow or
a sheep being some good to somebody, but a dog—-
well, no wonder they tax dogs and bachelors.
* * * »
“Ftp. Enterprise” : I want to introduce a
good Australian. On Mafeking Day, as a boy, I
brought home about a dozen young rosella-parrots from
the bush and distributed all but one amongst friends.
With the exception of this one all have since died.
The survivor is over 17 years of age and as well as
ever he was. I put it down to feeding him on the
flowers of the common milk-thistle. I saw one of
his relatives about 12 months ago in the London Zoo
competing, among a host of other birds of the world,
in the Beautiful Plumage Stakes, and winning every
time.
* *
“Jas. Hyde”: “Mrs. Lance Rawson” (B.
22/11/’l7) complains that “many writers allude to
all aboriginal women as lubras, whereas a lubra is a
young gin before she takes a mate.” When giving
the meaning of any abo. word the part of Australia
in which it is used should be stated. There are many
abo. languages and dialects in Australia, and a word
in one lingo often means something very different
from a word of the same sound used in another part
of this dustheap. Before me is a pamphlet purporting
to give the meaning of abo. words. “Wild animal”
is the meaning attached to one word. It may be the'
meaning of the word in some localities, but in Com-
laroi it has a shockingly indecent meaning. As far
as I’ve investigated the matter I’ve never yet been
able to prove that the word lubra originally belonged
to any Queensland abo. language. In the early days
I never heard it used by the blacks around Broad
Sound, Mackav or Bowen, nor on the Isaacs and
Suttor rivers. 1 admit that I'd little chance of get-
ting information from the murris, for at that time
they were not allowed in on most stations in those
parts. Incidentally, a complaint having been made
about stock being interfered with, native police came
and “dispersed” the offending tribe. In plain English,
the camps were rushed suddenly and as many male
adults shot down as convenient. I once asked a native
policeman if lie ever shot gins. His answer was:
‘.My wud, plenty time!” As for abos. being canni-
bals, the first cases of habitual cannibalism that I
can personally vouch for happened on the Johnstone
River, and it is probable that some of the tribes
north of Cardwell were cannibals more from choice
than want. All the blacks south of the Burdekin that
I came in contact with in the early times were quite
indignant if it was suggested that they were man-
eaters. I never heard Jimmy Morrel say a wo H
the blacks eating human flesh, and he had Hv f 1
years among them in the Bowen district befnrtVJ
whites settled there. After an absence of 35 the
I returned to the North in 1909, and have ''b^
ambling about north of Townsville since. I find in
some of the different languages on the coast words
of Kanaka, Chow, Hindu and Jap origin mixed with
the ancient yabber, and people who know nothing of
abo. lingo make curious mistakes in consequence.

“Jimmie Pannikin” : When I was in the
beautiful Yass-Gundagai district (N.S.W.) I saw
some queer bird-nests. A swallow made its home in
a cup that had been left on one of the beams inside
tiie Derringullen half-time school, a few miles out
of Yass. The headmaster, William Patrick Faulder,
an ardent nature lover, took the nest-lined cup down
for me to inspect, but he was careful not to touch
the rim of the vessel. In due course the brood was
hatched, and the cup-nest was kept as a specimen
in the school's most interesting museum. At the
Rugby provisional school, in charge of another bush-
lover, Ernest Collins, I was shown a thrush’s ne*t
built in a biscuit-tin used as a flower-pot. The
flower-tin was just at the side of the door, on a

CARRYING WATER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Bee-il! Bee-il! Where’s he to?
Pay some heed to what I say,
Or, my lad, I'll make you rue.
Think there’s nothin’ else to do
But to skip around and play,
Wastin’ all your time away?
Look into that barrel, will you?
When your dad comes home he’ll kill you.
Dry’s a bone, you lazy monkey, and to-morrow’s
washin’-day.”
Trampin’ down to Mint}' Spring,
Luggin’ water ’art a year!
When the grass-seeds dig and cling
And the lenpin’ ’oppers ping
Like a fiddle at your ear,
And your spine is out of gear,
Iron handles grind and blister!
“Look you slippy, my young mister—
Not a drop for washin’ father, and his knock-off
time is near.”^
Luggin’ water is a treat
That would break a giajit’s heart;
In the smoke and in the heat,
Tinglin’ eyes and burnin’ feet!
Two full buckets at the start —
Home with ’arf it if you’re smart;
Some you spill and some you swaller.
Twenty trips and more to foller—-
With the heat that ole rain-barrel’s gone and fairly
bust apart.
Now the sun his flamin’ spears
Flicks behind the ranges grey,
And the north wind grills and sears,
And my strainin’ joints appears
’Zif they’d surely pull away.
Them there youngsters I could flay,
Drinkin’ when they didn’t oughter,
And I'm prayin’, totin’ water,
Deal take and fry the feller went inventin’ washin'-
day!
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

FOSTER’S LAGER
“Ornis”: Dudley Le Souef, of the Mel-
bourne Zoo, has lately been rest-curing in Tassy, and,
incidentally, lecturing on behalf of patriotic funds.
Tiie other day at Hobart, in the course of a lecture
on birdlife, he passed some very forceful remarks
concerning a notice on all the Speck’s railway
stations offering a reward for the heads of hawks.
Next day the matter was taken up in Parliament,
and members of the Government tumbled over them-
selves in their anxiety to disclaim responsibility for
the notice. I have had a somewhat extended experi-
ence with all the smaller hawks, and can assert,
after having examined some scores of stomachs, that,
with the exception of the sparrow-hawk, all of these
birds do far more good than harm. The brown
hawk, so often seen around farms, and more fre-
quently shot than any other kind, I have never
found wdth anything other than the remains of
moths or grass-feeding caterpillars inside. The
swamp-hawk is far keener on young- rabbits, rats and
bandicoots than on chickens. The harriers and
kestrels live almost exclusively on four-legged ver-
min. I am willing to admit that all hawks take an
occasional chicken, but such lapses are more than
outweighed by the all-the-y ear-round good work
they do.
* * * *
“John Quill”: There is great truth in
what was said in the Abo. column -some weeks' ago,
viz., that our blacks are subject to a spasmodic
desire .to kill which may assert itself at any time.
An aborigine who has any regard for your health w 11
not allow you to proceed ahead of him in the bush.
The writer and others used to go out shooting near
Wagga (N.S.W.) with a blackfellow named Barney.
Barney would never allow us to go in front of him,
and he would even use force to make us cbm-
ply with his wishes. In our ignorance we always
imputed such conduct to his selfish desire to get the
best shots. Barney was well treated by his em-
ployer, he had little work to do, and received good
pay. He was deeply attached to his boss, and the
boss looked agreeably upon him. One day Barney
stole something and was scolded. Not long after-
wards he made an attempt upon the boss's life, and
was sentenced to six months’ gaol. He died of fret-
ting after three weeks’ confinement. I’ve known
Westralian blacks pull a friend back when he
attempted to take the lead, saying “Badja” and
tapping their heads. They mean “that sulky feller
tellem kill.”
“F.R.S.” : I’ve cursed Barcoo rot with the
best of you. Last June I was dropped into an
irrigation area in N. S. Wales to carry on the job
of schoolmaster. A few weeks later I had to use
the chalk with hands that were encased in packing.
I had good scran, too. My neighbor, a resident of
three years, has again developed the rot. He in-
forms me it was prevalent in Broken Hill, while
another resident avers that it belongs to Riverina
only. The area doctor told me that the channel-
builders were greatly troubled with the ailment, and
that it took him three years to discover an oint-
ment to cure it. By the way, can any bargain-
hunter beat this? Any family here, father, mother
and flock up to the age of 16, can gtet medical
attendance and medicine for 10s. per quarter.

“J. J.D.’’: A sow owned by me and reared
on Breakfast Creek station, Cowra (N.S.W.), aged
two years, recently gave birth to 19 fully-developed
young ones. Four were suffocated owing to the
mother lying on them; but the others are doing
well. Can any abo. sty come one on this?

“Steve Hart’’: I won't have it that a
rosella parrot is the one perfect bait for foxes (B.
1/11/’l7). His nibs wall take on any bird, even
crows. Here’s another cert. When you’ve fin-
ished your lunch of sardines, into the remaining oil
put strychnine and a little carbonate of soda, and
the fox is yours—-two if it’s the honeymoon season.
Also, when laying strychnine and jam for rabbits,
don’t waste money on oil of rhodium or aniseed, etc.,
as an attraction. Use good old eucalyptus oil, 12
of 15 drops to 21b. jam, and —well, mine's a pot
when you sell the skins.

“Bad Australian”: Away back in the
winter of ’O7 I was in charge of a tunnelling job
close up to the summit of the Andes. The mountains
were covered deep with snow, and one after-
noon, just as shifts were being changed, an
immense avalanche came down across the
tunnel mouth. The men who were inside
were all right; but the remainder of the
crowd, some 10 or 12, accompanied the slide
to the bottom of the gulch—about a mile
below. Those who were imprisoned in the
tunnel soon dug a way out and started to
prospect for their pals, a task which seemed
fairly hopeless. But they stuck to it like
machinery, and early next morning they
struck four or five cadavers frozen as stiff as
logs. I met several half-breeds coming down
towing what looked like sleig’hs behind them.
The sleighs turned out to be frozen corpses.
Each difunto was snaked along over the
hard, frozen snow by a lasso hitched around
its ankles for convenience of traction, and
most of them had the points of the hips and
shoulders worn down so flat that the articula-
tion of the joints showed like the graining
of an oaken panel. In one case the head had
suffered so much from attrition that about
one-fourth of the skull had been filed to a
perfectly flat surface. When I started in to
lecture one of the boys about inhuman con-
duct he simply shrugged his shoulders and
said, “Que vamos hacer, senor! They don't
feel it and it is easier to slide a man to his
last home in the Cordillera than to carry
him.” Seeing that it was no use arguing I
gave the boys a good nip all round and signed
the burial certificate.
“Dr. Nil’’: Long ago I read Mark Twain’s
remarks upon the ant. To-day I stood over a green-
head’s nest with one doorway, and tried to make
sense out of what the greenies were up to. It was
a warm afternoon and sunny, so the wise insects were
extra lively—though of course not half so rapid
as their 1-32 inch, congeners, the pismires. Several
were meandering around the dwelling, apparently,
like Sandy Macpherson, looking for a policeman to
tel? them where they lived. One of them suddenly
stumbled over his own doorstep, and immediately
dived half-way in, hesitated and backed out again;
rushed hysterically up and down the landscape for
a minute or so, and then fell into his hole again
by accident. A moment later he reappeared, stagger-
ing upstairs with a dead greenie in his pincers, and,
hoisting it over his left shoulder, made off at 50.9
miles per hour over the very roughest track he
could pick towards the jungle of barley-grass under
the fig-tree. No ant-nest known to me existed in that
direction. Presently a small pearly object appeared
coming out of the upcast; it proved to be a newly-
hatched egg in the jaws of a panting, straining ant,
who rushed away with it in the same direction as
No. 1, but by a different and, if possible, more im-
possible track. Some anxious minutes elapsed. Then
another I.W.W. exploded from the home hole with
a friend’s corpse and made a traverse around the
village and finally disappeared, bearing west-by-south-
three-quarters-west, towards the fig-tree. Later, two
greenies, wandering about in search of pleasure and
fresh air, met near the homestead sliprails and
failed to give the password. Promptly No. 1, who
was the bigger of the two, seized No. 2, using his
mandibles, hoisted him overhead and pranced off
with him to the cemetery under the fig-tree, in spite
of explanations and blasphemy on the part of the
weaker vessel. Later, more eggs were rushed west-
ward, more defunct ants were carted towards the
fig-tree. Then a small pismire fell foul of one of
the I.W.W.’s, and grabbed him by the middle groin
and held on like a bulldog. I.W. tried to get his
tweezers into pismire. No go—the latter was on to
his blind spot and I left them doing jiu-jitsu. About
sundown I went back to see the finish, but all I could
find was the undertaker straggling back with the
late lamented and looking for home, sweet home,
which he couldn t find. The difference between
instinct and reason ’ is that the latter can result
in mistakes, the former cannot. I said that years
ago. Now, have these frantic workers at nothing-in-
particular got “instinct” or “reason,” or are they
merely human ?
* * * *
“Jobber”: Here in England one rarely gets
any news of Australia except through the British
Australasian. No wonder the average inhabitant
of the country still knows very little of Australia;
and what’s more, he doesn’t seem interested. As for
the farmers—well, they’re pretty hopeless. Some of
them are good ; others are just cattle-dealers. Would
you believe it, they don’t, as a rule, christen their
COU'S t
“Banyena”: Here’s my personal experi-
ence. I was three summers in the Mallee (Quarnba-
took district) after the 1902 drought, and was
attacked by Rarcoo rot in the first and third years,
although I was living on good average tucker The
last season I was there (1905-’O6) the spew was very
bad, seasoned cookies being attacked with the same
enthusiasm as newchmns. I found the sudden-death
cure—leave the district. In 1904-’O5 the flies were
"xtra-bad, but there was no sign of rot or spew
Ihe furthest-south case of rot that I know of was
at Horsham (Vic.). That was not long ago
“Ses” : It looks as if the carpet-snake, one
of our largest reptiles and one worth preserving, will
be the first to disappear. Its habitat was only a
small strip of the eastern coast, where it was once
very plentiful, but is now rare. Our fowlhouse used
to be raided by the variegated- wriggler on an aver-
age about twice a week. A frantic squawk in the
night would be the signal to get after the burglar.
If we arrived promptly we found the python on the
roost, trying to get down without losing the poul-
try ; and w'hen it flopped on to the ground it pro-
vided as much excitement as two native cats. We
were sorry to be rude to him, for the carpet has
good points. In rat- or mice-haunted places it can
be recommended before the family cat. It is easily
tamed, and is a perfectly safe pet once it has been
taught to keep out of one’s bed. We placed one in
a big barn that was over-run with mice, and it
remained there, sleeping on the ridge-pole during
the day, till the rodents had all disappeared, though
men were working in the barn every day. Another
was lodged in a dairy. One morning it was found
swimming in the big vat of overnight milk. It
proved to have a weakness for milk, so we had to
issue a regulation under the War Precautions Act.
“J.N.A.”: The 320-miles stretch of
uncurved railway track is not the only record they
boast of for the Nullarbor Plain (S. Aus.). In 1905,
at the commencement of the big drought of that
period, rabbits were coming down in millions from
the Outback country to the coastal areas of the
plain, where there was at least some water, though,
as usual in such localities, religiously guarded. Five
blacks asserted their ingenuity, and in five days
killed and skinned no less than 13,000 bunnies.
Their plan was to open one or two small holes into
the water enclosures, and, so soon as the famished

get to work with waddies. Eyewitnesses state that

THE SCOFFERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the time of the golden wattle,
As I came riding through,
Sweet Mary sang in the morning,
And the Maggies were singing, too.
It is I that have the music
And the voice, that chimes again,
Though all of the men be jealous,
And the maids be like the men.
Sweet Mary sang in the bushes,
And the Maggies sang in the shade,
And ’twixt Them there in the dewtime
’Twas the wonderful song they made.
And so in the morning gladness,
A young man, big and bold,
I joined in the singing chorus
With a voice that was rich as gold.
Three Jackies came from the distance,
And oh, but they looked so wise!
Three feathered imps of the devil,
With the mischief in their eyes.
I sat with my arms akimbo,
My voice like a chiming bell,
Though Mary gave over her singing,
And the Maggies they stopped as well.
And merry of heart in the morning,
1 sang, though, in truth, I knew
Sweet Mary was laughing and laughing,
And the Jackies were laughing, too.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.

“Silas Snell”: The waiter was not com-
plaining ; but obviously he thought I ought
to know. “Yes, sir, a waiter has a deal to
put up with. People regard us as if we
was somethin’ a little different, somethin’
not quite, as it were. There was those sol-
diers I mentioned, for instance. Two of
them were all right almost, but the third,
the’ little, stocky feller, he had a bloodshot
eye and a way of looking at things. He had
been to the war for several years, and he
had had nothin’ to eat —nothin’ worth while,
that is. lie was makin’ up for it. He ate
everythin’, he drank everythin’, and after the
fifth course he says, all of a sudden, his
bloodshot eye on me, ‘Do we eat the waiter?’
The others, being very drunk, told him most
soberly, pronouncin’ their English very care-
ful, that the waiter was not to be ate. ‘Why
not?’ says the little feller with the bloodshot
eye. ‘Why not eat the waiter? There’s not
enough of anything else. There can’t pos-
sibly be enough of everything else. I sug-
gest we eat the waiter. Now, how?’ says
he. ‘How,’ he says, ‘do you prefer your
waiter —-stewed or on the arf shell?’ The
other gentlemen said lie really mustn’t think
of it. One, I remember, said : ‘lt’s never
done now, Billy. Since the war no one eats
the waiter.’ ‘Why?’ says the little feller.
‘Underbred,’ whispered the tall officer.
‘Righto!’ says the little feller, and he was
most sincere, ‘we’ll make a san’wich of him.
Everythin’ else is ate,’ says he, ‘now clean
up the waiter.’ Well, he kep’ at it about
entin’ the waiter, and when they was leavin’
without havin’ ate me, he was most dis-
gusted. When it came to takin’ up a shillin’
a head for my tip the little feller with the
bloodshot eye burst out again. He flung his
overcoat on the floor. ‘There you are,’ he
says, ‘what’id I tell you? A shillin’ for the
waiter! You gutter pay for the blighter
whether you eat him or not.’ They pulled
him away to wash his han’s, ’n' I forgot all
about him till about fi’ minutes later, when
all of a sudden somethin’ crawled from under
a table and bit me most dreadful on the
leg. It was the little feller with the blood-
shot eye, and says he : ‘So help me,’ he says,
‘l’m goin’ to have my bob’s worth, anyhow !’ ”
* * * *
“Trans-Australian” : I am wakened by
“Ouyen’s” shout that “at last the country
has got its travelling bank” (B. 15/11/ IT).
For the last four years the East-West Rail-
way has had one; at my end it covers 500-
odd miles. We also have a travelling
fruiterer; greengrocer, store, baker and
butcher. When the butcher runs out of
mutton he does his own killing on the train
(called “the Tea and Sugar”), as there
is always a truck of live jumbucks. Also,
in the event of anybody misbehaving, there
is a Jay Pay to administer justice, and a
John Hop to see that the penalty is carried
out before he moves on to the next camp.
"The lumpers on the Port Adelaide wharves
are taking steps to convince the local Cham-
ber of Commerce that the charges of pilfer-
ing ships’ cargoes ought to be sent to another
address. Two recent cases are mentioned:
in one, £4O worth of leather goods having
been abstracted, some bricks stamped with
their New York maker’s name were substi-
tuted ; in the other, £350 worth of rubber
tyres having been stolen, big cubes of stone
carefully packed took their place. The stone
is said to be as clearly American in origin
as the bricks, and the inference is that any-
thing beyond mere petty pilfering is done on
the other side of the ocean.
* * * *
“Joyce”: The shortage of silver in parts
of Asia is very acute. The dollar, which is
the standard of the currency, was in October
worth 3s. 4d., whereas a few years ago it was
worth as little as Is. 7d. In other words,
our merchants have lately had to give for
a golden sovereign only about six dollars;
whereas a few years ago they had to give
more than 12. Which means that they can
buy in gold-using countries as much for £1
as'they could a while ago buy for £2. The
local tradespeople rub noses, however, and
to a great extent maintain old prices, with
the result that the public is exploited mag-
nificently. The scarcity of silver has be-
come so marked in the Straits Settlements
that the Government has issued a paper
currency for 10 cents, which is equivalent to
2.-8 pence in English money, payable to
bearer on demand at Singapore. I don’t
know whether any other country to-day has
paper money in circulation of so low a
value, but this should constitute something
of a record. This “note” is made of fairly
stiff paper and is about the size of a theatre
ticket.
“Wesfy”: The hour was late and the com-
pany was well oiled. Suddenly a cold-sober
stranger arrived and seated himself. Oyster-
opulos rushed to get his order and then flew
to the shoot. “Eggs fresh —for a police-
man !” he whispered hoarsely.

“D.F.”: The Yic. Forestry Department
has curious ideas. If it wants to plant soft
woods it persists in going nap on pinus
insignis for all sorts of places. The pinus
irisignis gives a timber which is fit only for
fruit-boxes, and it will grow anywhere, so
that it seems only reasonable to plant it
where other soft woods won’t grow and keep
the suitable places for something else. In
the higher parts of the north-east, for in-
stance," larches, firs and spruce will grow
like debts if they are only given half a
chance. And what’s wrong with giving the
native Murray pine a look-in at times?
* * * *
“J.F.IL”: Latest London novels, still priced
at 65., are using paper which would have
been rejected for sixpenny reprints three
years ago. Publisher John Lane inserts a
note in NY. J. Locke’s new novel. "The Bed
Planet,” apologising for the quality. He
says it is the only kind procurable, and. had
as it is, cost him 150 per cent, more than
the far-better paper on which his novels
have hitherto been printed. And the price
is still mounting, while, to add ,to the
importer’s troubles, exchanges to Norway
and Sweden have been going sky-high. It
is actually at this moment costing the Eng-
lish importer £l2O to pay the Scandinavian
paper-miller £lOO, irrespective of freight and
insurance.
The pub at the old goldfields town of
Berringa (Yic.) has just been delicensed.
Yet there was a time when old Tom Farrell,
the licensee, reckoned that, though the bar-
counter was the longest in Australia, it
was none too long, and he’d taken over
£lOO a night across it. /
“Condenser”: I disagree with both
“Globe” and “N.K.” as to the Perth wire-
less station being badly placed. From solid
experience I can say that Perth’s Signals
are audible to ships coming down from the
equator long before Geraldton’s can be
heard. Certainly Esperance is not too well
placed and could be moved further eastward
with advantage, particularly for daylight
working: but Perth, on the standard wave
length, is one of the best working stations
in the world. I have with li-k.w. (plain
gap) gear cleared traffic to him at over 2240
miles, through fairly strong static at that.
Mount Gambier station is quite unnecessary,
but it is ridiculous of “N.K.” to say that
“right across the Bight ships are out of
call.” Perhaps “N.K.” uses the magnetic
detector. Give me.a good piece of West-
ralian galena, and I will guarantee to
exchange traffic in daylight with either
Adelaide or Esperance all the way across
the Bight. I’ve done it.
An out-of-the-way little industry has been
rubbing along for 40 years at Southport
(Tas.). It is the collection of little “whelk”
Shells, used for making necklaces which find
a market in America as well as locally. There
used to be money, too, in the trigonia shells,
made into hatpins and such things; but the
market is more limited and the supply isn’t
regular.
“Spud”: I halt my camel near Gaza to
give three cheers for Australian clothes and
tucker. (Don’t misunderstand me: I am
doing' the cheering, not the camel.) We’ro
proud of our Australian uniforms and get
a generous outfit when we leave home; but
that doesn’t last for ever. When our trous-
seau is worn out, no Australian issue is
available and we are disguised as Tommies
Objecting to this some of the boys hang on
to their original issue till they are almosi
in rags, or else buy a tailor-made “Wazza”
tunic when on leave. Our latest reinforce-
ments always come to us brand-new and
make us feel very threadbare. Why can’t
Australia keep on clothing us? As for our
country’s canned products, we have bought
and eaten tinned tucker from every nation
and are all firmly convinced that for quality
and flavor not one foreign brand can hold ii
soldering-iron to the Australian preserved
products, especially meats and fruits, lhit
we can seldom procure them. The War
Chest and the Y.R.C., with their numerous
and generous gifts, have done mucli
to advertise Australian goods amongst
the fighting Australians. I don’t think any
of us will look for “imported” on labels
when we return. It’s done most of us good
to travel and find out what grand things
Australia can produce.
“M'cNorth” : Let me nominate the coastal
she-oak (one of the casuarinas) for making
chisel-handles, mauls and other implements
that require a wood hard to split. Under
repeated hammering it will only “burr.”
Many of the beaches of the Far North are
fringed with she-oaks which supply black
brother with abundant .material for making
first-class woomeras (spear-throwing sticks),
spears and clubs. But you want plenty of
patience to cut the timber; its excessive
hardness will take the temper out of your
axe, and you, too, if you’re not a descendant
of Job.
THE PLAINT OF THE PLAIN BLOKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
She-looked at me and smiled on me
A luscious smile, both red and wide ;
And, as she beamed, quite suddenly
My heart swelled up with joy and pride
She gazed at me as ono who sees
A pleasant sight. Her lips beguiled
Me with a gentle, “Button, please?”
I parted—and again she smiled.
I passed her later in the day,
And once again she looked at me
In quite an earnest sort of way ;
Then turned aside indifferently.
No more on me her radiance shone ;
She coldly turned me down—-to try
Hoi' flashing, brilliant smile upon
A far, far plainer cove than I.
I am a loyal sort of bloke ;
I like to help a noble cause.
Like many other simple folk
Before a smile I gladly pause
To spend a bob ; yet, once it’s gone,
It’s gone. I always find that when
I put their bally button on
They never smile at me again.
VICTORIA,
O. L.
NERVES AND THE MORNING MILK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All the milk-carts in this world
(Seems sometimes there more are!)
Down our street each morn are hurled
Long ere comes Aurora.
I'm awakened by the din,
Bound about 5 a.m.,
And those milkmen-—where’s the sin?—
Gladly I could slay-’em!
Rattle, clatter, clash! they come,
In uncounted numbers ;
Causing pandemonium—-
He is dead that slumbers.
Howls of pain to Heaven ascend—
“ Milk-o’s,” dog-like, hellish!
Howls of passion with them blend,
And the morn embellish.
Curse the fluid of the cow!
Curse all those who sell it!
Curse the matutinal row !
Curse those who don’t quell it 1
Other items should be cursed—
Pardon, if I’ve missed ’em!
Here’s the last which should be first—
Curse my nervous system!
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

POLITICAL
POINTS
In their first real fight for life have Aus-
tralians in the hour of darkest trial washed
their hands of their army? The Bulletin
will not believe it. It prefers to think that
UNIMPORTANT.
“Surely, Miss Jones, you remember me!
Why, I proposed to you when we were
holidaying here last year.”
“No-o-o! How stupid of me! Can't you
recail some other incident—something I
could remember it by?”
they wanted to rid themselves of a vitupera-
tive little person who has got himself uni-
versally disliked. Two months ago The
Bulletin warned the Conscription party of
what was coming. It declared that you
could hardly carry Free Beer if it was a
condition that Hughes should serve it. The
vote, as far as the count has gone, proves
that. Which gives this paper hope that
there is something better in store when the
incubus has been got rid of.
The Antis protested that they were not
in the pay of the Kaiser. The unions should
inquire into this breach of Labor principles.
Why should any man work for the Kaiser
for nothing :
American visitor Trueblood, who pro-
fesses oratory at Michigan University, says
it is a delight to hear Australians utter the
long “u.” But that’s nothing. He should
see us utter the long lOU.
The truth is that the Commonwealth Par-
liament has paralysed itself, and until it
gets hold of at least some of the vanished
authority it will look more like a collection
of Bolsheviks than anything else on earth.
Many a good Conscriptionist did some hard
thinking last week. The thing that Aus-
tralia voted on was, of course, only a poor
apology for Conscription. But Hughes pro-
mised, or threatened —it depends upon the
point of view—the real thing if Yes wasn’t
carried. Wherefore many a good Conscrip-
tionist argued with himself that the only way
to get the real thing was to vote down the
other thing. There was logic in it; the prac-
tical drawback was that Hughes isn't omni-
potent.
AN AWKWARD PREDICAMENT.
STUART: “If you're goin' home, you better
call your dog.”
ELIZABETH: “ Would you mind doing it?
I’m not speaking to him since he tried
to break my doll.”
And nobody invited Rivett, the pacifist
parson, to preach a sermon, dearly beloved,
from the Book of Nehemiah, iv. 14
Remember the Lord, which is great and terrible,
and fight for your brethren, your sons and your
daughters, your wives and your houses.
A bigger ass than usual is the Yic. Thistle
Act. Under this law Local Authority can
enforce the destruction of thistles by land-
owners with not enough brains to do it will-
ingly. But there are among old
subdivisions hundreds of allotments
for which owners can’t be found.
Local Authority itself can cut
thistles on private land, but only
under an order of a court of petty
sessions. Petty Sessions can only
make an order upon a show-cause
summons being served on the
owner. Obviously where no owner
can be found, no order can be
made. Commonsense would say
that Local Authority should enter
and cut the thistles in the public
interest. But this another ass-law,
the Local Government Act, forbids
as an “illegal expense.” So the
mad situation arises of thistles
being religiously cut on some land,
while scattered through it are little
patches that are a mass of the
curse, sending their seed far
around. A shire councillor re-
cently had a brain spasm and sug-
gested trying to get the Thistle Act
amended, but the shire secretary
said that’d take two years at least, so it was
decided to let things rip, as they had ripped
for 60 years.
One of the humorous features of the Anti
campaign as conducted by the so-called
Labor leaders was the vigor with which thev
insisted upon free speech. Those who have
had intimate relations with the Censorship
know that never in the whole course of the
war was the freedom of the press so re-
stricted as during the reign of the Labor
Government. There was nothing that was
not liable to be censored on the ground that
it was “prejudicial to recruiting." Among
the Ministers then and now was one whom
the Labor Opposition to-day cannot men-
tion without declaring that he is almost tne
last word in incompetence. Yet when the
Labor party was in power this paper was
prevented from applying to him the very mild
adjective “drifting.” And Arcnoishop
Mannix may be astonished to know that
some verses by a fervent Irish patriot were
deleted by a Censor acting under in-
structions, of a Labor Government. Even
a cartoon, pillorying the profiteer, was con-
demned as "prejudicial to recruiting,” and
Tiie Bulletin was warned lay the agent of
the Labor Government that if such a crime
was again committed the publisher would
be severely dealt with.
The time has come to seriously consider
Bigjohu Forrest. lie is past 70 now, and if
he were in charge of the Bank of Australasia,
for instance, he would have been farewelled
more than five years ago. Many a man, no
doubt, has at 70 .as clear a head as ever he
had and almost as sound a body; but the
Treasurer hasn't the sound body, and the
head that guided exploring parties never was
the head of a financier. If ever a depart-
ment needed a vigorous administrator, with
plenty of initiative, the Treasury wants it
now; and. grateful though Australia is for
the old warrior’s services, the time has come
—it came a good while ago, in fact —to say
that the Treasury can’t have what it needs
while John Forrest is in command.
The Kaiser wasn’t the first to show Aus-
tralia what she could do for herself if the
foreign competitor was blocked. Napoleon
was the first great patron of her industries.
In his "Statistical Account of the Settle-
ments in Australasia,” published in 1824,
Wentworth, writing as a ferocious Free-
trader, sorrowfully remarked :
The progress which this Colony has made in manu-
factures lias, perhaps, never been equalled by any
community of so recent an origin.... The origin of
this unnatural and unprecedented progress in manu-
factures may be traced to the scanty and precarious
market which existed in the Colony for agricultural
produce previously to the close of the last war.
The supply of corn and home produce exceed-
ing the home demand after ISO 4. people in-
vested money in other lines of business. By
the close of the Napoleonic wars some of
these industries, owing largely to the protec-
tion afforded by the high cost of freight and
insurance, "had taken pretty firm root.” But
Australia was blind to the hint, and the next
great war found her still without a decent
tariff, and still dependent, therefore, on the
foreigner.
Labor in Vic. is trying to dance itself
back to solvency. Attention has been drawn
to the fact by recruiting-officers who have
lately been refused permission to address
"socials” where swarms of hefty young
fellows were gathered together to waltz
funds into union coffers. Those who
keep their feet warm by crawling through
the mazy alberts are not opposed to volun-
taryism. They prefer it. It is, in fact, one
of the great principles of their lives. But
that doesn't stop them getting off a lot
of nasty language to the recruiting-officer
when he invites them to put their great
guiding principle into practice.
Victorian Governor Stanley, seeking to find
an excuse for his existence, told a Warragul
audience:—
After all, a Governor is a sort of ambassador to
the State, and he should bring first-hand knowledge
from the old country to the new.
Then it is at least reasonable to insist that
candidates sit for an examination before they
get the job.

HE: "Hurry up! The motor's been honking
for half an hour—the whole street is
out.”
SHE: “Well, then, I'm coming now.”
From a Melbourne industrial suburb:—
In this fourth year of war there are at least signs
of a stirring towards national self-reliance. Within
the last few weeks half a dozen applications for
research “factory” Registrations have come within
my purview. Most of the experimenters were n *
tern-making patterns for little metal e( w] s m
gas-fittings and other brassware, spoutine- l a
shelf-brackets, door furniture, motor
etc., that have heretofore been commercially im’
practicable in Australia because it paid somp
porter better to fetch them from Germany *'
lately, from Japan. Two men have quite- a 'decent
THE STRENGTH OF HER WEAK
NESS.
“Your mother-in-law's one of those strong-
minded women, isn't she?”
" Yes, old chap — that's her principal weak-
ness.”
little business going in piano sconces and motor
wind-screen hinges. Another firm was on metal
buttons and those little hooks that are the topmost
fastening on men s pants. This experimenter was
disheartened : the others, however, told me there
were firms ready to make up their castings at pay-
able rates, and that the war was holding out possi-
bilities of success. But not one of them was. trying
to do anything big on account of the uncertainty of
the post-war tariff. One little lot of experimenters
in their application (the presence of any machine
makes a fowl-house or tool-shed a factory within
the meaning of some Act) mentioned that they were
at work all day, and proposed to do their indus-
trial research in their spare time. A P.L.C.-ridden
municipal council turned down their application
under the sacred one-man-one-job rule! An interest-
ing feature of all the applications was that in ererii
case the applicant was a “pommy”—not immedi-
ately immigrant, but immigrant within the last five
years, and still retaining the burrs of speech. That
is a very serious matter. Does the native prefer the
imported thing? Or is it that he knows what a
horrible struggle most manufacturers have had under
the always uncertain and generally low tariffs of the
past, so that now it doesn’t occur to him to use
his brains in thinking out things and his labor and
skill in founding new industries? There is some-
thing wrong, anyhow.
RECESSIONAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Victorian Parliament is spending two months
in recess.”
M here are you, brother Bowser ? Are you fishing
down the river,
In the deep and ferny fragrance grimly working
up your punch?
In your own wild Wangaratta, are you bracing
vou-r poor liver
Vi ith the little extra somethings that a Premier
has for lunch?
Tell us what you’re doing, Bowser! Are you
swearing now-or-never,
Swinging gently in the hammock underneath
the apple-tree?
Do you sit in your old rocker, dwelling there on
past endeavor,
Let-her-ripping in the gloaming with the Arcus
on your knee?
IV ith some tea in a quart bottle and the legs of a
cold carver
In a basket, are you roving where your boyhood
days were spent, - >
Thinking over the deep meanings of the bygone
year’s palaver
And the glad New Year’s bright blessing of
enlarged emolument ?
Are you practising, o’ mornings, how to be a push-
and-shover,
With a jaw that’s grown prognathous and a
fiercely-knitted brow ?
Will you presently berserkishly come bounding
out of cover
With a howl of loud defiance and a yell of Do
It Now ?
Where are you, brother Bowser —are you camping
on the river
With a little bunch of cobbers and some fish-
hooks and a gun,
In quietude preparing now your people to deliver?
Oh, Bowser, what's the answer. Do you think
you are the one?
VICTORIA.
F. N.
LAFAYETTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
That young dragoon, with ready sword, who came
From France to aid the cause of liberty,
Made of his gallant blade a very flame
To light the path of wofld-wide chivalry;
He left his bride with tearful, pleading eyes,
Laughed at his King, and joined the high emprise.
Men set the silver tankard on the board,
And cried him fool who dared so much, to win
\\ hat peasant lifts a pike for, with his sword,
W hose veins ran noble blood, whose haughty kin
W ould cast him off, as one who rabble leads—
“ Not for the common herd a Marquis bleeds!”
The boat has slipped her moorings ; tost and torn
By many contrary winds, she yet speeds on
Until upon a thrice-approved morn
Voung Lafayette has met with Washington;
W bile, down the clouded years, old beacons shine
Recalling that first fight at Brandywine!
The days are gone of gorgeous gentlemen
With cobweb ruffles made of Brussels lace;
But France's daughters weep to-day, as when
Tears for a bridegroom stained a lovely face,
And her fine brows against the pane were set
k or one last tender look at Lafayette 1
America has wept for France's tears
And like a phantom from that buried day
She sees the young dragoon of storied years
Leap from the wooden barque that rides the bay;
And Stars and Stripes for France's shores are set —
“Me come, for we remember Lafayette!”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
