SPORTING
NOTIONS
Ihe A.J.C. Summer meeting opened a
Hand wick on Saturday with an extended men
seven events being- listed. The totalisator w
working in the paddock and leger and on the fla
and the meeting also had the assistance of the usua
hundred Bags. The machines were worked on
basis of 10s. inside and ss. outside. They got i
more than a fag-end of the business. Th
amount handled at all three machines in cl
Villiers was only £3858, and on the Grandstai
Handicap £1904.
In the Villiers Stakes the calamity which usual!
waits on backers of the favorite in that event wa
doing business as usual. The regularity with whic
the best-backed nag in the Villiers fails to even ru
into a place is one of the brightest incidents in th
history of Israel. It is a race that has fallen man;
times to roaring outsiders—rarely to a really wel
backed customer. The explanation is, of course, tha
the Villiers is run at a season of the year whe
horses that had been coming on in the Spring ar
either being spelled or going off, and others o
which little is known are getting into form. Thi
year Mehi King was solidly backed when the weight
appeared, and, in spite of a subsequent unpromis
ing display at Rosehill, money was pushed in o
him right up to the death. On the King’s best pei
formances, such as his run in the last Epsom ant
a win at Rand wick with 9st. 101 b. aboard at Tatt.’
meeting in September, he seemed a possible gooc
thing. On some of his other deeds it looked wise t
select something else. But in consequence of recen
track work there was also much preliminary backing
for Sobrina, Cetigne, Promised Land, Wedding Day
and Norbury. It was Cetigne who capsized th
favorite, with last year’s winner, Wedding Day, nex
and the frequently-surprising Merrimee third.
Cetigne, one of the best middle-distance
horses racing in Australia, is now five years old and
owns a record which might have been still bettei
with a little extra luck. At two years he hac
three wins and two seconds, and three times ran ou
of place. In the following year he was only twice
unplaced in 15 starts—got six wins and sevei
seconds. At four years he won but once, was foui
times in a place and five times not noticed. Then
he was sold for £l5OO and was well worth the
money. He has won three times since and re-
turned the new owner all he parted with and a
bit over. _ Always a brilliant miler, Cetigne
had sometimes shown an indisposition to hop oft
the mark quickly. But what he could do at the
business end of the distance was not in much doubt
when, with 9st. 31b. on top, he buried Wedge in
the Spring Stakes at the Rosehill September meet-
ing in “38.” On Saturday he carried 9st. 21b.,
started at B’s and, making a wonderful run from
the top of the straight, ended up in “381,” with
two lengths to spare.
Last year’s winner of the Villiers, Wedding Day,
made a gallant effort to get away with the cash
again. It was not until near the distance that she
got a clear run; then shakine- off Merrimee and Some
Boy, she looked momentarily a sure winner—until
Cetigne was noticed coming up with every stride.
He just caught the brilliant chestnut in the last
bit. It was lucky for Wood, on the winner, that
Connell had not been able to make an earlier start.
Mehi King was the seventh consecutive favorite
for the Villiers Stakes to miss a place in the race.
He rounded the incident off by being the seventh to
arrive at the post after a bad passage. For a time
on the course Promised Land was at a shorter price,
but eventually drifted away to 10’s. He finished
sixth. He had every chance so far as position went
in the earlier stages of the trip.
Newcastle, which is addicted to sending nags
down to Randwiek to unobtrusively appropriate the
proceeds at bargain prices. N did it again on Satur-
day. When the field for the Nursery Handicap was
being saddled, most people were inspecting Woorawa,
a handsome, promising colt who ran second in the
Breeders’ Plate, or gazing hopefully at Sunnvside and
Lingle’s sister, Pamphlet, and Shell Shock' and Sir
Hugo. When the race was finishing they hurriedly
became aware that something named Aranap was in
it. Subsequently they discovered that the aforesaid
juvenile had shown Woorawa the way to the line,
and that Ike was paying Newcastle 25’s for doing
it. Aranap is of the Linacre family, a sister to Pan-
acre, and was making a second visit to the course.
On the first occasion she was a starter in the Gim-
crack, won by Sweet Lady, but wasn’t sighted on the
way. A couple of weeks ago she started at 6 to 4
in a race at Newcastle, and ran second. Aranao
was bracketed on the totalisator with Salary, a
youngster inhabiting the same stable, and the ring
betting indicated that Salary was the more highly
thought of.
The Pistol gelding Greek Fire showed what sort
of performer he is over hurdles in June by winning
the jumping double at Randwiek without excessive
effort. On Saturday he showed what he can do in
December and, incidentally, notified that the trouble
which overwhelmed him while he was getting ready
for the Grand National at Melbourne during the
winter had disappeared. Ridden by Maxwell 1m
carried 12st. 51b., which included a lot of lead, and
did all that was wanted at 2 to 1. He won bv
nearly 50yds.
* * * *
The problem of whether Amberdown’s
nomination was in order for the Albion Park (Bris-
bane) Five Hundred, won by the colt on August 13,
has been settled bv the appeal against this win
being upheld. The ground of the appeal was that
Ainberdown was not registered at the time of run-
ning, as required by the rules. The prize-money,
therefore, goes to Barley Bree. Prior to winning
at Albion Park the horse scored several other races,
and the question whether the stakes can be recov-
ered at law is understood to be engaging the earnest
attention of Costs. Meanwhile argument about bets
on these various races rages with great fury. It also
happens that, prior to being scratched for the
last Epsom, Amberdown was backed for that event,
and ever since there has been disputation between
Ike and the punters as to which of them is en-
titled to the money wagered. If Amberdown’s
nomination had a hole in it, then he was not entitled
to annex the Epsom, and backers had no chance of
winning. As he was apparently ineligible for
Randwick, it seems as if the punter’s protest against
being made a loser has been sounder than Ike’s
anxiety to make a profit.
* * * if
A lot of people have been guessing lately
how much a stallion with a reputation would fetch
just now, and as two such nags are to be
put under the hammer during the next fortnight,
the guessers have not long to wait for an answer.
The two are Tressady, of the Brown stud, and Sol
Green’s Comedy King. The lift given to Green’s
horse by Biplane has been considerable, and there
are many people who reckon he will realise the
bigger sum. It may turn out so; but Tressady is
a remarkably fine animal and quickly made a name
for himself. The most interesting point about the
sale of these horses, though, will be the light shed
on the estimate breeders have of the future value
of yearlings and of racing generally. The habit
of paying high prices for young stock is certainly
declining, not without good reason. Of course
the money to be won by a first-class horse will in
all likelihood continue to be such as to make
original cost of very little importance. But the
question of real importance is whether there is any
probability of racing being continued on such lines
as to cause keen demand for anything with breed-
ing and four legs. This page is fairly positiv
that there is little likelihod of it, and that the time
when Australians provided for themselves mor
racing than any other people on earth are beginning
to pass. On the whole, there will be nothing
this to be sorry for.

become so infirm at 26 that the misery had to b
ended last week with some chloroform and a bullet
A great racehorse and a great sire. He won many
notable races at Randwick, Caulfield and Flemington
and begat such nags as Emir, Trafalgar, Patrobas
Kingsburgh, Mountain King, Lady Wallace, Wolaw;
and Amata. The famous mare Cruciform went to
Wallace several times, but the partnership was
disappointment. The old horse was bred by W. R
Wilson. At the sale of Wilson’s stud he was bough ;
by J. V. Smith for 750 guineas and went to Bun
doora Park (Vic.). Up to the end of last racing
season his family had won 761 races and gatherec
in £213,500 in stakes.

There are good judges in Maoriland who
-believe, or at any rate say they believe, that the cob:
Ilymestra, lately sold for a hatful at the break-up of
the Ormond stud, is better than Biplane. There are
others who hold that he would beat Desert Gold ovei
a middle distance. Still this writer would be sur-
prised if there is anything in Maoriland to beat
Biplane at w.f.a. over a mile or 10 furlongs. That
there are two nags that could do it is incredible.
Apart from one shocking exhibition, Biplane gal-
loped in Australia like the champion that only
arrives on the scene now and again. But it is not
certain what he would look like in a first-class Derby
field. Australian experience of him gives no line on
that—except to this extent, that there was nothing
here to run him down or go anywhere near it.
The haughty boarders of a St. Ivilda (Vic.)
hash-foundry had the unprecedented weariness of
foraging for their tucker the other morning. It
seems that the chef and the duchesses who usually
waited at table had departed hurriedly at something
a.m. without giving notice of the evacuation. The
whole house rocked with inquiries, but it was not
until the end of a frantic day that the landladv
heard that her staff had vanished to cash a first
prize which they had caught in Tatt.’s.
Forecasted that one of the things certain
to happen soon after peace is a fly from England to
Australia. The thing seems easilv within the
bounds of probability, for by taking' a roundabout
trip there would be a dry place to fall on most of
the way, and the stages could be easily regulated
so as not to get beyond reach of oil and repairs.
The estimates of the necessary time hover round
nine days. The weakness is that, while it is
known that a man can ride an aeroplane at full
speed for a dozen hours or so, it is not known what
he would be feeling like if he had to ride for 20
hours daily for more than a week. It would cer-
tainly be a tremendous trial of endurance and nerve
stability. The idea that mails betwen Svdney and
London will be per air before the kids are much older
seems a bit over the odds until a new race of post-
men has been raised.

* -X * *
Ilex Norman, the best of the left-handers
in Sydney cricket this season, is a good all-round
sport. He has already taken 30 wickets averaging
14.56 for Glebe, a return to the form he showed in
1915-16, when he topped Glebe’s club list with 50
wickets averaging 15.50. Norman’s eight for 31
against Petersham was next to Jack Scott’s nine for
32, the best effort in that season’s premiership
matches. In colder months Rex Norman kicks for
S. Sydney in League football, while brother Ray does
similar things for Eastern Suburbs.
Dave McAllister, a N. S. Wales champion
rifleshot, has been killed in France. He won the
King’s Prize in 1913 and was sixth in the same event
in the previous year. In 1912 he represented N. S.
Wales in the Commonwealth matches in Tasmania
and in Brisbane the next year. In 1914 he was at
Bisley, and came away laden. He leaves old club-
mates at Harden, Goulburn, Gundagai and Junee.
* * * *
Tasmania has a few “firsts’ 1 in cricket.
The Speck defeated Victoria by three wickets in
the first “intercolonial" at Launceston on February
11, 1851. Charlie Eady’s 566 at Hobart in 1902 is
the heaviest biff with the bat registered in Aus-
tralia. Also Tasmania fell for the lowest inter-
colonial score (18) against Victoria in ’69. The
sheaf of records against Tasmania includes the first
century in inter-State cricket, made by Jim Phillips
(Vic.) in ’69; while G. Elliott’s (Vic.) nine for
two in ’58 and Sam Cosstick’s (Vic.) six for one
are the best intercolonial averages, and both were
inflicted on the little Island. Possibly because Tassy
doesn’t arise and awake, the Australian Board of
Control refuses to give it any Sheffield Shield
matches.
* * * *
J. Gray, of Sydenham (M.L.), who at
Christchurch recently smote 343 of a total of 597
for four wickets against Christ’s College, put a new
grade record on the slate. H. B. Lusk’s 334 for
West Christchurch in 1914-’15 was the previous best.
Gray’s figures, however, do not wreck the M.L. record
of 354 perpetrated by J. N. Crawford, one time of
Adelaide.
* * * *
When Keith Kirkland cleaned up the slate
at N.S.W. High School swimming championships
recently, the lad on his heels was S. Willsher, of
Sydney High School. Young Willsher foolishly set
out to pick up the programme, and finished second
in the 50yds., 100yds., 220yds., and third in the
440yds., 880yds. and diving. Every youngster badly
needs a solemn warning against cultivating such ah
appetite. In club matches Willsher decorates the
scratch-mark, and the other day won a 68$yds.
handicap in 38sec. With a couple of years added
to his 16, he is likely to be one of the State’s best.
“Murkah” : Re time diving (B. 6/12/T7).
This seems to be a test where poor physical condition
is an asset. When I was the round-shouldered owner
of a 33in. chest with a half-inch or so expansion, I
was locally invincible at this submarine foolishness.
In contests on the Goulburn River (Vic.) and in
Stubb s Baths, South Melbourne, I was never ex-
tended. The reason, as a doctor explained it to me,
was low vitality—that, though I had no capacity for
air, on account of my general miserableness I didn’t
require much. He also promised me faithfully that
if I didn’t change my job (telegraph-operating) I
would end up with consumption. 1 swapped the tele-
graphing for five years in the bush. There, with the
development of a 38in. barrel, I lost most of my
ability at the diving business. “Tam O’Shanter’s"
friend’s 3imin. performance with only a 36in. chest
would seem to indicate something of the same nature.
* * * *
N.S.W. Education Department arranged
last week to have 5090 children schooled in swim-
ming. Weather conditions were favorable to the
venture, and made a wholly enjoyable outing for
the scholars and 60 teachers. Fifteen enclosures about
Sydney were commandeered for the biggest scheme of
the sort yet launched in Australia. In charge of the
proceedings were two of Australia’s best-known enthu-
siasts—Walter E. Betbel and Miss Ella Gormley.
Bethel, a sprite of 54, has been pushing amateur
swimming for about a third of a century, and is
remembered as one of the few who stuck to Barnev
Kieran when that great youngster was seeking world’s
championships. When not in bathing costume, Bethel
is chief clerk in the Education Department. Miss
Gormley’s job is to put 4000 children a week through
the water and teach each one how to kick. A great
scheme and well worth while.

Jess Willard’s proclamation that he is
willing to defend the heavy-weight championship
against anyone on earth, provided the whole of the
cash on the premises goes to the Red Cross, sounds
as if the vast cowboy was more anxious to keep the
advertising department supplied than provoke attack.
Since tearing the halo from the murky brow of
Brother Johnson, the big chap has had' one fight
and annexed a fortune by exhibiting himself. Per-
haps the last thing in the world he wants is another
disturbance. Anyway, the invitation to engage in
biff for nix is not likely to create much enthusiasm
just now among American pugs, for the money is
evaporating out of the profession as quickly as the
beer out of a dry State.

“J.L.H.”: The platypus must be firs
cousin to the bulldog, if ability to scrap is anv indi
cation. The other night I was fishing in the' Sout
Esk (Tas.), and heard a tremendous splashing anc
churning in the water. I was quite at a loss t(
account for it at first. Closer investigation provet
it to be a couple of large-sized platypi engaged ii
an all-in mix-up. They floated down the stream
quite oblivious to surroundings, or the sticks anc
stones with which I pelted them; I even managed tc
get close enough to prod them with a pole, but they
refused to be separated, and after making the serai
hot and strong for about half an hour they sank
slowly beneath the surface, still locked in a deadh
grip.
* * * *
“Geo. L. Hobson”: I think “Rio” (B.
22/11/’l7) is overshooting the mark a bit when he
says .303 military cartridges cost 255. per 100 to
produce. That is about the price a profit-grabbing
gunsmith will charge you for them. Before the war
the empty shells were worth about 7d. per lb. in
Sydney. Twelve months after the war started sd.
was all I could get for them. At the present time
you can buy 35 calibre automatic rifle cartridges
for 20s. per 100 in Sydney; they are nickel bullets
with a lead point, made in America, and Heaven
knows what percentage of profit the dealers make out
of them, but it must be considerable. I agree that
the waste of ammunition that goes on at Randwick
and other ranges is tremendous. Men shoot there
every Saturday year after year, and what for? As
a sport, no doubt, it is fascinating. Target-shooting
teaches a man to load and fire quickly; but it cannot
teach him to shoot accurately in the field. Once a
target-puncher gets off the grass mound and has to
shoot without his sling, he is all at sea. In the
field, with a military rifle, at unknown ranges, with-
out the use of a sling and the usual two sighting
shots, the best rifle-club man is simply lost by the
wallaby and kangaroo hunters, who use no artificial
aids.
* * * *
“Billy T.” ; They were repairing the road
under my window, and using a long, heavy, wire-
covered hose which, when not in use, was rolled on
a large, spindle-like contrivance on two wheels.
This quaint vehicle was standing on the edge of the
footpath when Sylvester Well came up and collided
with it. In his sober moments my friend is an
excellent lawyer, but he has not been following the
King of late. When he bumped the hose-reel he
first apologised, hat in hand, and then manifested an
expert’s interest in the mechanism of the thing. He
looked the contrivance over carefully. He was pleased
with it, and, being pleased, decided to give it an
unprejudiced trial, with a view to purchase. He
passed one long leg over the drum, and —“Be seated,
gentlemen.” The drum revolved, and Well went
over to the left, and fell off into his hat. Now,
Well is not a man easily put down (morally),
especially when in drink. He arose with an air of
great determination, and mounted again. Again the
drum revolved, and again Sylvester fell off, once
more into his hat. His hat was ruined, his watch
was in the gutter, silver was scattered on the foot-
path ; but he was going to see this thing through.
He rushed the reel, literally flung himself at it, and
the drum, revolving rapidly, shot him along the side
walk. Hurrying to the rescue, I found him involved
with a corporation laborer. I extracted my friend.
He was damaged, but his composure was wonderful.
He gave half a crown to the laborer. “Now,” he
said, “since you're ho smart, show us how you do
ride the damn thing!”

began with a deficit of £l7B, preliminary
expenses:—•

The co. runs a cheap lodging-house on
sanitary principles; and probably because so
many young men have gone to the war, while
those who haven’t have been enjoying the
high wages that have been produced by war
expenditure, the takings have been steadily
shrinking—lols, £5722; 1916, £5397; 1917,
£5253. In the past year, moreover, there
have been exceptional payments for mainte-
nance (some of these might properly be
charged to capital account). Hence the
reduced net profits. However, the co. can’t
complain that the bread of its philanthropy
cast upon the waters hasn’t come back to it.
While paying the comfortable dividend the
co.’s liabilities have been reduced as fol-
lows :—•

The capital at present consists of £64,270
in permanent £5 shares and £4670 in invest-
ing £2O shares; and the withdrawal of some
of the latter" capital explains the reduction
in dividend during the last couple of years.
The Langridge has one of the most honor-
able records among Melbourne financial insti-
tutions ; not only was no depositor ever
required to take less than 20s. in the £, even
after the general smash among building soci-
eties 24 years ago, but the present dividend
is on the original capital. Further, the soci-
ety is still run on lines which make deposits
absolutely secure. Look at this:—
Deposits, Fore- Other
etc. Advances, closures. Assets.
1905. . £12,229 £59,172 £54,245 £3678
1910.. 21,351 96,143 24,997 Nil
1915.. 21,402 103.022 3,962 9139
1916.. 20,229 110,235 3,929 1012
1917.. 18,987 108,332 3,929 1839
Deduct liquid assets from the amount
owing by the society, and the remaining
liabilities are covered seven times over by
other assets. Certainly practically all those
other assets are advances; but as they have
been made under building-society tables,
which means a steady reduction of the
amount advanced and a consequent improve-
ment in the security, and as there are obvi-
ously few new advances, the risk is com-
paratively very small. It is a little remark-
able that the co. can pay an 8 p.c. div. so
comfortably in the circumstances. It says
a lot for the society’s management and its
goodwill. What there is in the years ahead
depends very, largely, not only upon the
rural industries, but upon the vigor with
which new industries are maintained and
established through an effective tariff. That
will do a lot to save the situation for these
building societies. If there isn't this indus-
trial activity, then there will be a slack time
for them, though there is pretty sure to be
use for their own free capital at good rates.
* * * It
“Platman”: Treasurer Theodore gave as
a reason for relieving the Chillagoe deben-
ture-holders of their railway that “the dis-
trict hadn’t been scratched.” Well, Mr.
Theodore’s own experience of that district
dates back to about 1907, and 25 years be-
fore that there were some of the finest pro-
spectors in Australia over it. The tin
country about Lancewood was found nearly
40 years ago. and has been worked ever
since. Old Dargalong, a silver-lead patch,
has been abandoned over 25 years; the
galena turned to zinc. The New Dargalong,
a lode that for length and regularity is sec-
ond to few in Queensland or perhaps Aus-
tralia, got a good trial many years ago. It
runs for some five miles, and "has, I should
think, about 50 shafts on it. some down to
a depth of 80 feet; but none of them struck
payable ore, beyond a few tons of picked
stuff. About two years ago I was taken
on a wild-goose chase to that lode and gave
it a fair sampling, with a result that showed
it was valueless. Of course it would be
foolish .to say that nothing new will be
found in the Chillagoe district, but it’s a
safe bet that whatever is found won’t be
sticking up over the surface, and the pick-
and-shovel prospector has to get some
encouragement at the grass roots before he
takes on blind stabbing for lodes There
may be one reason for the purchase of the
railway, but it hasn’t been spoken of so far
and that is the connection with the Croydon
line. There are over 400.000 head of cattle
m and about the country that such a line
would run through, and it might mean a big
help to small holders to go in for cattle-rear
mg; besides which there is very good
land on the Gilbert suitable for agriculture
I>ut as for the district not being scratched,
there are the remains of too manv dead town-
ships too many rotten poppet-legs, too manv
abandoned shafts for anyone to take anv
serious notice of that statement, even though
it be made by the Treasurer of Queensland

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character') will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned

F.T.W.: Much obliged, and same to you.
During the past three months the B.’s prose yarns
were written by 18 different people, of whom three
were new men—which, on the whole, isn’t a bad
proportion. We are looking for them all the time.
....Worker: Glad to be informed that The Bulle-
tin has fallen into the hands of the Ilughes-Cook
party. No doubt this accounts for the profound
respect it has been showing Hughes and Cook lately.
....Narked: Thanks: but the awful Meiklyjohn’s
“New Geography” (1910) has already hid attention.
Is it still in Australian use ? If so, where 1.... Murkah:
Right... .Ed. S.: Keep going. .. .R.R.: That dyna-
mite incident happened to you, did it? Methuselah,
it’s time that you were permanently dead... .Jack-
pot: Don’t know the contributor to the Adelong
Argus who signs himself “Jimmy Pannican.” Any-
way, he’s not “Jimmie Pannikin” —not by paddocks.
• N .. .C.H.S.: See recent back issues; fully explained
how to deal with the pest.... Tasman: This paper’s
thirst for paragraphs is never satisfied. But the
sample to hand seems to deal with a personal
grievance, and that’s not the sort we want....
Bellerive: That’s the way to deal with them: —
A ghastly pallor spread o’er the face
Of a treacherous spy of war,
Sentenced to be shot by a British squad
After a trial by court-martial law;
A gallant force the miscreant betrayed
To the staff of the crafty Huns,
Their position behind a sheltered hill
To their number of men and guns;
At the hour of sunrise in the woodland,
A volley of rifles did boom —-
Vengeance was wreaked on the traitor,
He’d met a justified doom.
. ...G.H.: Seemed to us that there was a lot in
the Defence Department’s contention that a soldier’s
military record gave no indication of what a man
was likely to do in civil employ. What’s the
answer to that, if there is one 1... . N.M.: Nothing
exciting about that... .P.F.H.: Have it your own
way; but it's a bumpy way over an intellectual
desert... .W.J.G.: No mirth in it,.. .H.8.J.: Poor,
unrhymed stuff which jars the ear all the way,
says only the old things badly, and can’t even keep
true to its own scansion. .. .D. 11.: Sorrowful frag-
ment. Can’t “Australia” rouse something better than
that?. .. .E.E.S.: The pathos of it moved the
Religious Editor to beers: —
I wandered on for miles and more,
And then I came to a cottage door.
And there before a dreary fire
A sad-faced woman wept.
I looked upon the sad, sad scene,
The saddest I had ever seen,
For was she not my own dear wife
Whom I had left in pain and strife?
Then as I silently upon her gazed,
She turned and looked up in amaze,
And with one low cry of pain,
She fell into my arms—for ever to remain.

In a not very old Tasmanian pension-list
“Solomon Blay, public executioner,” is down
for £2O. Solomon Blay sent more men into
Eternity than did Botany Bay’s very own
“Nosey Bob,” whose record was 75, or there-
abouts.

RESTORES and INVIGORATES.
It builds up tissue and is convertible into fat and heat. Maximum results are obtained at minimum cost by the use of
MEGGITT’S LINSEED OIL MEAL because it is all food and easily assimilated.
Analyses show that LINSEED OIL MEAL contains more protein than other food, which makes it especially invaluable to
draught horses. If you expect the best from your horses —give them the best food obtainable.
Mr. PETER L. TIGIIE, Ferodale, Raymond Terrace, says:—“You seldom, if ever, require medicine for your stock if
you use Linseed Oil Meal. If you want a clean skin and glossy coat on your horses give them Linseed Oil JVleal.”
HOW TO FEED MEGGITT’S LINSEED OIL MEAL TO HORSES.
Commence lightly, the Meal is very rich, and you must get your horse used to it. Small handful each feed for the
first week; after, increase to from 2 to 4 pounds per day, according to the work your horse is doing.

First Australian theatres: Sideaway’s,
which stood in the neighborhood of Mar-
garet-street, Sydney, 1796; Theatre Royal,
Royal Hotel, Barnet Levey’s, 1833; The
Victoria, Joseph Wyatt’s, .1830-1 (it stood
where McCathies Ltd. now sells fluff in
Pitt-street) ; The Llobart Town Theatre,
1840; Launceston, 1843; Queen’s Theatre,
Melbourne, 1845; Adelaide, 1846; Theatre
Royal, Melbourne, 1855; Olympic, 1855
(this was in Lonsdale-street east). There
was also a “gaff” in Bourke-street, near the
Bull and Mouth site, in the first ’forties, but
it did not count for much.
From a Bulletin Sundry-Show-man ;
I want to wish Florence Young a happy New
Year. One of my most vivid small-boy recollec-
tions is of watching poor Violet Varley’s flower-laden
hearse pass Melbourne Princess’s, where the little
actress had been idolised. Good-humored Florence,
belongs to that period ; yet here she is still leading
brilliantly, still whipping up a flagging stage, still
throwing her song-words to the remotest seats of
the biggest J.C.W. theatre! I heard her the other
night after I had been listening to a lot of other
people, and was startled by the bell-like purity of
her high notes, and the fact that you knew all the
time what she was singing about. In the midst of
much vocal fog an interpolated line froip Florence
comes across like a flash from an occulting light.
And she never makes faces while she is doing it.
Some singing actresses look as if they were about
to take a mouthful of spaghetti, or yell “Fire!” or
have a tooth out. This buxom Australian never does.
May her shadow never grow less —unless she wants
it to.

Charlie Tait, writing from California,
gives a new testimonial to our Allies :
I have found courtesy in every walk of life. This
has been most marked. Never once have 1 heard a
boorish word, nor noticed any incivility on tram,
train, street or caf£. Of course, one is always
spending money, but that happens to the traveller in
any country. Tipping runs to about 10 per cent, of
your bill. Soldiers! These surprised me. They are
a fine lot of boys, free from skite. They seem a
serious-minded lot of chaps—hold themselves like
men and act like men. If the rest of them are as
good as the Californians —-well, the Herr Gott Kaiser
will have cause to remember them.
“C. Ockney” : It seems strange nowadays
to come across the names of real live authors
on a pantomime bill, but the programme of
Drury Lane’s latest annual (“Aladdin”' in-
dicates a return to old conditions. The
writers are F. Anstey, Frank Dix and
Arthur Collins, the first-named being the
old-established Punch humorist, who in
addition has written many bright farces —
“Vice Versa’’ and “Man from Blankley’s”
are two of the best known. The last
Anstey whimsicality seen in Australia was
the tricky “Brass Bottle,” out of which not
much brass was made. Aladdin, by the
way, will be that clever Australian, Madge
Titheradge; and the Widow Twankey will
be Stanley Lupino, a brother of Barry, who
is the magician in the Bailey-Tait show on
the same subject.
“Cecil M. B.”: I went to see lan Mac-
laren in “David Garrick,” and came away
wondering why those old-timers didn’t warn
us about what was coming. On Mr. Ingot’s
table were copies of London Graphic and
The Great War.
“J.F.H.” (London) : The long innings of
revue is coming to an end at last, and,
thank Heaven! most theatre-goers say.
With hardly an exception the variety houses
are reverting to the old turns, or, as in the
case of the Palace, the Oxford and the Pavilion,
devoting the whole evening to dramatic fare.
The brief career of “Airs and Graces,” at
the Palace, in spite of such a reigning
favorite as Gertie Miller, convinced Alfred
Butt that the public was fed up with revue
even of the most gorgeous, expensively-
dressed and beauty-chorused description.
For his Christmas attraction at the Palace
he is therefore going to present a new musi-
cal comedy, and has persuaded one-time pro-
fessional beauty Lily Elsie, who married a
Caledonian millionaire, to emerge and appear
in the leading role. G. P. Huntley will also
be in the cast.
Gaby Deslys, the French damsel with the
scanty wearing apparel and the astonishing
headgear, threatens to pay her “first pro-
fessional visit to Australia” when the war
is over.
Stalls of Her Majesty's, Sydney, at “The
Bing Boys ” Rehearsal.
Producer Jack Haskell demonstrating a step
for the legs of the chorus.
“0.K.”: “II.O.” asks, “Whence the con-
vention that Hamlet should be a slim, god-
looking Dane?” That’s easy. Ophelia
deplores the mental breakdown of a scholarly
courtier who has hitherto been “the glass of
fashion and the mould of form, the observed
of all observers.” It has suited an occa-
sional plump star of burly proportions to
argue that Hamlet may be presented as a
thickset fellow of 35. George C. Miln,
rather short, heavy and middle-aged, pleaded
justification for his stage picture, but the
evidence of the text was against him. The
gone-mad Prince, according to the Grave-
digger. was a man of 30 at the time of
Ophelia’s burial. Yorick, dead 23 years,
had borne him on his back a thousand times.
The gravedigger had held his billet, man
and boy, for 30 years; and he came to it
on the very day when young Hamlet was
born. As for his reference to his “too solid
flesh.” it would apply under the circs, to
the flesh of the leanest of morbid mourners.
The only sound argument in favor of a
fleshy Hamlet is Gertrude’s remark during
a pause in the fencing test between Hamlet
and Laertes: “Our son is fat and scant of
breath. Here, take my napkin, rub thy
brows.” But Gertrude may be assumed to
speak as a sport, who observes that the
matured athlete is a trifle out of condition
after his recent holiday-trip to England. He
would be comparatively fat and scant of
breath if he were only 101 b. above his nor-
mal fighting weight, and this comes to him
in the last two acts only. No, Hamlet is
understood to be a handsome, active, shapely-
expert in fence. He talks of his sinews,
and some words spoken by his father s spook
imply that the young man is spry and mus-
cular.
Leonard Willey, the Australian actor, and
his wife, Irby Marshall, of Queensland, both
well known for their good work in the
LEONARD WILLEY, Australian actor, as
Allen Doone’s villain in New York.
Williamson dramatic cos. of a few years
back, have been doing excellently in N’York.
Margaret Anglin during her tour here
admired the work of Willey, and when he
arrived in America three years ago she
engaged him as leading man for “Lady
Windermere’s Fan.” Next, he was starred
with his wife in Shakspearean plays, making
a big success in Boston with “The Taming
of the Shrew.” Willey then joined the Punch
and Judy Theatre in N’York for a most
interesting production of a boy’s idea of
“Treasure Island.” The pirates were pirates
with tremendous buckles, swords, slashed
faces and fierce beards, and other pirates
who carried green parrots and threw bags of
gold about. Willey was the juvenile lead
playing Captain Smollet, and Willey, junior,
claims that it was the best thing Pa has ever
done. From “Treasure Island” the Aus-
tralian went to Greek plays under Granville
Barker, and after another interesting season
he was with John Drew. Then he joined
Cyril Maude’s company, playing Jarvis in
“Grumpy.” Lately he was appearing as
“Stunning Warrington,” with Drew in
“Pendennis” on Broadway, and before that
was the villain w T hom Allen Doone thwarted
in “Lucky O’Shea” at New York.
Phil Finkelstein, who has issued the
Firm’s communiques in Melbourne for some
years, has been promoted to the business
manager’s beat in front of Her Majesty’s.
Harry Musgrove, who was Phil’s predecessor
in the job, has been fitted into a berth at
headquarters in Sydney.
Said now that Herbert Tree’s death in a
London nursing home was due to unnatural
causes.
Lydia Yeamans Titus, Australia’s old
friend of the baby imitations, is here again.
She comes in a “Butterfly” 5-reeler as an
old Irish lady bent on rushing her lovely
daughter and her husband’s dollars into the
hands of the frequent foreign-Count chap.
There is the usual forcefulness about Lydia’s
work, which amounts to a careful character-
study.
“M.P.A.” : Some P.P. contributor told us
a few weeks back that Geraldine Ferrar, the
prima donna of the Metrop. Opera House.
N. York, had gone into pictures. Quite 12
months ago writer dragged The Bulletin’s
most blas@ Sundry Show-man to view Gerald-
ine in “Maria Rosa,” a Spanish story which
gave the star fine scope for her temperamen-
tal acting. We agreed that it w T as worth the
admission-money to see Wallace Reid hold
Geraldine in a passionate half-Nelson.
“Private Boxer”: E. J. Tait, explaining
things to the New York Dramatic Mirror:
Australia must depend upon America for its
theatrical companies, as very few of my countrymen
choose the stage as a profession.
Which, of course, makes it quite clear why
so many of Edward’s countrymen have gone
to America to get a job.

“Jwosp”: N’Yark has a negro stock co
which has been doing excellent work in a
range of plays from “Charley’s Aunt” to
Shakspeare. Two years ago, when the first
all-black co. played “Othello,” there were
ribald outbursts, but the scoffers closed their
faces when Herbert Tree congratulated the
players on their Shakspearean stuff. The
scenery was not top-notch, but the acting
and dressing were spoken of in the highest
terms. The management is going in exten-
sively next year for plays by negro authors.
From the other side :
Dion Titheradge is a lieutenant and Claude Beer-
bolim is in the Canadian Field Artillery. Maori-
lander Jerome Patrick has climbed into the best
color, and is with the Officers’ Training Corps in
Canada, where he has Kenneth Hunter, who used
to be with Geach and latterly was in Fox films, as
a camp chum.
Henry Arthur Jones’s 20-year-old comedy,
“The Liars,” a favorite in Australia in the
days of Brough and Boucicault, has been
revived at the St. James’s, London, to very
affable business. If war kills, it also resur-
rects.

SONGANDANCE OF THE AGES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Adam did his songandance
And Eden was extremely gay,
No wowser had a sporting chance—
That was before the wowser’s day.
In came the Serpent ripe for fun,
And Adam’s songandance was done.
The turns then followed thick and fast,
For with the Fall the whole earth stirred :
Each loud performer hoped to last,
But kings and nations “got the bird.”
Each found that ere he’d well begun
He and his songandance were done.
The sea’s green drums that never cease,
The wind’s wild pipes that play and play
Make nfusic when we long for peace,
And dance the struggling shapes away.
“Next turn! Get off!” the prompter sings.
“The Future’s waiting in the wings!”.
With shuffling feet and backward glance,
While sea-pipes shrill and sea-drums roar,
We hear, tip-tapping on the floor,
Young feet impatient for their dance.
x.s.w.
E. O’FERRALL.
A STAR AT LAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Busk, the well-known actor, has enlisted.”
He has the part. On playing it intent,
The odds are that he'll make a biggish hit.
He signed up for the run, then happy went
To see about his make-up and his kit.
1.0 ! Everything was “found,” and ne'er a cent
Had he to part. He’d jumped from walking gent.
To leading man, a star in the legit.!
No imitation props! Make no mistake,
His togs down to a button are correct;
His rifle and his ba; <>">'t no fake—-
They’re meant for ! tiling, not for stage effect;
To pot the villain ev'iy time and break
His power for evil. What applause will shake
The playhouse when his wild career is cheeked!
“Lines” are but few, but “business” takes up time,
And “rotten”-perfect in it he must be
Before he goes on ; though there’ll be no lime,
His acting ail the world will plainly see.
A bigger chance ne’er comes to any mime—
The company supporting him how prime,
The theatre one where he’ll make history!
The play is “War”; khaki’s the only wear!
A patriot each member of the cast.
How strange to find that jealousy is rare,
The centre-of-tlie-stage idea is past!
His comrades’ luck only too proud to share,
To martial music, head up in the air,
He steps and knows he’s got the part at last!
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
ny Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous liair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For this
purpose pure powdered plieminol mav be
applied directly to the objectionable hair-
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair, but to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Removing Her Skin.
The quickest and surest way to make a
good complexion out of a bad one is to
actually remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can
be done easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion.
Get some pure mercolised wax from your
chemist and apply it at night like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few
days the mercolide in the wax will have
absorbed the disfiguring cuticle, revealing
the fresh young skin underneath; you will
have a beautiful, clear, natural complexion.
The process is pleasant and harmless, making
the face look brilliantly attractive and youth-
ful. It also effectually removes such blem-
ishes as freckles, tan. moth patches, liver
spots, pimples, etc. Every woman should
keep a little mercolised wax on hand, as this
simple, old-fashioned remedy is the best com-
plexioned beautifier and preserver known.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the
skin. You have only to- drop a tablet of
stymol, obtained from the chemist, into a
glass of hot water and bathe the face with
the liquid after the effervescence has sub-
sided. The blackheads will then come right
off on the towel. The enlarged pores immedi-
ately contract to normal and the greasiness
disappears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and
cool and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent it is
advisable _to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need, not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily be
changed back to a natural color in a few
days time merely by the application of a
simple, old-fashioned and perfectly harmless
home-made lotion. Procure from your chem-
ist two ounces of tammalite concentrate and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this to the hair a few times with a smail
sponge, and you will soon have the pleasure
of seeing your grey hair gradually darkening
to the desired shade. The lotion is pleasant,
not sticky or greasy, and does not injure the
liair in any way.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
v stallax not only makes the best possi-
ble shampoo, but has the peculiar property of
leaving the hair wjth a pronounced natural
wave, an effect presumably desired by many
ladies. A tcaspoonf.ul of the stallax granules,
dissolved in a cup of hot water, is ample for
a luxurious head wash, and leaves the hair
with a jpeculiar lustre and fluffiness obtainable
from nothing else as far as known. It is per-
fectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been but
little used for this purpose it comes only in
ilb. sealed original packages, enough to make
up 25 or 30 individual shampoos. [2

A new tipple consisting of a mixture of
cordite and gun-oil was invented a while ago
by a thirsty gunner at the Front whose
scientific knowledge told him that there was
alcohol in explosives. His example isn'r
likely to be followed by anybody who knew
what the recovery was like.
“S.” : In proof of the fact that the bush-
man likes his pill with plenty of venom in it,
here’s a testimonial received from Billjim

for insurrecting debilitated livers. Billjim

The latest D.S.O. is a Victorian Light
Horseman, Lieut.-Col. M. \V. ,7. Bourchier,
whose people live at Strathmerton (Vic.).
Born in 1881, he swapped a lieutenancy in
the local Light Horse for similar rank in
the A.1.F., and after some strenuous stunts
in Gallipoli, Egypt and Palestine, secured
two “mentions,” three promotions and his
present honor.
Ned Stanley, who some 50 years ago was
known as one of the dandiest roughriders
in the Victorian police force, had eight sons,
seven of them over six feet and scaling from
12 to 17 stone, and the eighth was so near
the limit that the difference didn’t matter.
The seven are still alive and hearty, with
the exception of one who was accidentally
killed in the Rockies about 15 years ago.
“Murrumbidgee, V.C.” : After church par-
ade tin l C. of E, padre said, “I wish to speak
to those who have not been confirmed. Will
the men divide themselves into two parties,
please? Those who have fall out on the
right and those who have not on the left.”
Most of us fell out on the right. In the
I heard this: “You been confirmed.
Bill' “Bet yer life! Got the marks on me
arm yet.”
‘O. Longlook”: Lovely woman must be
wearing a lot of scrumptious lingerie these
war-vexed days if the signs and portents
mean anything. One Collins-street shop
gives a daily display in a special island
window, showing a different nightie every
time, flanked by an assortment of etc., etc.,
all in China silks of the most alluring tints.
Another indication of what is going on
may be observed in the frequency of raffle-
tickets for all manner of intimate items in
champagne, sweet-briar, pale-blue and lilac.
Black lady-pyjamas, also in China silk, are
regarded as very—but, there! Most of ’em
will be back to pink flannelette again before
they are five years older.
A. T. Saunders, who spends half his time
digging up old facts relative to S. Aus.,
declares that neither Randall nor Cadell
can, as usually supposed, be claimed to have
first navigated the waters of the Murray.
Sturt of course discovered it, rowing down
fiom the Darling. But after him came a
German named Gerstacker, who in May.
1851, canoed himself down from Albury
into S. Aus., thus (incidentally) anticipat-
the “River Rovers” bv
half a century or so. Brady, by the way, is
in these days pioneering the remotest part
or the Gippsland forest, almost opposite
where the Cumberland was recentlv mined,
pome day he hopes to be nearly as well
known for his pigs as his poems.
D.M.F.”: Federal Government Depart-
ments, whose heads are continually platitu-
dinising to the public about economy, don’t
know what the word means. Here is an
example. One Department sends over 200
telegrams daily, each containing about 30
words. Por each message a sheet of fine
foolscap bearing a variety of official head-
ings and signatures is used. Except for
about three words all the messages are iden-
tical, and about 50 are sent to each of five
addresses. By coding, the whole wmuld be
contamed in five 200-w r ord messages, one to
each btate, where now over 6000 words are
transmitted. The characteristically dull
departmental explanation is. “Oh. these
messages cost us nothing.” But they cost,
the originating department the time and
stationery, and load the wires with 5000
unnecessary words.

HIGH day and holiday shoes, for wear on
extra-special occasions with the extra-
special gown, ought, one feels, to have
something distinctive about them, some-
thing that lifts them above the shoes for every-
day wear and tear. It is this distinctive, this
different look, that makes the charm of the most
elegant shoes in the Bostock range, the delightful
little black satin shoe here illustrated, for ex-
ample. One has only to glance at the picture to see
that here is something very smart and desirable.
Then think of it on, showing daintily, captivat-
ingly, beneath the flounced or scalloped hem of
the summer gown. Nothing could be more appro-
priate or more charming. And -with all its
daintiness and piquancy, it is the most comfortable
and well-fitting of shoes, as indeed are all those
short-fronted styles, and will suit most women,
giving that ease, lightness and coolness which the
Australian climate requires.

The Inky Way.
“A.H.A.”: One Australian, at least, owes
deep thanks to Dr. Morrison, nowadays the
Political Adviser to the Government of
China, and at home on six months’
leave. This Australian was a war-corres-
pondent for M.L. papers during the Boxer
Rebellion. He took the precaution to get
a letter of introduction from the M.L. Gov-
ernor, Ranfurly, to a gubernatorial cobber
of his at Hongkong. This genial Excellency
armed him with a letter of introduction to
Sir Claude Macdonald, British Ambassador
at Peking. When he had managed to reach
Peking, per transport, French gunboat,
Chinese wheelbarrow, river sampan, boot
and mule, he looked everywhere for a lodg-
ing. There wasn’t a hotel habitable in
the shelled city, and he knew no one in
Peking. Then he remembered his letter of
introduction, and made his way to the Brit-
ish Legation. He butted in, a painfully
shabby object, at afternoon tea—the after-
noon tea that ladies will insist on having on
Judgment Day. He wasn’t welcomed effu-
sively. The Ambassador’s wife seemed to
think that there were too many correspon-
dents about; she was quite bothered with
them —and they all brought letters of intro-
duction. Also, there wasn’t an inch of
accommodation left in the Legation. Surely
he knew someone in Peking? A memory of
Dr. Morrison came up, and the lady politely
lent the correspondent a “boy” to guide him.
Xow, this correspondent had never met Dr.
Morrison ; but by chance it was Dr. Morrison
whom lie encountered at his own door.
“Hullo!” he said, “what can I do for you?”
The correspondent answered, “I’m an ' Aus-
tralian !” The word was enough. “Come
in !” Morrison said to the battered and sus-
picious-looking object on the Chinese mule.
“You’d better stay with me till you find
somewhere else to go.” And the correspon-
dent did, for some weeks.
“B.B.”: ' Humorous dog, my friend
Blanker, on the reporting string of the
Sydney Evening Snaps ! He was due for
an afternoon off yesterday, and I was to
take over his work while attending to my
own. This is how he broke the news to me:
Dear Bill,—l have not left you much. There is a
verdict to get in murder trial at Central Criminal
Court, and there will he sentences (only) at the
Quarter Sessions. It will be necessary to keep an
eye on the High Court in case some rush applica-
tion is made by the Ryans or the Cattses. You
might_ drop into Hevdon’s Court, also, Rolin’s; but
I don't think you’ll get more than a column from
either. There are a couple of cases coming on in
the District Court, and there may be some Quarter
Sessions appeals. You won’t get much else. If
you find time hanging heavy you can do a week-end
special. But I leave that to yourself. It is a fine
afternoon for a swim.
It was! I took the first boat to Manly.
* * * *
The Ovens and Murray Advertiser,
which enlightens Beecliworth (Vic.) two or
three times a week, is the oldest country
paper in Vic. outside Geelong, Ballarat and
Bendigo. It has been emerging for 62 years,
ever since January, 1855, and has been
owned by the one family all the time. Mrs.
Warren, widow of the original founder,
owned it up to the time of her death, which
took place the other day.
Will Hope, a Maoriland ai’tist who went
to the U.S.A. a couple of years back and
fractured Uncle Sam’s neutrality with pro-
Ally cartoons, recently put away his pencil
and left for active service in England. At
latest Hope was taking his old cobbers,
Faith and Charity, for aeroplane trips over
the German lines in France.
Here’s a neat heading. “Wake up ! Your
country needs you!” has been written on a
gatepost at the entrance to the Beaufort
(Vic.) Cemetery. Melbourne Herald calls
it “Playing the Last Trump.”

THE UNAPPRECIATIVE ONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poet all a breezy day
Sang down the long, rich sweep of hill
To where the creek in wanton play
Gave echo to his happy trill.
He sang of joyousness and love
To liehened rock and twinkling tree,
The green below, the blue above,
And Nature answered him with glee,
The red bird and the golden bee.
“The whistling song is good, is good,”
The parrot said, and caught the tune;
The bugling magpie understood,
The wind took up the singer’s rune,
And carried it among the boughs ;
The sly, brown jester laughed in long
Approval, and the placid cows
Were pleased to hear the minstrel’s song;
The keets responded in a throng.
The poet let his hair blow free,
He drew creation at his heels,
Felt lilt of Spring, and stir of sea ;
He gave no craven thought to meals, *
But Pan-like ruled, and softly smiled
To hear the crows’ delighted taws,
And see the creatures of the wild,
With little beaks and ready claws
Bestowing well-deserved applause.
Alas! that joy may not abide!
The poet wears an ugly scowl.
The song that won a countryside,
Charmed snake and kangaroo and owl,
He did up in a packet neat,
And posted townward o’er and o'er,
To find success was incomplete.—•
The ravished things of sea and shore
Did not include an editor!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The Happy Tower.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HEAD a tale of a mighty tower
On the shores of a northern sea,
Where the mourning wind came wild
and wet
And muttered hatred and shrieked regret;
But that is no tower for me,
With the long grey line of the wintry foam,
And the spars of a ship that drifted home.
The tower I build is a happy tower ;
It is slender, strong and high,
And through its windows your eyes may see
The wing of a bird float glossily
Or nothing at all but sky,
The delicate jest in the dawning’s smile
Or the pale reflex of a sunlit mile.
It is not so far that, on summer nights
Should I from the window lean,
I could not smell through the dusky air
The breath of a thousand blossoms rare
That come from the gardens green;
To hark to the fret of a sun-sick rose
Or guess the joy that the young grass knows.
It is not so low that the prying eyes
Of the small earth-people can look within;
It is far too lofty for id'e tales.
For whispering corners and sordid vales;
Too high for a sneer to win ;
And the vain earth-contests and foolish lies
Are changed to music before they rise.
So close to the stars that they seem as
friends;
So near to the moon’s white face
That she brushes the ledge with her powdered
bloom,
And writes with her pencils on the gloom
Until, in that quiet place,
She has graven into the soul of me
A silver dream for a legacy.
The tower I build is a happy tower.
It lifts to a cloudless cope,
And into the opal-tinted eve
With eager fingers I sit and weave
A ladder of fairy rope.
Perhaps some day, when the dream comes
true,
I will make that ladder a stair for you!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Mr. Dampier’s Young Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
riIOWARDS the end of August in the
year 1699, Mr. William Dampier-
of Yeovil in Somersetshire, the
Spanish Main and tile China Seas, and now
commanding 11.M.5. Roebuck upon the N.W.
coast of Australia —came ashore in a little
cockle-boat on a sandy beach somewhat
nor’-east of Shark Bay. With him were
two of his crew —the fat boatswain. Richard
Corbin of Deal, and a seemingly hand-
some gunner’s-mate named Jan Pentraeth,
of Falmouth in Cornwall.
There are two versions of the incidents of
that sunny morning in the sand dunes—the
one that Mr. Dampier records in his fasci-
nating “Journal,” and the other that Tan
Pentraeth wrote to his sister, Mistress Mary
Polwarne, the brewer’s wife of St. Ives, and
sent home from Timor in the following
December.
This is what journalist William Dampier
says. He omits a good deal —as do most
great journalists. They had stumbled
across, or been stumbled across by, some
representative Australians of the period :
So a nimble young Man that was with me, seeing
one of them hear, ran towards them; and they for
some time ran away before him. But he soon over-
taking them, they faced about and fought him. He
had a Outlaws and they had wooden Lances; with
which, being many of them, they were too hard
for him. When he fir§i ran towards them I chas’d
two more that were by the shore ; but fearing how
it might be with my young Man, 1 turned back
quickly, and went to the top of a Sandhill, whence
I saw him near me, closely engag’d with them. Upon
their seeing me, one of them threw a Lance at me
that narrowly miss’d me. I discharged my Gun to
scare them; till finding the young Man in great
danger from them and myself in some, and that
though the Gun had a little frightened them at first,
yet they had soon learnt to despise it, tossing up
their Hands, and crying Pooh, pooh, pooh; and
coming on afresh with a great Noise, I thought it
high time to charge again, and shoot one of them,
which I did. The rest, seeing him fall, made a
stand aggin ; and my young Man took the Opportunity
to disengage himself, and come off to me; my
other Man also was with me, who had done nothing
all the while, having come out unarm’d ; and I re-
turned back with my Man, designing to attempt the
natives no farther, being- very sorry for what had
happened already. They took up their wounded
Companion ; and mv young Man, who had been struck
through the Cheek by one of their Lances, was
afraid it had been poison’d. But I did not think
that likely. His Wound was very painful to him,
being made with a blunt Weapon. But lie soon
recovered of it.
That is what Captain Dampier makes of
it. And this is the very incomplete and
somewhat bitter comment of Jan Pentraeth,
in his letter to bis sister :
Ye bloudy-minded pint, hee did design to Maroon
me upon ye barrefi shoars of New Holland if so be
thatte I did not informe him of what ye First Luf-
t’nint did saye toe mee construing his intention to
bring ye Cap’n before . a Coart Marshal when ye
shippe did come agayne to England or mayhap at ye
Cape of Good Hope, for his hard usage of him, Mr.
Fisher, during ye V’yage. Which I w’d in no wise
consent to saye to his satisfying. Wherefore ye
bloudy tyrant give orders to ye bo’s’n, that fat
raskle Dick Cawbin, to row him back to ye shippe
and did present hys piece at my buzzom, crying he
w’d leave me ashore. And I perceving some of ye
Indians approache did damn him for a crewel knave
and did saye I w’d cast in my lot with ye Indians
for that they were whiter Men than was Hee, Will’m
Dampeer, and soe in my Despayre did run toward
them. But they did give me but poor welcome with
Lances, and w’d like to have slayn mee. For hys
shame ye lousy villen could not but come to my
rescue and wee drove ye Indians off, but not before
a Lance had pearced my cheake. And I do solemnly
sware to be even with ye Hound of Hell, Cap’ll Will
Damnecr, and will sneke for Mr. Fisher at ye Coart
Marshal, and if I doe find Cap’ll Damneer ashore in
England will bete him sounly. Soe help me God.
This is what happened.
The commander of the Roebuck’s business
ashore was entirely free, in the first in-
stance, from any design of marooning Jan
Pentraeth. as that hot-headed youth sup-
posed. He merely sought for water.
Jan Pentraeth was a Cornishman, and.
like all Cornishmen, he had a grievance. His
was that he had had no desire whatsoever
to make a voyage to New Holland in H.M.S.
Roebuck, or any other ship. Very naturally.
On the 12th of January, 1699, The Merry
Thought, of Bristol, of which he was second
mate, had anchored in the Downs, after two
years’ voyaging in the West Indies and the
Americas. On the evening of the 13th he
had gone ashore in Deal, had taken his ease
and a considerable quantity of brown Octo-
ber ale—which is strong drink to-day, and
was stronger in Queen Anne’s time—in The
Maid of Kent Tavern, and in the forenoon
of the 14th had staggered up the ladders
from the Roebuck’s lower deck to an aston-
ishing panorama of the white cliffs of Dover,
looming up through the driving grey rain
squalls of an easterly gale, on the starboard
side.
For a full minute Jan Pentraeth had
stared at Dover Castle, high placed on its
green height, as it receded rapidly over the
starboard quarter, and then, with a sense
of injury and wrong, had made his way to
the quarterdeck, where he encountered Dick
Corbin, the boatswain, busily superintending
the stowage of some cabin stores which yet
remained on deck.
“God’s bones!” he exclaimed, “ivhere be
to, ye fat firkin—where be to?”
“New Holland, ye Cornishman; an
ye’re rated A.B. ’Tis not your watch —get
ye below, an’ be d —d to ye !”
Without any explanation further than
this, Mr. Corbin had seized a belaying-pin
from the mizzen-mast and had whacked him
on the head.
Subsequently the captain had merely
smiled at his complaint, and had made him
a gunner’s-matej which was small consola-
tion. Mr. Dampier had himself, with the
aid of his boatswain and some ’longshore-
men, shanghaied Jan Pentraeth. as he lay
like a log at midnight in the ingle nook of
The Maid of Kent. There were few Navy
captains of that time who, being in need of
such a likely seaman, would not have done
the same thing. But neither was that any
consolation to the homesick sailorman, and
his grievance rankled. It rankled bitterly.
The seven-months’ voyage—across to
Brazil and then over an immense waste of
water to the north-west coast of Australia
—had not been a yachting cruise. The
Roebuck was old, unhandy and unseaworth.v,
and was badly found in every respect. It
was the way of My Lords Commissioners
of the Admiralty to send their captains
round the world with the craziest of equip-
ment —they were to treat the great Anson
even more wretchedly in a few years’ time.
And her commander had a temper as vari-
able and unpleasant as that of an equally
famous navigator who came to gnef over
it a century later in 11.M.5. Bounty—
William Bligh. Dampier’s treatment of his
first-lieutenant was so bad that the court-
martial which investigated it in 1702 found
it necessary to “Adjudge that Ilee be Fined
all Ilis Pay to ye Chest at Chatham exclu-
sive of about Fifty Pounds slop'd in ye
Treasury of ye Navies’ Hand for indemnify-
ing the Queen as to a Bill drawn on ye
Navy Board by Lt. Fisher from Lisbon:
And itt is farther the opinion of ye Court
that the said Capt. Dampier is not a Fitt
person to be employ’d as Coindr. of any of
her Ma’ty ships.”
So mutiny smouldered all the voyage, and
not the least mutinously inclined amongst
the crew was that aggrieved seaman out of
Falmouth, Jan Pentraeth.
“ ’Tis as dry and barren-seeming here as
elsewhere, Dick,” resumed Dampier gloomily
as he stared shoreward from the water’s
edge at the white hummocks of the sandhills
glowing in the morning sunlight, with
splashes of green spilt over their snowy
bosoms where the samphire grew. Beyond
their swelling crests was the clear blue of a
cloudless sky, and, through the V-shaped
valleys between, the grey-green tops of
stunted forest-growths appeared. When the
little boat grounded on the beach, and they
stepped ashore, the lonely place was as
silent as an arctic waste. There was nothing
of life but the wheeling seabirds circling
and swooping over the deep blue strip of
ocean behind them. A mile out, the Roe-
buck rolled sluggishly at anchor, the flag of
St. George drooping lazily over the great
stern lantern on her high-built poop. There
was not enough breeze to waft the great
ensign clear of the rail.
That bandy-legged, broad-beamed, paunch-
bellied mariner of East Kent, the boatswain,
spat on the sand disgustedly, as he waddled
in his captain’s wake.
“ ’Tis a poor country, sir, this New
Holland, and like to have no hopes of ever
being any better,” he grunted. “D’ye think
we’ve any chance of finding water here-
about? ’Tis but nine casks left in th’ ship,
an’ that rotten an’ stinldn’.”
“Pigs wouldn’ drink ’en, so be ’twas
swilled in their troughs,” growled Jan Pen-
traeth.
Dampier wheeled on him in a moment. It
was as though the seaman’s muttered better-
ing of Corbin’s complaint was the last thing
the vexed and disappointed leader —scurvy in
the ship, three weeks’ unavailing search for
water on this arid coast, and always the
whimper of mutiny—the last thing that he
would stand. The storm of his strained
temper broke fiercely over the head of Jan
Pentraeth.
“God’s curse on ye, ye craven-hearted
wastrel!” he shouted at the young man, his
usually gentle face convulsed with wrath
and his dark eyes lit with a light of anger.
“Zounds, ye knave! ’Tis nought but a
trough is fit drinking-place for such swine as
you! By God, ’tis enough to spoil the
humor of an angel to look at your sorry
face! I’ve had naught but trouble with ye
since I shipped ye in the Downs!”
“Did I ask ye for to ship me?” the young
man flung at him sullenly.
“Silence, fellow! For Dios. I’ll rid the
ship of ye. ye ill-conditioned dog. I’ll give
ye some of the discipline of the Caribbees,
my man—a taste of the old buccaneer treat-
ment. ’Twill cure your peevish vapors, I’ll
warrant ye. Look ye! If ye like not King
William’s ship Roebuck, ye can take your
bellyful of New Holland. Dick —Dick Cor-
bin,” he called to the boatswain, who was
securing the boat with a small anchor.
“Dick, we leave this fellow, here to-day.
He’ll set no foot aboard my ship again. By

God—we’ll maroon the saucy hound ! I’ve
had enough of his scowling visage to last me
a lifetime. D'ye hear me, bo’s'n?”
“Aye, aye, sir,” growled the old salt, with-
out emotion. “Aye, aye, sir—l’ll send him
his duds ashore when you and I go back
aboard.”
“Maroon me!” gasped the young man,
paling a little. He had come across what
was left of other men who had been
marooned in such spots as this. Stinking
water and bad food in the Roebuck were
better than no water at all in this land of
devils.
, "Ye can’t do it!” he sneered doubtfully.
", Tis a King’s ship. ’Tis agin th’ Articles
o War.”
“Is it, by God? We’ll see! Now do you
walk in front of us whiles we go seek an
Indian to show us where their water is.
Ye’ll render me this last service, my pretty
fellow, before we part.”
He cocked his piece and pointed with its
barrel to the sandhills. Then he covered
Jan’s breast a moment, and lowered the mus-
ketoon again.
“Go !” he said. “Lead the way, ye d—d
rogue or I’ll put a ball into your hog’s body.”
“ ’Twill not alone be Mr. Fisher ye’ll face
the court-martial over, Cap’n Dampier, I tell
’fee sure,” growled Pentraeth, as they walked
up the beach.
“Mr. Fisher! What’s to do with Mr.
Fisher and courts-martial? By God, that
settles me! Ye’ll stay here and rot, ye dog.”
And so they went into the sandhills, and
found the Australians.
But Jan was not to be "the first Cousin
Jack to settle in Westralia. Mr. Dampier’s
rage abated with the shooting of the black-
fellows. Not so the bitter Cornislnnan’s.
* * * *
Interval of-three years.
* * * *
In the twilight of a beautiful June even-
ing in 1702, Mr. William Dampier was
rowed ashore from H.M.S. London, lying at
Spithead, where he had been spending one of
the unpleasantest afternoons of his career.
His mind was possessed by bitter considera-
tions of the injustice of raking up old scores,
the advisability of allowing sleeping dogs to
lie, the vindictiveness of some men, and the
deplorable fact that his career as an officer
in the Royal Navy was abruptly and rudely
terminated. So the court-martial had decreed.
He reflected sadly, as he set foot on Ports-
mouth Hai’d. that his career as a certificated
explorer and navigator was also at an end.
Whatever of the strange parts of this inter-
esting planet he might investigate in the
future, he would do so in a private capacity
—as lie had done before for the best part of
bis life —and not as the captain of a Queen’s
ship. He would have to go back, for a very
subsistence, to the old buccaneering, priva-
teering, piratical days that had preceded his
first visit to New Holland with the muti-
neers of the Cygnet. The navy was a closed
book. Maybe, he brooded miserably, he
would close his adventurous life a-dangling
from the yard-arm of a man-o’-war. Will
Dampier was a sad man when he came
ashore that summer’s night in Portsmouth.
And outside the door of The Nag’s Head
—a low tavern frequented by inferior naval
ratings, situated in the narrow street through
which his way lay to his inn—there awaited
him the gunner of the London, who had tes-
tified enthusiastically against him in the
forenoon, in the presence of that hard-bitten
tableful of captains.
The gunner of 11.M.5. London was one
Jan Pentraeth, native of Falmouth, in Corn-
wall. And Jan Pentraeth was pot at all
grateful for his good discharge from H.M.S.
Roebuck, signed “Win. Dampier,” even
though it had gained him his present exalted
position in a line-o’-battleship.
“You mark me, in’ lads,” vaunted Mr.
Pentraeth —-“you mark me, an’ see how I
deal wi’ such pirates as Master Will Dam-
pier. Maroon me, would he! Gi’ me two
dozen at th’ gangway, bigod! Call me a
stinkin’ o’ th’ gar’son o’ Pendennis
Castle! By th’ livin’ Saint Michael, ’tes
Jan’s turn noiv 7”
Up the winding street strode the broken
man, sore from the grins of the boat’s, crew
of the London —who had not, every day of
their hard lives, the happy felicity of rowing
disrated post-captains ashore.
Jan Pentraeth swaggered out into the
roadway to meet him, and stood, with folded
arms across a bosom that swelled with exul-
tation, opposite the tavern door —a sneer on
his dark Phoenician face, and very conscious
of the delightful admiration which lie in-
spired in the group of his cronies, who clus-
tered about the low doorway to witness his
triumph.
Dampier lifted up his dim eyes, as he
became aware of the tall figure that blocked
his passage below the gable of the old inn. A
shipmate—one of the men who, had shared in
the vicissitudes of the crazy old Roebuck !
Well, ’twas a relief to meet with any old
shipmate—even with one who had proved
himself an enemy—at the close of this weary
day. He was glad even to meet with his
malcontent gunner’s mate, sink him if he
wasn’t!
Pentraeth opened his mouth to pour forth
the vials of his contemptuous and triumphant
wrath —when he met his captain’s eye,, as
the latter came to a standstill before him, a
little smile playing about his lips, with his
hand extended.
Dampier’s deep brown eyes had something
in them that had won the affections of many
men who cared little enough for their kind.
There was a gleam of humor in them, a sug-
gestion of kindliness, a hint of fellowship—
when they were not hard in anger—that
found men’s hearts.
“Well, Jan. my hearty,” he said, a little
wistfully. “ ’Tis all over. I bear ye no
illwill. Ye’ll drink a dram with your old
captain ?”
To the astonished disappointment of the
crew in the doorway, they beheld the trucu-
lent, the implacable and so lately triumph-
ant gunner of the London put forth his
horny hand and grasp that of “the ——
pirate.”
God’s shoe-buckles! They were bewil-
dered.
“Make way there —make way !” bellowed
Mr. Dampier’s nimble young man. “Give
way for Master Dampier, who found New
Holland!”
“Nay, nay. .Tan.” laughed Damnier. “Say,
rather, who lost the cursed country.”
Half an hour after, when they came out
of the candlelight into the darkness, Dam-
pier was saying :
“So that’s the way of it, Jan. I’ve to go
a-roving again for my bread. And these
City o’ London merchants, they’ve found me
two ships, the St. George and the Cinque
Ports—an’ I’ll warrant ye they’re better
found than the old Roebuck—and Igo to the
Spanish Main to harry the d —d Dons. 'Twill
be profitable venture for all who sail with
me. Ye’ll leave the London, then —I’ll find
ye good hiding in Deptford—and sail with
me as gunner in the St. George? ’Tis such
as you I’m needing. I’ve got good old Dick
Corbin of Deal. Ye’re willing to sail with
me?”
“By God, I be, Cap’n! Wi’ none better,”
enthusiastically cried Jan Pentraeth. “I’ll
post wi’ ye to Lunnon this night—an’ dam'
th’ Queen’s sarvice!”
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
The Gins’ Corroboree.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BY certain sounds from around the
corner of the verandah —grunts, slaps,
splashes and the rattle of a kerosene-
tin—l knew that black Mary had a job to
“scrub-em floor.” Presently she turned the
corner, and sat up on her heels. She was
sucking an obviously empty pipe, so 1 asked
her if she would have a fill of ’bacca. Mary’s
answer was a fat black hand held out. I
gave her a fill, she loaded her pipe and stowed
the overflow away in her dillybag, and lit the
pipe with a match ferreted from her woolly
hair.
When the pipe was going full blast Mary
made her first remark. “You like see black
gins’ corroboree?”
“Ge.t out, Mary,” I said. “You’re pulling
my leg. Gins don’t have corroborees.”
“Ilabbit this one,” said Mary. “Nellie
teach it this feller.”
“When is it to be?” I asked.
“When moon jump-up little-feller way,
alonga blackfeller camp,” said Mary.
Paddy’s Nellie had always been considered
lazy because she scorned to earn stray six-
pences by wash-em clothes. It seems now
that Nellie was not lazy, but an artist.
After tea I strolled over. Big friendly
stars studded the sky—not a bit like the cold
distant stars of temperate latitudes, but
each with a color of its own. The full moon
had just risen, like a big golden lantern,
picking out the landscape in intense high
lights and clear-cut shadows.
The camp was on the bank of the creek,
just across the bridge. I followed the road,
though there was no necessity to cross the
bridge these times, as there were only a few
pools of water left.
Mary met me, and after driving off fifty
dogs of forty-seven different breeds, con-
ducted me to a seat by the old men’s fire.
The smooth sandy bed of the creek was
being prepared for the corroboree. The
audience was sitting around fires on the
raised banks, gins on one side, men on the
other. There was no doubt that Nellie was
a person of importance to-night; she bustled
around giving orders here and arguing there.
No author at a first production could be
more particular about detail.
The artists,'a dozen of the youngest gins,
were now marched on to the. ground. They
had discarded the miscellaneous clothing they
usually wore, and were decorated with gum-
sprays—a fringe around the ankles and
wrists; some hanging pendant to the arms;
a bunch, behind, like a tail. When I saw the
two rows lined up facing one another I
recognised that there was more reason behind
the average White Mary’s insistence on
Black Mary wearing clothes than is apparent
at first glance; by the light of the fires they
looked like rows of bronze statues.
Nellie gave the signal for the corroboree to
start. The older gins were all provided with
time-sticks, which they beat at first slowly
and softly. The two rows bowed to each
other and spun round, then bowed again. I
had just begun to think Nellie was giving us
an imitation of a set of ouadrilles, when they
all began to lift their arms and softly chuckle
and bow towards the centre. Then I saw at
once that they were native-companions danc-
ing. The time-sticks beat louder and faster;
the dancers kept time, spinning and moving
their arms like wings, bowing and stretching
their necks forward towards the centre of
the ring. At last, with a final crow, all
stood as still as statues. After a couple of
minutes’ interval the figure was repeated
with a few variations.
The gins and younger men were getting
more and more excited, but the old men so
far showed not the slightest sign of approval.
Nellie now drew the performers up in two
lines, facing the old men’s fire. They stood,
with hands behind their heads, swaying
slowly to the rhythm of the time-sticks, con-
tinually keeping the muscles of the thighs
quivering in an extraordinary manner. At
a signal from Nellie they sank down on their
heels, swinging the knees outwards, then
sprang up into the air, bringing the legs
together with a sounding slap. An audible
“Ugh!” of appreciation from the old men
testified that this part of the performance,
at least, was a success. The time-sticks
grew louder; the performers swayed quicker
and leaped higher until, with a final shout
for a climax, they rushed away off the
ground.
I presented the old men with a few sticks
of tobacco, and Nellie with a stick of tobacco
and a pipe, congratulating her on the success
of the show, and inquiring who taught her
tin' corrcfboree.
“Native companion teachit that one,” said
Nellie.
“Who teachit that last one?” I asked.
“Mine tlvinkit that one,” was her answer.
As I strolled home I wondered whether the
old men had coined a word to describe the
latest result of the emancipation of women.
Next morning, soon after breakfast, I met
Paddy smoking the pipe which I had pre-
sented to Nellie the night before. Paddy’s
greeting was, “Very good corroboree that one
belonga me an’ Nellie, boss. Gibbit tiepen.”
I handed out the coin and decided there
was no hurry for the word. Paddy was still
boss in his own gunyah.
H. W. STEWART.
